Answering the Fourth Objection: Priority and Posteriority
The author answers that the same problem would apply to contingency (huduth) known by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the concept of dependence (iftiqar) by showing that the same logical problem of priority and posteriority applies to origination (huduth), which is empirically certain.

Core Idea
If the objection applies to the undeniable, it cannot refute the debatable.

The fourth objection raises a logical puzzle: if something is judged as dependent, then the thing judged must be both prior and posterior to the judgment, which seems problematic. The author responds not by solving the puzzle directly, but by pointing out that the same puzzle applies to origination (huduth), which we know to be true through everyday observation. This defuses the objection without needing to resolve the underlying logical issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice that the author first quotes the objection, then gives his answer.
The passage is incomplete but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the author's strategy
The author concedes that the concept of dependence might be ambiguous, but then shifts focus to origination (huduth), which is known necessarily through examples like light after darkness.
He uses an analogy: the same issue arises for huduth, so the objection is not unique.
3
Understand the implication
If the objection is equally problematic for an undeniable fact (origination), then it cannot be a fatal objection against the less certain concept of dependence.
This is a common dialectical move in kalam: turn the objection back on the opponent.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you see how the author refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : وهي قولكم : (١) و الحكم على الشيء بالافتقار يقتضي أن يكون المحكوم عليه بالافتقار مقدماً على ذلك الافتقار ومتأخراً عنه ، قلنا : هب أن الافتقار إلى الشيء معنى وقع في محل الشك والشبهة ، إلا أن كون الشيء حادثاً بعد العدم أمر معلوم بالضرورة ، فإنـا نشاهد أن النور يحدث بعد الظلمة ، وأن الحر يحدث بعد البرد ، ونقول : ما ذكرتموه في تقرير هذه الشبهة ، فهو بعينه قائم في الحدوث ، وذلك لأن ذلك الشيء موصوف بالحدوث، والموصوف متقدم بالرتبة على الصفة ، ويقتضي أيضاً ، أن يكون متأخراً عنه ، لأنه وجد بعد أن لم يكن ، وكما أن هذه الشبهة
And the answer to the fourth objection: Your statement: 'Judging a thing as dependent (al-hukm 'ala al-shay' bi al-iftiqar) requires that the thing judged as dependent be prior to that dependence and posterior to it.' We say: Even if dependence on something is a meaning that falls in the place of doubt and ambiguity, yet the thing's being originated (hadithan) after non-existence is a matter known by necessity, for we observe that light occurs after darkness, and heat occurs after cold. And we say: What you mentioned in formulating this objection stands exactly with respect to origination (al-huduth), because that thing is described by origination, and the described is prior in rank to the attribute, and also requires that it be posterior to it, because it came into existence after it had not been. And just as this objection...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the fourth objection. The opponent argues that judging something as 'dependent' (muftaqir) entails that the thing judged is both prior to and posterior to the dependence, which is problematic. The author concedes that the concept of dependence might be ambiguous, but then points out that origination (huduth) — the fact that something comes into existence after non-existence — is known by necessity through empirical observation. The same logical issue (priority and posteriority of the subject to the attribute) applies to origination. Since origination is undeniable, the objection cannot be fatal. The text cuts off, but the argument is essentially that the objection is not specific to dependence and thus does not refute it.

محكوم عليه
افتقار
حادث

عدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the priority/posteriority problem is unique to dependence (iftiqar) and therefore refutes it.
Correction: The author shows that the same problem applies equally to origination (huduth), which is known by necessity, so the objection is not specific to dependence and cannot refute it.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to counter the fourth objection about priority and posteriority?
A) The concept of dependence is clearly defined.
B) The same issue applies to origination (huduth) which is necessary knowledge.
C) The objection is invalid because it is self-contradictory.
D) Dependence is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the fourth objection by showing that the same logical issue of priority and posteriority applies to origination (huduth), which is undeniably true through observation, so the objection is not a valid refutation of dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical objection to your beliefs, do you immediately doubt them, or do you first see if the same objection applies to things you know for certain? What does your reaction reveal about your relationship with the truth?
?
The author uses the undeniable fact of 'origination'—light after darkness—to defend a deeper theological concept. What undeniable realities in your own life (your own beginning, your dependence) do you ignore because they demand an uncomfortable answer?
?
If the objection to dependence is the same as the objection to something you cannot deny (like your own existence after non-existence), then the objection is not a valid reason to reject. What objections to faith have you accepted without checking whether they also apply to undeniable truths?

Refutation of the Fifth Sophism: Reductio ad Absurdum
Sanusi refutes the fifth sophism via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the fifth sophism by demonstrating that its logic would deny obvious changes in the world.

Core Idea
If a philosophical objection denies empirical facts, it is self-defeating.

This passage presents a classic kalam argument: a reductio ad absurdum. The opponent claims that only quiddity, existence, or their connection can need a cause. The author turns the tables, showing that this very claim would make it impossible for anything to change—like air becoming illuminated. Since we know changes happen, the opponent's position is unsound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the author's concise style.
Pay attention to the logical flow from the opponent's claim to the absurd consequences.
2
Identify the opponent's sophism
The fifth sophism states that whatever needs an efficient cause must be either the quiddity, existence, or their connection. Understand this claim.
3
See how the author applies it to origination
The author says the same reasoning applies to the concept of origination (ḥudūth). If something originates, it would also be one of those three. Since the opponent considers this impossible, they would have to deny origination altogether.
4
Examine the empirical examples
The author gives two examples: air becoming illuminated after being dark, and a person standing after being seated. These are undeniable changes.
The first example was corrected from 'الجو' to 'الهواء' in the source.
5
Understand the reductio
If the opponent's principle were true, these changes could not occur. But they do occur. Therefore, the opponent's principle must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من الشبهة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : « المفتقر إلى المؤثر إما الماهية ، أو الوجود أو اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ، فنقول : هذا الإشكال وارد في الحدوث ، فإنه يقال : لو حدث شيء لكان الحادث إما الماهية أو الوجود ، أو موصوفية الماهية بالوجود ، والكل باطل للوجوه التي ذكرتموها في تقرير هذه الشبهة ، وهذا يقتضي أن لا يضيء الهواء بعد أن كان مظلماً ، وأن لا يقوم الإنسان بعد أن كان جالساً ، وكما أن هذا باطل فكذلك ما ذكرتم .
And the answer to the fifth sophism, which is their statement: 'The thing in need of an effect is either the quiddity, or existence, or the connection of quiddity with existence.' We say: This difficulty applies to origination. For it is said: If something originates, the originated thing would be either the quiddity, or existence, or the qualification of quiddity by existence. And all of that is invalid for the reasons you mentioned in formulating this sophism. And this necessitates that air would not become illuminated after being dark, and that a person would not stand after being seated. And just as this is false, so is what you have mentioned.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the fifth sophism in a kalam debate. The opponent argues that whatever requires an efficient cause must be either the quiddity, existence, or their connection. The author responds by showing that this same reasoning can be applied to the concept of origination (ḥudūth) itself, leading to absurdities. If the opponent's objection were valid, then any change, like air becoming illuminated or a person standing, would be inexplicable. Since those are empirically certain, the opponent's reasoning is unsound. The author thus argues by reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's principle, if applied consistently, would deny obvious facts, so it must be rejected. The correction of 'air' from the variant reading is noted.

Key Terms
مفتقر إلى المؤثر
ماهية
وجود
حدوث
هواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sophism correctly identifies the only possible candidates for needing a cause, and therefore the need for a cause is problematic.
Correction: The author shows that the sophism leads to the denial of evident changes, so it cannot be correct. The sophism is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue against the fifth sophism?
A) The sophism is valid for origination.
B) The sophism leads to denial of obvious changes.
C) The sophism proves the need for a cause.
D) The sophism is only about quiddity.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the fifth sophism demonstrates that a philosophical principle leading to the denial of empirical facts is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has a clever argument ever made you doubt something you know to be true from the world around you, like the fact that things change and begin to exist? How will you guard against such sophisms in the future?
?
When you read the examples of air becoming illuminated and a person standing, do you take these everyday changes for granted? How can this passage deepen your gratitude to Allah, who constantly brings about origination and sustains all things?
?
Are there areas of your life where you allow abstract doubts or philosophical puzzles to keep you from acting on clear truths? What one concrete step will you take this week to live out the certainty that Allah is the Creator of all that happens?

Refuting the Claim That Possibility Cannot Apply to Simple Entities
Refutation of the sixth sophism on possibility in simples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the application of possibility to simple entities by redefining possibility as a simple capacity rather than a relational attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility is a simple capacity, not a relational attribute.

The sixth sophism argues that only composite things can be contingent because possibility is relational. The author counters by showing that simple essences like blackness are also contingent and that possibility is nothing but the non-impossibility of permanence or removal. This lesson unpacks the debate and the author's decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that need for a cause only applies to composites, not simples. Read the initial objection carefully.
The opponent uses a dichotomy: simple or composite.
2
See the author's counterexamples
The author uses blackness and substance as examples of simple entities that are contingent. Notice how they are made by an agent.
These examples show that simplicity does not preclude need for a cause.
3
Analyze the definition of possibility
The opponent says possibility is relational. The author redefines it as non-impossibility of permanence or removal. Understand this shift.
This redefinition is key to the refutation.
4
Follow the final rebuttal
The opponent tries to argue that this makes the essence composite. The author calls it a verbal quibble and reaffirms that possibility applies to simples.
The author insists on the simple subject of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السادسة : وهي قولهم : « المحكوم عليه بالحاجة إما أن يكون بسيطاً أو مركباً » فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون بسيطاً؟ فإن عندنا السواد إنما صار سواداً بالفاعل، والجوهر إنما صار جوهراً بالفاعل. قوله : « علة الحاجة هي الإمكان، والإمكان صفة نسبية، والنسبة لا يمكن حصولها في المفردات » قلنا : المراد عندنا هو أنه لا يمتنع بقاؤه ولا يمتنع زواله، ولا نسلم أن الإمكان بهذا التفسير لا يعترض البسائط. فإن قالوا : قول القائل «السواد من حيث إنه سواد لا يمتنع بقاؤه ولا يمتنع زواله» لا يفيد غرضكم، وذلك لأن ما ذكرتم يقتضي الحكم على السواد بإمكان البقاء تارة وبإمكان الفناء أخرى، والمفهوم من كون السواد سواداً مغايراً للمفهوم من البقاء والفناء، فما ذكرتم يدل على أن الإمكان يمتنع حصوله في الماهية البسيطة. قلنا : هذا تمسك بمحض اللفظ، والمراد من الإمكان كون الشيء في نفسه بحيث يصح أن تبقى هويته وأن لا تبقى، ولا شك أن المحكوم عليه بهذا المفهوم بالإمكان مفرد لا مركب.
And the answer to the sixth sophism, which is their statement: 'That which is judged to have need is either simple or composite.' We say: Why is it not permissible that it be simple? For according to us, blackness only becomes blackness by an agent, and substance only becomes substance by an agent. Their statement: 'The cause of need is possibility, and possibility is a relational attribute, and relation cannot be obtained in simple things.' We say: What is meant by us is that its permanence is not impossible nor its removal impossible. And we do not accept that possibility in this interpretation does not apply to simple things. For if they say: The statement 'blackness inasmuch as it is blackness, its permanence is not impossible nor its removal impossible' does not serve your purpose; because what you have mentioned necessitates judging blackness as having possibility of permanence and possibility of extinction, and the concept of blackness being blackness is different from the concept of permanence and extinction. So your statement indicates that possibility cannot be obtained in a simple essence. We say: This is clinging to mere words. What is meant by possibility is that the thing in itself is such that its identity may remain or not remain. And there is no doubt that that which is judged by this concept of possibility is simple, not composite.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the sixth sophism. The opponent argues that need for a cause applies only to composite things, not simple essences. The author counters by giving examples: blackness and substance are simple yet made by an agent. The opponent then says that the cause of need is possibility (imkān), which is relational and cannot apply to simples. The author redefines possibility: it is not a relational attribute but the non-impossibility of permanence or removal. This interpretation applies to simple entities. The opponent protests that possibility of permanence and extinction are two attributes, while the thing itself is one, so it's composite in meaning. The author calls this a verbal quibble and reiterates that possibility is the inherent capacity of a simple thing to persist or cease. Thus, a simple entity can be the subject of possibility.

Key Terms
إمكان
بسيط
مركب
ماهية
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a relational attribute that cannot exist in simple entities.
Correction: The author shows that possibility is the simple non-impossibility of permanence or removal, which applies to simple entities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of possibility?
A) A relational attribute.
B) The non-impossibility of permanence or removal.
C) The need for a cause.
D) The composition of the thing.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not a relational attribute but a simple capacity of a thing to remain or cease, and this applies to simple entities like blackness and substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your own existence is a simple capacity—entirely dependent on an Agent at every moment—or do you secretly feel self-sufficient, as if your permanence were necessary? What habit or attitude in your daily life reveals that feeling?
?
When you consider your own identity—the 'I' that remains from childhood to now—do you truly believe it could cease at any moment by the will of its Maker? If not, what in your heart resists that contingency?
?
If possibility is not a relation but a simple fact about your own essence, then your need for God is not something added to you—it is you. Are you ready to let go of the illusion that your existence is self-standing and admit that your very essence is a need? What would change if you lived from that admission?

Refuting the Charge of Infinite Regress in Causality
Refutation of the infinite regress objection to causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's rebuttal to the argument that causality necessarily leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The opponent's division is unsound; infinite regress is not proven.

This passage continues the refutation of a key anti-causality argument: that if a cause has an effect, the causal relation must be distinct, leading to infinite regress. The author argues that the opponent's classification of premises is flawed, and that formal correctness does not guarantee truth. The garbled text may refer to a self-evident premise, but the main point is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the opponent's argument and the author's initial response.
Note the garbled phrase 'فيكون بالطلا' – it likely indicates a self-evident premise.
2
Identify the author's two responses
The passage contains two main rebuttals: first, the opponent's initial division is unsound; second, the objection about a formally correct but materially false syllogism is rejected by denying the premises.
The author does not concede any point; he attacks the opponent's logic.
3
Analyze the strategy
Recognize that the author uses a dialectical method: he does not directly prove causality but shows the opponent's argument fails on its own terms.
This is a common strategy in refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السابعة : وهي قولهم : « القول بثبوت شيء يؤثر في شيء آخر يقتضي كون ذلك التأثير مغايراً لذات المؤثر ولذات الأثر وهو يوجب التسلسل » . فنقول في الجواب : قد ذكرنا أن مثل ذلك التقسيم الذي ذكرتم قائم فيها علم وجوده بالضرورة فيكون بالطلا. قوله : « فإذا سلمتم وجود كلام صحيح الشكل صحيح المادة مع أن نتيجته تكون باطلة فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بشيء من الدلائل على صحة شيء من المطالب » قلنا : نحن لا نسلم صحة الوجوه المذكورة في ذلك التقسيم ، بل نحن إنما أوردنا هذه المعارضات لتدل على اشتمال التقسيم الذي ذكرتم على مقدمات فاسدة .
And the answer to the seventh sophism, which is their statement: 'The assertion that something exists that affects something else necessitates that this effect is distinct from the essence of the effect and the essence of the affected, and this leads to infinite regress.' We say in answer: We have already mentioned that such a division which you have presented has in it knowledge of its existence by necessity, so it is [garble]. His statement: 'If you accept the existence of a syllogism correct in form and material while its conclusion is false, then it would not be possible to use any proof to establish any claim.' We say: We do not accept the validity of the aspects mentioned in that division. Rather, we presented these critiques to indicate that the division you mentioned contains unsound premises.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage refutes the charge that causality leads to infinite regress because the effect is distinct from the cause. The author argues that the opponent's division is flawed. The garbled word 'بالطلا' is likely a corruption; the intended meaning may be that the division is self-evident or leads to contradiction. The opponent then says that if a syllogism is formally correct but materially false, no proof can be trusted. The author replies that they do not accept the opponent's premises; their objections are meant to show the opponent's division is unsound, not to establish a counter-thesis.

Key Terms
تأثير
تسلسل
تقسيم
مقدمات فاسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any distinction between cause and effect necessarily leads to an infinite regress.
Correction: The author argues that the opponent's division is unsound, not that the distinction itself leads to regress. The infinite regress charge is based on flawed premises.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main response to the seventh sophism?
A) He admits infinite regress is unavoidable.
B) He denies causality altogether.
C) He argues the opponent's division is unsound.
D) He accepts the opponent's syllogism as valid.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the infinite regress objection by showing the opponent's division is unsound, not by proving causality itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present a logical argument, do you check whether your premises are sound, or do you assume your conclusion is right because it sounds good? How often do you examine the foundations of your beliefs?
?
The opponent assumed that if a syllogism is correct in form, the conclusion must be true. Have you ever accepted a claim just because it seemed logically tidy, without verifying its material truth?
?
The author exposed unsound premises in the opponent's division. Think of a belief you hold strongly—what if its premises are unsound? Are you willing to examine them honestly?

Refutation of the Eighth Sophism: Possibility and Non-Existence
The author refutes the claim that possibility would require a cause for non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that possibility (imkān) does not require a cause for non-existence, by distinguishing between the need for existence and the concept of causality.

Core Idea
Non-existence does not need a cause; possibility only necessitates a cause for existence, not for absence.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: if possibility is the cause of need, then non-existence would also need a cause. He carefully disentangles the logic, showing that need applies only to existence, and that even if causality were involved, absence of cause could be the cause. This lesson unpacks his layered refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the initial distinction
Read the Arabic passage and the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the author's first response: possibility causes need only for existence, not non-existence.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لم لا يجوز أن يقال' – it signals a permissible alternative.
2
Follow the hypothetical concession
The author hypothetically accepts that possibility causes need in both respects. He then asks why the cause of that need could not be the absence of a cause.
This is a dialectical move – he is not conceding the point, but testing the opponent's logic.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that causality is a positive attribute, so it cannot be mere absence. Understand the force of this objection.
The opponent assumes that every cause must be a positive thing.
4
Understand the rebuttal by analogy
The author counters that non-existence is described as contradictory to existence – a positive description – yet that does not make non-existence existent. Similarly, causality can be a negative concept.
This is a key refutation: descriptions can be positive without entailing existence of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة : وهي قولهم : « لو كان الإمكان علة للحاجة لافتقر المعدوم حال عدمه إلى المؤثر » فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإمكان علة لاحتياج وجود الممكن إلى المؤثر ، وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من هذا الكلام احتياج عدم الممكن إلى المؤثر ؟ سلمنا : أن الإمكان علة للحاجة في الطرفين ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال علته عدم العلة ؟ قوله : « العلية صفة ثبوتية » قلنا : لا نسلم قوله لأنها مناقضة للمفهوم من قولنا : إنه ليس بعلة ، قلنا : ينتقض هذا بقولنا : العدم مناقض للوجود ومقابل له. فإن ما ذكرتموه إن دل على قولكم لزم أن يقال : إن كون العدم مناقضاً للوجود صفة موجودة ، فيكون العدم موصوفاً بصفة موجودة ، وما كان كذلك كان موجوداً ، فيلزم كون العدم محض الوجود ، وهو محال .
And the answer to the eighth sophism, which is their statement: 'If possibility were the cause of need, then the non-existent in its state of non-existence would need an effect.' We say: Why is it not permissible to say: possibility is the cause of the need of the existence of the possible for an effect? And if so, then the need of the non-existence of the possible for an effect does not follow from this statement. We accept that possibility is the cause of need in both respects, so why is it not permissible to say that its cause is the absence of cause? His statement: 'Causality is a positive attribute.' We say: We do not accept his statement because it is contradictory to the concept of our statement that it is not a cause. We say: This is contradicted by our statement: non-existence is contradictory to existence and opposite to it. For what you have mentioned, if it indicates your statement, it would necessitate that the contradictoriness of non-existence to existence is an existing attribute, so non-existence would be described by an existing attribute, and whatever is so described is existent, leading to non-existence being pure existence, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is a refutation of the objection that if possibility (imkān) is the cause of need, then a non-existent would need a cause to remain non-existent. The author distinguishes: possibility causes the existence of the possible to need a cause, not the non-existence. He then hypothetically accepts that possibility causes need in both cases, and suggests that the cause of need could be the absence of cause. The opponent objects that causality is a positive attribute, hence cannot be absence. The author rebuts by analogy: non-existence is described as contradictory to existence, which is a positive description, but that does not make non-existence existent. So the opponent's objection is invalid.

Key Terms
إمكان
علية
عدم
وجود
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence would need a cause because possibility is the cause of need.
Correction: Possibility only causes the existence of the possible to need a cause; non-existence does not require a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the author, possibility (imkān) is the cause of what?
A) The need of existence for a cause
B) The need of non-existence for a cause
C) Both existence and non-existence
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Possibility does not entail a cause for non-existence; only existence needs a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself lacking something you desperately want (a job, health, a relationship), do you automatically assume that the absence must have a 'cause'—something you did wrong or some external factor? This lesson reminds us that non-existence does not require a cause. Will you trust Allah's wisdom even when you cannot pinpoint a reason for your lack? Make a firm resolve today to stop searching for a 'cause' for your emptiness and instead focus on what exists: His mercy and provision.
?
Have you ever been like the opponent, insisting that everything positive in your life must have a tangible, positive explanation—that your blessings are solely due to your own efforts? This lesson shows that even causality itself can be a negative concept. Are you willing to admit that your very existence is a gift from Allah, not something you caused? Commit to thanking Allah for each breath as an unearned favor.
?
The author uses an analogy: describing non-existence as 'contradictory to existence' does not make non-existence existent. Similarly, when you are in a state of spiritual emptiness or doubt, you may describe your condition negatively—but that does not make the emptiness a reality or excuse. Will you allow this truth to push you toward seeking the light of faith, rather than wallowing in the 'non-existence' of your worst moments? Take one concrete step today to replace a negative description with a positive action of worship.

Answer to the Seventh Objection on Causal Regress
Sanusi refutes the infinite regress objection to causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the argument that causality implies infinite regress by pointing out the flawed premises in the opponent's division.

Core Idea
Regress objections fail because they rely on corrupted premises.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to an objection against causality: that positing an effect distinct from its cause leads to an infinite regress. He first notes that the objection's own division presupposes something necessarily known, then argues that if the opponent's logic allowed false conclusions from valid forms, all inference would collapse. His real point is that the objection's premises are corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without trying to interpret the unclear phrase 'فيكون بالطلا'. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice that the text has a unclear phrase; we will handle it in commentary.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that causality implies regress because the effect is other than the cause and the effect itself. Find this in the passage.
The phrase 'يوجب التسلسل' indicates the claim of regress.
3
Identify the answer's two parts
Sanusi gives two responses: first, the opponent's division is based on something necessarily known; second, if the opponent concedes a valid argument with a false conclusion, then all proof fails. The real answer is that the premises are corrupt.
The second part is signaled by 'قوله: فإذا سلمتم...'.
4
Recognize the key takeaway
Sanusi is not conceding regress; he is exposing that the objection's own premises are flawed. This is the main point.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السابعة : وهي قولهم : القول بثبوت شيء يؤثر في شيء آخر يقتضي كون ذلك التأثير مغايراً لذات المؤثر ولذات الأثر وهو يوجب التسلسل . فنقول في الجواب : قد ذكرنا أن مثل ذلك التقسيم الذي ذكرتم قائم فيها علم وجوده بالضرورة فيكون بالطلا. قوله: فإذا سلمتم وجود كلام صحيح الشكل صحيح المادة مع أن نتيجته تكون باطلة فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بشيء من الدلائل على صحة شيء من المطالب قلنا : نحن لا نسلم صحة الوجوه المذكورة في ذلك التقسيم بل نحن إنما أوردنا هذه المعارضات لتدل على اشتمال التقسيم الذي ذكرتم على مقدمات فاسدة.
And the answer to the seventh objection, which is their statement: 'The statement of the existence of something that affects another necessitates that effect be other than the essence of the effecter and the essence of the effect, and it entails regress.' So we say in the answer: We have mentioned that such a division which you have mentioned is based on something whose existence is known necessarily, so it is ... His statement: 'If you concede the existence of a discourse correct in form and correct in matter, while its conclusion is false, then it is impossible to infer by any of the proofs the correctness of any of the claims.' We said: We do not concede the correctness of the mentioned aspects in that division; rather, we only brought these counterarguments to indicate that the division you mentioned contains corrupt premises.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the seventh objection. The objection claims that causality implies a regress because the effect is other than the cause and the effect. The response first notes that the objection's own division is based on something necessarily known, then points out that if the opponent concedes valid reasoning with a false conclusion, then all inference fails. The author rejects the validity of the objection's premises, stating that the counterarguments were brought only to show the corruption in the opponent's division.

Key Terms

مؤثر (effecter)
أثر (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author is conceding that causality leads to infinite regress.
Correction: Sanusi only shows that the objection's premises are corrupt; he does not accept regress.

Checkpoint

A) It assumes causality is impossible.
B) It contains corrupt premises in its division.
C) It denies the existence of effects.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the regress objection by demonstrating that the opponent's division contains corrupt premises.

Answer to the Eighth Objection: Possibility as Cause Only for Existence
Sanusi refutes the eighth objection by distinguishing that possibility causes need only for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that possibility would require a cause for non-existence, and why he argues that possibility only necessitates a cause for existence.

Core Idea
Possibility necessitates a cause only for existence, not for non-existence.

The eighth objection claims that if possibility is a cause of need, then even a non-existent thing would need an effecter during its non-existence. Sanusi answers by carefully distinguishing: possibility only makes the existence of a possible thing need a cause, not its non-existence. He also addresses the claim that causality is an affirmative attribute that cannot apply to non-existence, showing that such a view leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that if possibility is a cause of need, then the non-existent would also need a cause. Read the opening lines.
The objection is quoted at the beginning.
2
Read the first response
Sanusi asks: why not say that possibility is a cause for the need of existence only? This distinction blocks the objection.
3
Read the second response
Sanusi concedes possibility could be cause for both, but then the cause can be the absence of a cause.
This is a clever move.
4
Examine the rebuttal about attribute
When the opponent says causality is an affirmative attribute, Sanusi rebuts by showing the opposite: non-existence is opposed to existence but the opposition is not an attribute of non-existence.
This leads to absurdity if we take it as an attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة : وهي قولهم : ولو كان الإمكان علة للحاجة لافتقر المعدوم حال عدمه إلى المؤثر فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإمكان علة لاحتياج وجود الممكن إلى المؤثر وإذا كان كذلك لم يلزم من هذا الكلام احتياج عدم الممكن إلى المؤثر؟ سلمنا : أن الإمكان علة للحاجة في الطرفين فلم لا يجوز أن يقال علته عدم العلة؟ قوله: العلية صفة ثبوتية قلنا : لا نسلم قوله لأنها مناقضة للمفهوم من قولنا: إنه ليس بعلة قلنا : ينتقض هذا بقولنا: العدم مناقض للوجود ومقابل له. فإن ما ذكرتموه إن دل على قولكم لزم أن يقال: إن كون العدم مناقضاً للوجود صفة موجودة فيكون العدم موصوفاً بصفة موجودة وما كان كذلك كان موجوداً فيلزم كون العدم محض الوجود وهو محال.
And the answer to the eighth objection, which is their statement: 'If possibility were a cause of need, then the non-existent would need an effecter during its non-existence.' So we say: Why is it not permissible to say: Possibility is a cause for the need of the existence of the possible to an effecter? And if that is so, then from this speech the need of the non-existence of the possible for an effecter does not follow. We concede: that possibility is a cause for need in both aspects, so why is it not permissible to say that its cause is the absence of a cause? His statement: 'Causality is an affirmative attribute.' We said: We do not concede his statement because it contradicts the concept of our saying 'it is not a cause.' We said: This is rebutted by our saying: Non-existence contradicts existence and is opposite to it. For what you mentioned, if it indicates your statement, then it would necessitate that the fact that non-existence contradicts existence is an existing attribute, so non-existence would be described by an existing attribute, and whatever is so is existent, so it would necessitate that non-existence is pure existence, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage answers the eighth objection. The objection argues that if possibility requires a cause, then the non-existent would need a cause. The response distinguishes: possibility is only a cause for the need of existence, not for non-existence. Even if it were for both, one could say the cause is the absence of a cause. The opponent then says causality is an affirmative attribute, implying non-existence cannot be described by it. The author rejects this by analogy: non-existence is opposite to existence, but the opposition itself is not an attribute of non-existence. If it were, non-existence would become existent, which is absurd.

Key Terms
إمكان
علة
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility as a cause of need would mean that non-existent things also need a cause.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that possibility only makes existence need a cause, not non-existence. Even if it did, the cause could be the absence of a cause.

Checkpoint

A) Possibility itself
B) The absence of a cause
C) The existence of the possible
D) The attribute of causality

Key Takeaway
Possibility only entails a need for a cause for the existence of a possible thing, not for its non-existence.

Answer to the Tenth Objection on Choosing Without Preponderant
Sanusi refutes the objection by identifying two preponderants in the fleeing person's choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that a person can choose without a preponderant by identifying two preponderants in the act and grounding the will in celestial causes.

Core Idea
Free will is not unguided; there is always a preponderant, even if unknown.

Sanusi addresses a classic objection: if someone fleeing a lion can choose a path without apparent reason, then choice without preponderant seems possible. He denies that no preponderant exists and explains the two preponderants present: the movement itself and the will behind it. He further clarifies that this will does not come from another will (to avoid infinite regress) but from celestial causes beyond human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated, then Sanusi denies it and provides two aspects of proof.
The objection is the tenth in a series; we focus only on this specific answer.
2
Identify the two preponderants
The passage lists two preponderants: first, the actual movement to one place rather than the other; second, the will (irada) for that movement. Understand why each qualifies as a preponderant.
These are not external factors but inherent in the agent's action.
3
Understand the cause of the will
Sanusi notes that the will is not caused by another will (to avoid infinite regress). Instead, it arises from celestial causes (asbab 'ulwiyya) whose details we do not know. This shows that all choices have a cause, even if hidden.
This aligns with the Ash'ari principle that all events are caused by Allah, often through intermediate causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة العاشرة : وهي قولهم : الهارب من السبع يختار أحد الطريقين لا لمرجح . فنقول : لا نسلم أنه لم يحصل المرجح هنا وبيانه من وجهين : أحدهما : حركته في أحد المكانين دون الثاني والسبب الثاني : إرادته لإحدى الحركتين دون الثانية ثم لا نقول إن تلك الإرادة حصلت لإرادة أخرى من قبله وإلا لزم التسلسل بل إنما حدثت تلك الإرادة في قلبه لأسباب علوية لا اطلاع لنا على تفاصيلها.
And the answer to the tenth objection, which is their statement: 'The one fleeing from a lion chooses one of two paths without a preponderant.' So we say: We do not concede that no preponderant occurred here, and its clarification is from two aspects: one of them: his movement to one of the two places and not the other, and the second cause: his will for one of the two movements and not the other. Then we do not say that that will occurred due to another will from him, otherwise regress would follow. Rather, that will only occurred in his heart due to celestial causes whose details we have no knowledge of.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage answers the objection that a person fleeing a lion chooses a path without any preponderant reason. The response identifies two preponderants: first, the actual movement to one direction rather than the other, and second, the will for that specific movement. The author then notes that this will is not caused by another will (to avoid regress) but rather by celestial causes beyond human knowledge.

Key Terms
مرجح (preponderant)
إرادة (will)
أسباب علوية (celestial causes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human choice can be completely uncaused or self-generated without any preponderant.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even in apparent 'free' choices like fleeing a lion, there is always a preponderant (movement and will), and the will itself has a cause (celestial causes). No choice is without a cause.

Checkpoint

B) Movement to one place and the will for that movement
C) The lion and the distance
D) External causes only

Key Takeaway
In every human choice, there is always a preponderant, even if its ultimate cause is beyond our knowledge.

Answer to the Eleventh Objection: The Need for a Cause Does Not Impugn Possibility
Sanusi answers the eleventh objection by showing it applies equally without a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the necessity of a cause for possible things by showing that the same objection would apply even if we deny that need.

Core Idea
The objection against causality is self-defeating: it applies to possibility itself, not just the causal view.

Sanusi now addresses a clever objection: if something possible needs a cause, doesn't that undermine its possibility? He answers by turning the argument around, showing that the problem exists regardless of whether we affirm or deny the need for a cause. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Sanusi's response.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find the statement of the objection and then Sanusi's initial counter-argument. Note how he turns the tables by saying the same objection impugns existence regardless of causality.
3
Grasp the clarification
Read the clarification about existence and non-existence. Understand why Sanusi says any excuse for the tension between existence and non-existence applies to the objection.
This shows the objection is not specific to the causal view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الحادية عشر : وهي قولهم : إن القول بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر يقدح في كونه ممكناً. فنقول : إن الكلام الذي ذكرتم يقدح في وجود الممكن سواء قلنا بافتقاره إلى المؤثر أو لم نقل به وبيانه : وهو أن المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً فإن كان موجوداً كان وجوده منافياً لعدمه وكان منافياً لإمكان عدمه وكذلك القول في جانب العدم فكل ما يصلح عذراً عن هذا يصلح عذراً عما ذكرتم.
And the answer to the eleventh objection, which is their statement: 'The statement that the possible is in need of an effecter impugns its being possible.' So we say: The discourse which you have mentioned impugns the existence of the possible, whether we say it is in need of an effecter or not. And its clarification is: The thing judged to be possible is either existent or non-existent. If it is existent, its existence is opposed to its non-existence, and it is opposed to the possibility of its non-existence. Similarly for the side of non-existence. So every excuse that suffices for this suffices for what you mentioned.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage answers the objection that affirming a need for a cause contradicts possibility. The response argues that the same problem arises even if we deny the need for a cause: the very judgment of possibility is about an existent or non-existent, and in both cases there is a tension between existence and non-existence. Whatever excuse resolves that tension applies equally to the objection.

Key Terms
إمكان (possibility)
ممكن (possible)
افتقار (need)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something needs a cause, it cannot be truly possible.
Correction: Need for a cause does not affect possibility; the possible is defined as that which can exist or not exist, regardless of causality.

Checkpoint

B) It applies equally whether we affirm need or not.
C) It is a valid objection that cannot be answered.
D) It is based on a misunderstanding of possibility.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the objection against needing a cause is self-defeating because the same problem arises from the nature of possibility itself.

Answer to the Twelfth Objection on the Timing of the Cause
Sanusi responds to an objection about the cause being present before the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves an objection to the timeline of causation by introducing the concept of a condition (shart) that removes an obstacle.

Core Idea
The cause is not temporary; an obstacle's removal is the condition for the effect.

Sanusi now addresses a subtle objection about the timing of the cause and effect. The objection claims that the cause must be either present or absent before the effect. Sanusi offers a third possibility that preserves the eternal nature of the cause while explaining the appearance of the effect. This passage clarifies a key point in the theory of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The passage is short but dense — each phrase matters.
2
Identify the dilemma and the solution
The objection presents a false dichotomy: either the cause was present before the event or it was not. Sanusi rejects this by introducing a condition (shart) that explains the timing without making the cause temporal.
Focus on the phrase 'passing away of the existent' — that is the condition.
3
Reflect on the importance of conditions in causal reasoning
This solution shows that a cause can be eternal yet still produce an effect at a specific time if an obstacle is removed. Consider how this applies to the relationship between the Creator and creation.
This is a key move in Islamic theology to avoid associating temporality with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية عشر : و المؤثر في كل ما لا بد منه في حصول المؤثرية إما أن يقال إنه كان حاصلا قبل حدوث هذا الحادث أو ما كان حاصلاً فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : ذات المؤثر كانت حاصلة إلا أن انقضاء الموجود الذي كان حاصلا قبل كان شرطاً لفيضان هذا الحادث.
And the answer to the twelfth objection: And the effecter in everything indispensable for the occurrence of effecter-ness, either it is said that it was present before the occurrence of this event or it was not present. So we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the essence of the effecter was present, but the passing away of the existent that was present before was a condition for the emanation of this event?
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage answers the twelfth objection, which likely questions whether the cause must be present before the effect. The response offers a third option: the essence of the cause was always present, but the removal of a prior existent (a condition) allowed the new event to occur. This avoids the dilemma that the cause was either present or not before the event.

Key Terms
مؤثر (effecter)
شرط (condition)
فيضان (emanation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the cause must be temporally prior to the effect (i.e., the cause must exist before the effect).
Correction: Sanusi shows that the cause can be eternal and simultaneous with the effect if a condition (like removal of an obstacle) is fulfilled.

Checkpoint

A) The presence of the effecter's essence
B) The passing away of a prior existent
C) The beginning of time
D) A new will of the effecter

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends causal simultaneity by distinguishing the eternal cause from the conditional removal of an obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What is that 'previous existent'—a habit, a sin, a distraction—that needs to be removed for you to experience His emanation more fully? Be honest and specific.
?
This lesson challenges the assumption that a cause must be temporally prior. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes feel you are waiting for Him to act? What if the cause is already there, and the only missing condition is the removal of an obstacle within yourself? What step can you take right now to remove that obstacle?

Refuting the Claim that Absence of Effect Implies Absence of Cause
Sanusi refutes the claim that absence of effect implies absence of cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of an effect does not necessarily imply the absence of its cause, and how the dependence of effect on cause preserves the cause's necessity.

Core Idea
The effect depends on the cause, not the other way around.

Sanusi tackles a subtle doubt: if the effect is absent, must the cause also be absent? This would make the First Cause contingent. He clarifies the logical relationship between cause and effect, distinguishing between necessary absence and causal dependence, and then addresses an objection involving conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Pay attention to the two-part structure: the main answer and the objection.
2
Identify the two claims Sanusi separates
Sanusi begins by distinguishing a true claim (absence of effect implies absence of cause) from a false claim (that this absence comes from the effect). Understand why the effect cannot cause the cause's absence.
This hinges on the fact that the effect is dependent (tābi') and cannot become independent (matbū').
3
Analyze the objection and its answer
The objector says that if every removal of effect requires prior removal of cause, then the cause must be removable. Sanusi answers that removal of effect may be due to removal of a condition of the cause, not the cause itself. The philosophers add that new effects from the eternal cause require prior termination of other events.
This preserves the cause's necessity while explaining temporal change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة عشر : وهي قولهم : « « إنه يلزم من عدم الأثر عدم المؤثر ، فيلزم أن يكون المبدأ الأول ممكناً لذاته .. فنقول : حق إنه يلزم عدم عند عدم الأثر ، ( عدم المؤثر ) (١) أما ما ليس بحق : فهو : أن عدم المؤثر إنما حصل من عدم الأثر ، لأن الأثر تابع . والتابع لا يصير متبوعاً ، لا في جانب الوجود ، ولا في جانب العدم ، قـولـه هب أن الأمر كذلك ، إلا أن هذا الكلام يقتضي أنه كلما ارتفع الأثر فقد ارتفع المؤثر أولاً ، وذلك يقتضي كون المؤثر قابلا للارتفاع ، قلنا : ارتفاع الأثر قد يكون لارتفاع المؤثر ، وقد يكون لارتفاع حالة من أحوال ذلك المؤثر ، كانت تلك الحالة شرطاً لصدور الأثر عن المؤثر. وعند الحكماء : إن صدور كل ) أثر ) (٢) حادث عن المؤثر القديم مشروط بانقضاء حادث آخر وجد قبله ، وهكذا لا إلى أول .
And the answer to the thirteenth doubt: it is their statement: 'Indeed from the absence of the effect follows the absence of the cause, so it follows that the first principle is possible in itself...' So we say: It is true that absence occurs at the absence of the effect, (absence of the cause). But what is not true is that the absence of the cause came about from the absence of the effect, because the effect is dependent. And the dependent does not become the independent, neither in the aspect of existence nor in the aspect of non-existence. His statement: 'Grant that it is so, except that this statement requires that whenever the effect is removed, the cause is removed first, and that requires the cause to be capable of removal.' We say: The removal of the effect may be due to the removal of the cause, or it may be due to the removal of a state of that cause, that state being a condition for the effect's issuance from the cause. And according to the philosophers: The issuance of every newly-arrived effect from the eternal cause is conditioned upon the termination of another newly-arrived thing that existed before it, and so on without a beginning.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes a doubt about the relationship between cause and effect. The opponents argue that if the effect is absent, the cause must also be absent, implying that the First Cause is not necessary. Sanusi distinguishes two claims: (1) that absence of effect necessitates absence of cause (true), and (2) that this absence of cause comes from the absence of the effect (false), because the effect is dependent on the cause, not vice versa. He then addresses an objection: if every removal of effect implies prior removal of cause, then the cause must be removable. The answer is that removal of effect may be due to removal of a condition of the cause, not the cause itself. He then cites the philosopher's position that each new effect from the eternal cause requires the prior termination of another event, ad infinitum.

Key Terms
مؤثر
أثر
تابع
متبوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of an effect proves that its cause does not exist.
Correction: The absence of an effect may be due to the removal of a condition required for the effect, not the removal of the cause itself.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is NOT true about the relationship between cause and effect?
A) The absence of the effect always coincides with the absence of the cause.
B) The absence of the cause is caused by the absence of the effect.
C) The effect is dependent on the cause.
D) The removal of the effect may be due to the removal of a condition of the cause.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect does not necessitate the absence of its cause; it may be due to the absence of a condition, preserving the cause's necessity.

How might you view that loss differently now, and how can this shift your gratitude today?
?

Refutation of the Fourteenth Doubt: Preponderance and Specification
Sanusi refutes the problem of preponderance without a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the doubt that the specification of a point or time requires an external preponderator.

Core Idea
Not every specification needs an external cause; nature itself can explain specificity.

Sanusi addresses a doubt raised by opponents who argue that both the philosopher's and theologian's views on specification require a preponderator. In this passage, he argues that the specification of the pole follows from motion, which itself is determined by the inherent properties of celestial matter. This challenges the assumption that a preponderator must be external.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Begin by reading the doubt that Sanusi is responding to. Notice how the opponents claim that specification of a point or time necessitates a preponderator.
The doubt is phrased as a conditional: if specification occurs without a preponderator, it is problematic.
2
Identify Sanusi's first move
Sanusi first says that the determination of the pole is dependent on motion. This shifts the question from the pole itself to the motion.
He says 'تابع للحركة المعينة' — it follows the specific motion.
3
Understand the explanation for motion
Sanusi then explains that the specific motion may be caused by the nature of the celestial matter, which only accepts that type of motion. No external preponderator is needed.
The phrase 'لا تقبل إلا ذلك النوع' indicates a natural restriction.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how this argument defeats the doubt: if specification can arise from the inherent nature of matter, then an external preponderator is not required for every determination.
This refutation applies to the philosopher's position; the theologian's part is addressed later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة عشر : وهي قولهم و اختصاص النقطة المعينة بالقطبية عند الحكيم ، واختصاص الوقت المعين بحدوث العالم فيه عند المتكلم ، يوجب رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن لا لمرجح ، فنقول : إن تعين النقطة المعينة للقطبية تابع للحركة المعينة ، وأما حصول تلك الحركة بعينها ، فيحتمل أن يكون السبب فيه أن المادة الفلكية المعينة لا تقبل إلا ذلك النوع المعين من الحركة . هذا على قول الحكيم .
And the answer to the fourteenth doubt: it is their statement: 'The specification of a particular point as the pole according to the philosopher, and the specification of a particular time for the occurrence of the world according to the theologian, necessitates a preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible without a preponderator.' So we say: The determination of a particular point as the pole is dependent on the particular motion. As for the occurrence of that particular motion itself, the reason for it may be that the particular celestial matter does not accept except that particular type of motion. This is according to the philosopher.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes a doubt about the need for a preponderator (مرجح) for the specification of time or point. The opponents argue that both the philosopher's claim of a specific point being the pole and the theologian's claim of a specific time for the world's creation require a preponderator. Sanusi's answer is that the pole's determination depends on the motion, and the specific motion itself may be due to the nature of the celestial matter only accepting that type of motion, thus no external preponderator is needed.

Key Terms
قطبية
رجحان
مرجح
مادة فلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every specification of a possible requires an external preponderator.
Correction: Some specifications can arise from the inherent nature of matter, as Sanusi argues regarding celestial matter only accepting a particular type of motion.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi explain the specification of a particular point as the pole?
A) It is due to an external preponderator.
B) It is dependent on the particular motion, which itself may be due to the nature of the celestial matter.
C) It is arbitrary with no cause.
D) It is determined by the theologian.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that specification can be explained by the nature of the thing itself, without requiring an external cause.

What would change if you focused more on how your own actions and the nature of things shape your outcomes, trusting that Allah's wisdom is embedded in the system He created? The Cause Acts by Its Essence
Sanusi refutes the fifteenth doubt about causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the claim that a cause must be considered as affected in its effect, and how he distinguishes between essential causality and accidental relations.

Core Idea
Causes act by their essence, not by being affected.

Sanusi addresses a classic doubt about causality: if one thing affects another, doesn't that imply the cause is itself affected? This would lead to circularity. Sanusi clarifies that the cause's action stems from its own essence, not from being affected in any way. The relations of 'causing' and 'being caused' are accidental additions that arise only after the effect exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the doubt and Sanusi's reply
The doubt claims that if A affects B, then A must also be considered as affected in B. Sanusi replies that the cause acts by its essence, not by being affected. Find this key distinction in the text.
The key phrase is 'بل يؤثر باعتبار ذاته المخصوصة'.
3
Distinguish essence from accidental relations
Sanusi explains that after the effect exists, two accidental relations arise: the cause and the effect. These are not part of the cause's essence. Understand why this solves the doubt.
The relations are 'إضافتان عارضتان'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الخامسة عشر : وهي قولهم : «لو أثر شيء في شيء ، لكان المؤثر في ذلك الأثر باعتبار كونه مؤثراً فيه ( أولاً باعتبار كونه مؤثراً فيه ، فنقول : الحق أنه يؤثر لا باعتبار كوني مؤثراً فيه) (٣) بل يؤثر باعتبار ذاته المخصوصة ، فذات الأثر توجب حصول الأثر ، ثم عند حصول ذات الأثر تحصل الإضافتان العارضتان للذات أعني : كون أحدهما مؤثراً في الآخر ، وكون الآخر أثراً للأول .
And the answer to the fifteenth doubt: it is their statement: 'If one thing affects another, then the cause in that effect would be considered as being affected in it, firstly as being affected in it.' So we say: The truth is that it affects not by virtue of my being affected in it, but by virtue of its particular essence. So the essence of the effect necessitates the occurrence of the effect. Then upon the occurrence of the essence of the effect, the two accidental relations occur to the essence, namely: that one is the cause in the other and that the other is the effect of the first.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes a doubt about causality and reciprocal relations. The opponents argue that if A affects B, then A must also be considered as being affected in B (a form of circular causation). Sanusi responds that the cause acts by its own essence, not by being affected. The effect's essence necessitates the effect's existence. After the effect exists, two relations arise: one being the cause and the other being the effect. These are accidental relations, not intrinsic to the cause's essence.

Key Terms
مؤثر
أثر
ذات
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If A affects B, then A must itself be affected by B.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the cause acts by its essence, not through being affected. The cause is not passively affected in its effect.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi affirm about the cause's action?
A) The cause acts by being affected in the effect.
B) The cause acts by virtue of its particular essence.
C) The cause and effect are identical.
D) The effect creates the cause.

Key Takeaway
The cause acts by its essence; the relations of cause and effect are accidental and arise only after the effect exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your actions, do you often see yourself as a passive victim of circumstances (being affected) rather than an active agent acting by your essence? How can you shift to taking responsibility for your own nature today?
?
The lesson says the cause-effect relation is accidental. What relationship in your life have you been treating as essential to your identity? How can you re-center on your essence rather than these accidental roles?

Necessity Belongs to the Cause, Not the Effect
Sanusi refutes the sixteenth doubt on necessity of emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi resolves the sixteenth doubt by showing that the necessity of emanation is an attribute of the cause, not the effect.

Core Idea
Necessity resides in the cause, preserving the contingency of the effect.

The sixteenth doubt argues that a possible thing must become necessary to exist, which seems contradictory. Sanusi agrees with the premise but reinterprets it. He explains that the necessity belongs to the cause, meaning the cause must be such that its effect is necessary, while the effect itself remains possible. This subtle shift dissolves the doubt entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi first states the doubt, then agrees, but reinterprets it.
The key phrase is 'إلا أن ذلك الوجوب يكون صفة لذات المؤثر لا لذات الأثر'.
2
Identify the doubt and the response
The doubt claims a possible must become 'necessarily emanating' to exist. Sanusi says this is true but clarifies the necessity is on the cause's side.
3
Understand the distinction
Grasp that necessity is an attribute of the cause (the agent), not the effect. The cause must be in a state where its effect is necessary, but the effect itself is not necessary in itself.
This preserves the contingency of created beings.
4
See how the doubt collapses
Once the necessity is placed on the cause, the objection that the possible becomes necessary loses its force. The possible remains possible as an effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السادسة عشر : وهي قولهم : « الممكن ما لم يصر واجب الصدور عن المؤثر لم يوجد ، فنقول : هذا حق : إلا أن ذلك الوجوب يكون صفة لذات المؤثر لا لذات الأثر ، وتفسير هذا الكلام : أنه ما لم يصر المؤثر بحيث يجب تأثيره في ذلك الأثر (١) لم يصدر عنه ذلك الأثر ، وبهذا التفسير يسقط ما ذكروه من الشبهة .
And the answer to the sixteenth doubt: it is their statement: 'The possible, as long as it does not become necessarily emanating from the cause, does not exist.' So we say: This is true, except that that necessity is an attribute of the essence of the cause, not of the essence of the effect. And the interpretation of this statement is that as long as the cause does not become such that its effect in that effect is necessary, that effect does not issue from it. By this interpretation their doubt collapses.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage refutes a doubt about the necessity of emanation. The opponents say that a possible thing, in order to exist, must become necessarily emanating from the cause. Sanusi agrees but clarifies that this necessity belongs to the cause, not the effect. The cause must become such that its effect is necessary. Once this is understood, the alleged doubt (that the possible becomes necessary) is resolved.

Key Terms
واجب الصدور
وجوب
مؤثر
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The effect must become necessary in itself to exist.
Correction: Sanusi says the necessity is in the cause, not the effect. The effect remains possible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, where does the necessity of emanation reside?
A) In the essence of the effect.
B) In the essence of the cause.
C) In both equally.
D) In the relationship between cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of emanation is a property of the cause, not the effect, so the possible never becomes necessary in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Condition from Cause in Causality
Sanusi distinguishes condition from cause in causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes a doubt about causality by distinguishing between a condition (شرط) and a cause (علة).

Core Idea
An effect's need is a condition for causality, not the cause itself.

Sanusi addresses a doubt that questions the efficiency of causes by focusing on the effect's need. He clarifies that the effect's possibility and need are merely conditions (شرط) for the cause's action, not the cause (علة) itself. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the correct model of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic sentence once. Notice its brevity - a single concise response to a doubt.
2
Identify the key distinction
Sanusi contrasts 'condition' (شرط) and 'cause' (علة). Underline these terms and note that the effect's need is merely a condition, not the cause.
In kalam, a condition enables but does not produce; a cause produces.
3
Reflect on the doubt
Consider what the seventeenth doubt might be: perhaps that the effect's need would render the cause ineffective. Sanusi's answer shows the need is only a prerequisite, not a flaw.
This preserves the cause's true efficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السابعة عشر : أن نقول كون الأثر ممكن الوجود ومحتاجاً ، شرط لتأثير المؤثرية فيه ، والفرق بين الشرط وبين العلة معلوم .
And the answer to the seventeenth doubt: that we say: the effect's being possible and in need is a condition for the cause's effectiveness upon it, and the difference between condition and cause is known.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage refutes a doubt about the effect's need serving as a condition. The opponents likely argued that the effect's need implies the cause is not truly efficient. Sanusi replies that the effect's possibility and need are merely a condition for the cause's action, not the cause itself. The distinction between condition (شرط) and cause (علة) is well-known in kalam.

Key Terms

محتاج

A) It is the cause itself.
B) It is a condition for the cause's effectiveness.
C) It is irrelevant.
D) It prevents the cause from acting.

Key Takeaway
The effect's possibility and need are a condition for the cause's action, not the cause itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refutation of the Objection: Causality as an Additional Attribute
Sanusi refutes the claim that causality is an additional attribute leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why causality cannot be an additional attribute and how Sanusi's argument avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Causality is the cause's relation, not an extra attribute.

In this brief but potent passage, Sanusi wraps up his responses to a series of doubts by addressing the claim that when a cause becomes effective, it gains a new attribute called causality. He shows that this would lead to an infinite chain of attributes, proving instead that causality is not an independent entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of the refutation.
2
Identify the doubt
Recognize the claim being refuted: causality (المؤثرية) is a new determination (حكم حادث) when a cause becomes effective.
3
Understand the counter-argument
Sanusi argues that if causality were an additional attribute (صفة زائدة), it would require another attribute to bring it about, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible.
This is a classic application of the infinite regress argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, causality is not an attribute existing in external entities (ليست صفة موجودة في الأعيان). It is a relation or consideration of the cause.
This aligns with the broader theological principle that attributes are not multiplied unnecessarily.
5
Reflect on the broader context
This passage concludes the series of answers to doubts. Consider how it fits into discussions about divine attributes and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة عشر : وهي قولهم : و المؤثر إذا صار مؤثراً في الأثر فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ، فنقول : لو كان المؤثر صفة زائدة للزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، فظهر أنها ليست صفة موجودة في الأعيان . فهذا جملة الكلام ( في الجواب ) (٢) عن هذه الشبهات ( والله ولى الإرشاد والهدايـــة والعصمة بفضله ) (٣) .
And the answer to the eighteenth doubt: it is their statement: 'When the cause becomes a cause in the effect, that causality is a new determination.' So we say: If causality were an additional attribute, it would entail infinite regress, which is impossible. Thus it becomes clear that it is not an attribute existing in external entities. This is the summary of the discourse in answer to these doubts. And Allah is the patron of guidance and rectitude and protection by His grace.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage refutes the doubt that causality (المؤثرية) is a new attribute added to the cause, which would lead to infinite regress. Sanusi argues that if causality were an additional attribute, then that attribute would itself need another attribute, ad infinitum, which is impossible. Therefore, causality is not an extra attribute but is the cause's essence in relation. This concludes the whole set of answers to the doubts.

Key Terms
مؤثرية
صفة زائدة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that causality is an additional attribute that emerges when a cause acts.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if this were true, it would lead to an infinite regress of attributes, so causality is not an additional attribute but a relation.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi deny that causality is an additional attribute?
A) Because it would lead to an infinite regress.
B) Because it is a relation, not an attribute.
C) Because attributes are eternal.
D) Because the effect does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Causality is not an extra attribute but the cause's relation; otherwise, an infinite regress would follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you ever feel a sense of personal power as if you were the ultimate cause? How does the truth that causality is only a relation—not an attribute inherent in you—change your understanding of your own role, and what attitude should you adopt right now towards Allah?
?
If causality were an attribute, it would lead to an infinite chain. In your daily life, are there any small 'causes' you treat as independent, forgetting they are merely instruments? What one specific habit can you change today to sincerely attribute causality only to Allah?

The Essence of the Cause Necessitates the Effect
Statement about essence necessitating effect and arising relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an effect is necessitated by the specific essence of its cause, not by any external condition, and that relational attributes like 'effecter' and 'effect' arise only after the effect occurs.

Core Idea
The cause's essence alone suffices to bring about its effect.

In this brief but dense passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, the author presents a key metaphysical claim: the specific essence of a cause necessitates the occurrence of its effect. Only after the effect exists do the relational concepts of 'effecter' and 'effect' come into being. This lesson unpacks that statement and explores its implications for understanding causality and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the enumeration and sigla — these are manuscript markers. Focus on the main claim from 'باعتبار ذاته المخصوصة' onward.
The numbers (1), (2), (3) refer to manuscript variants and are not part of the doctrinal content.
2
Identify the two stages
The passage presents two moments: (1) The essence of the effect necessitates the effect itself. (2) After the effect occurs, two accidental relations arise: the cause being an 'effecter' and the effect being an 'effect'.
The relations are attributes that accrue to the essence after the effect exists.
3
Reflect on the metaphysical principle
Consider why the author emphasizes that the necessity comes from the essence itself, not from anything added. What does this imply about God's action and attributes?
This principle guards against the idea that God needs an external condition to create.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أن يصدر ذلك (س) . (۲) من (س) . (۳) من (ز). باعتبار ذاته المخصوصة ، فذات الأثر توجب حصول الأثر ، ثم عند حصول ذات الأثر تحصل الإضافتان العارضتان للذات أعني : كون أحدهما مؤثراً في الآخر ، وكون الآخر أثراً للأول .
Considering its specific essence, the essence of the effect necessitates the occurrence of the effect, then upon the occurrence of the essence of the effect, the two accidental relations to the essence arise, namely: that one is an effecter in the other, and that the other is an effect of the first.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a metaphysical statement about causality and relations. It asserts that when considering the specific essence (dhat) of an effect, that essence itself necessitates the occurrence of the effect. Then, after the effect occurs, two relational attributes (idafatan) accidentally attach to the essence: one is that the cause is an effecter (mu'aththir) in the other, and the other is that the effect is an effect (athar) of the cause. The point is that the necessity of the effect's existence is grounded in the essence of the cause, not in an external condition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that the effect's occurrence requires an external attribute or condition added to the cause's essence.
Correction: The passage argues that the effect is necessitated solely by the specific essence itself, not by any external attribute.

Checkpoint
