A) The effect itself
B) The two accidental relations
C) The cause
D) The possibility

Key Takeaway
The specific essence of a cause is sufficient to necessitate its effect; relational attributes are secondary and arise only after the effect exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that your actions, prayers, or efforts are the real causes of outcomes, as if God's essence needs your contribution to bring about effects? Where in your life does this subtle shirk appear?
?
If the cause's essence alone is sufficient for the effect, why do you still feel anxious about results? What does that anxiety reveal about your trust in the sufficiency of God's essence?
?
The relations of cause and effect are merely accidental—does this change how you view your own role in bringing about good deeds? Will you now rely solely on God's essence and abandon the illusion that your efforts are independently effective?

Answer to the Sixteenth Shubha: The Necessity Belongs to the Effecter
Refutes the claim that a possible being must become necessarily emanating from the effecter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the necessity of emanation in causality is an attribute of the cause's essence, not of the effect's, and how this resolves the sixteenth objection.

Core Idea
Necessity resides in the cause, not in the effect.

This lesson addresses the sixteenth objection (shubha) against the emanation of possible beings. The objectors claim that a possible being only exists when it becomes necessarily emanating from its cause. The author agrees but draws a crucial distinction: the necessity belongs to the essence of the cause, not to the effect. Understanding this distinction is key to seeing why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: objection, concession, correction, explanation, conclusion.
The passage is dense; take time to parse each phrase.
2
Identify the shubha and the initial response
The objectors say a possible being must become 'necessarily emanating' from the effecter. The author first agrees ('this is correct') but then qualifies.
The author does not reject the statement entirely; he reinterprets it.
3
Focus on the key distinction
The necessity (wujub) is an attribute of the effecter's essence, not of the effect's essence. That is the core of the answer.
This shifts the location of necessity from the effect to the cause.
4
Understand the reinterpretation
The author explains: 'as long as the effecter does not become such that its effecting is necessary, the effect does not issue.' This clarifies that it is the cause that becomes necessarily productive, not the effect that becomes necessary.
With this reinterpretation, the shubha dissolves.
5
Reflect on why this settles the objection
Ask yourself: If necessity is in the cause, does that contradict the contingency of the effect? The effect remains possible in itself; only the cause's action is necessary given its attributes. This preserves both divine agency and the contingency of creation.
This connects to broader themes in kalam about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السادسة عشر : وهي قولهم : « الممكن ما لم يصر واجب الصدور عن المؤثر لم يوجد ، فنقول : هذا حق : إلا أن ذلك الوجوب يكون صفة لذات المؤثر لا لذات الأثر ، وتفسير هذا الكلام : أنه ما لم يصر المؤثر بحيث يجب تأثيره في ذلك الأثر لم يصدر عنه ذلك الأثر ، وبهذا التفسير يسقط ما ذكروه من الشبهة .
And the answer to the sixteenth shubha: which is their saying: 'The possible being, as long as it does not become necessarily emanating from the effecter, it does not exist.' So we say: This is correct, except that that necessity is an attribute of the essence of the effecter, not of the essence of the effect. And the explanation of this statement: that as long as the effecter does not become such that its effecting in that effect is necessary, that effect does not issue from it. And with this explanation, what they mentioned of the shubha falls away.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage answers an objection (shubha) regarding the necessity of emanation. The objectors argue that a possible being only exists when it becomes necessarily emanating from the cause. The author agrees but clarifies that the necessity (wujub) is a quality of the cause's essence, not of the effect's essence. In other words, the cause must become necessarily productive of the effect; the effect's own essence does not possess necessity. By reinterpreting the claim as being about the cause's attribute, the objection is resolved.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that for a possible being to exist, the possible being itself must become necessary (wajib al-wujud) in its own essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the necessity is in the effecter's essence, not the effect's. The effect remains possible in itself; only the cause's action is necessary.

Checkpoint

A) In the essence of the effect
B) In the essence of the effecter
C) In both equally
D) In the relation between them

Key Takeaway
The necessity of emanation belongs to the cause's essence, not to the effect, thus preserving the contingency of the possible being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems inevitable, do you feel like you have no choice? How does the teaching that necessity belongs to the cause, not the effect, change how you see your own actions? Will you now look for the cause of that necessity and reclaim your responsibility?
?
The objectors thought that for something to exist, it must become necessary. Have you ever felt that your own existence or worth depends on being 'necessary' or 'inevitable'? How does understanding that you are a possible being, contingent yet real, affect your sense of humility and gratitude to God?
?
This lesson says the effect remains possible even if the cause is necessary. Do you ever blame your circumstances (causes) for forcing you to act a certain way? If the effect is still possible, then your choice remains real. Will you stop hiding behind causality and own your choices starting today?

The Condition vs. Cause Distinction in Effecting
The effect's possibility as condition, not cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa) in the context of divine effecting, and why the effect's possibility is only a condition.

Core Idea
Possibility of an effect is a condition, not a cause, for the effecter's action.

In responding to objections about causality, the author clarifies a crucial conceptual distinction: the effect's being possible and needy does not cause the effecter to act; rather, it is a prerequisite condition. Understanding this difference prevents conflating what is merely required with what actually produces the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the key terms 'shart' (condition) and 'illa' (cause).
The passage is a direct response to a shubha; see how it first states the answer.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify 'shart' (condition) and 'illa' (cause). What are their Arabic roots? Understand that a condition is an external requisite, while a cause directly produces the effect.
The passage says the difference between them is 'known' – it is a standard distinction in kalam.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on why the effect's being possible and needy cannot be the cause. If it were the cause, then every possible thing would necessarily exist, which is false. Instead, it is a condition that must be present for effecting to occur.
This distinction protects divine freedom: Allah is not compelled by the possibility of things.
4
Apply to the shubha
The shubha likely argued that because the effect is possible and needy, the effecter must act. The author points out that this conflates condition with cause. The possibility is merely a condition, not a cause that necessitates action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة السابعة عشر : أن نقول كون الأثر ممكن الوجود ومحتاجاً ، شرط لتأثير المؤثرية فيه ، والفرق بين الشرط وبين العلة معلوم
And the answer to the seventeenth shubha: that we say: the effect's being possible of existence and needful is a condition for the effecter's effecting in it, and the difference between the condition and the cause is known.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage addresses a shubha by distinguishing between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa). The objectors might have conflated the two. The author states that the effect's attributes of being possible and needful are conditions for the effecter to act upon it, not the cause of the effect. The distinction between condition and cause is well-known in kalam: a condition is an external requisite, while a cause directly produces the effect. Thus, the possibility of the effect does not necessitate the effecter's action; it merely sets the stage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the effect's possibility is the cause of its existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the effect's possibility is a condition for the effecter's action, not a cause. Conditions do not necessitate; they are prerequisites.

Checkpoint

A) It is the cause
B) It is a condition
C) It is irrelevant
D) It is the result

Key Takeaway
The effect's being possible and needy is a condition, not a cause, for the effecter's action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience deep need and helplessness, do you expect Allah to act as if your need were a cause, or do you accept it as merely a condition that He may or may not address? How does this shift your reliance from demanding to trusting?
?
If the possibility of your repentance is only a condition, not a cause, for Allah's forgiveness, then what is the true cause? Is it solely His will? If so, why do you often feel your own remorse is the reason?
?
You often list your good deeds as reasons for Allah's blessings. But this lesson says they are conditions, not causes. Are you relying on conditions or on the Causer? How can you change your gratitude to reflect this?

Answer to the Eighteenth Shubha: Effecting as a Relational Attribute
Refuting that the attribute of effecting is a new attribute leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of effecting (mu'aththariya) cannot be an additional existent attribute without leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If effecting were an extra attribute, it would need another cause—trapped in infinite regress.

The eighteenth doubt claims that when a cause becomes effective, that effectiveness is a new attribute. But does that attribute itself need a cause? In this final answer, Sanusi shows that positing a real attribute of effecting leads to an infinite chain, proving it must be a relation, not a real attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the gist of the argument.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that when a cause becomes effective, that effectiveness is a new judgment (hukm hadith). Locate this in the passage.
The objector implies this new judgment is an additional attribute.
3
Understand the author's reply
The author replies with a conditional: if this effecting were an additional attribute, it would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Follow the logic.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, effecting is not an existent attribute in external reality (a'yan). It must be a relational concept. Note the final du'a.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters for understanding divine attributes: Allah's attribute of causing existence is not a new, created attribute but is one with His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثامنة عشر : وهي قولهم : و المؤثر إذا صار مؤثراً في الأثر فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ، فنقول : لو كان المؤثر صفة زائدة للزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، فظهر أنها ليست صفة موجودة في الأعيان . فهذا جملة الكلام ( في الجواب ) (۲) عن هذه الشبهات ( والله ولى الإرشاد والهدايـــة والعصمة بفضله ) (۳)
And the answer to the eighteenth shubha: which is their saying: 'And when the effecter becomes effecting in the effect, then that effecting is a new judgment.' So we say: if effecting were an additional attribute, then infinite regress would follow, and that is impossible. So it becomes clear that it is not an existent attribute in individuals. This is the sum of the speech in answer to these shubuhat. And Allah is the Guide and Protector by His grace.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage concludes the responses to the shubuhat by addressing the claim that the attribute of 'effecting' (mu'aththariya) is a new, contingent attribute. The objectors argue that when a cause becomes effective, that effectiveness is a new attribute. The author replies that if this effectiveness were a real attribute, it would itself require another cause, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the effecting is not an existent attribute in the external world; it is a relational concept. The passage ends with a du'a.

Key Terms
مؤثرية
حكم حادث
صفة زائدة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that effectiveness (mu'aththariya) is a real, additional attribute that exists in the cause.
Correction: The author shows that if it were an additional attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress of causes, which is impossible. Therefore, it is a relational concept, not an existent attribute.

Checkpoint
What would follow if the attribute of effecting were an additional existent attribute?
A) The effect would become necessary
B) Infinite regress
C) The attribute would be eternal
D) The cause would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The attribute of effecting is not an additional existent attribute; it is a relational concept, because positing it as real leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a cause produce an effect, do you ever think that cause has an inherent power of effecting? This lesson shows that such a belief leads to infinite regress. How will you remind yourself that all effectiveness ultimately comes from Allah?
?
Have you ever assumed that a change in a creature implies a new attribute in that creature? The refutation here shows that not every new judgment corresponds to a real attribute. How will you guard your thinking about causality from this mistake?
?
If effecting is not an extra attribute, then your own actions do not have an extra attribute of effectiveness. Does this humble you or make you feel powerless? How will you turn your reliance away from your own supposed power and toward the only True Effecter, Allah?

Classification of Possible Beings and the First Objection
Classification of possible beings and an objection to the need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of possible beings based on their acceptance of existence and non-existence, and the objection that preponderance of existence might exempt a possible from needing a cause.

Core Idea
Preponderance of existence does not make a possible being self-sufficient; the cause must be established by proof.

In this passage, the author begins a new chapter by defining possible beings and dividing them into two categories. He then presents a crucial objection: if existence is naturally preponderant for a possible, why would it need a cause? The response will require establishing that preponderance itself is not self-explanatory. Let's examine the author's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the definition of 'possible' and the two divisions.
The text has some OCR errors, but the main ideas are clear.
2
Identify the two types of possible beings
Find where the author divides possible beings into two categories. What distinguishes them?
One type has equal acceptance, the other has preponderance of one side.
3
Understand the objection
The author raises a question: why can't we say that when existence is preponderant, the possible being is independent of a cause? Summarize this objection.
This is the key challenge that the rest of the chapter aims to refute.
4
Note the beginning of the first proof
The author mentions 'flowing existents' as evidence that preponderance requires a cause. Since the passage cuts off, consider what the author might be leading to.
This proof will be completed in subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادسة في إيرار نواب آخر بين أن العمال على قولين ، : المكن لا بد له محبت مرجع : أما تقرير السؤال الأول : فهو أن نقول : الممكن هو الذي يقبل الوجود ، ويقبل العدم، إلا أنا نقول : الذي يكون كذلك على قسمين : أحدهما : أن يكون قبول الماهية للموجود والعدم على السوية من غير تفاوت ( اصلا ) . والثاني : أن يكون قابلاً ) لهما ) ، إلا أن أحد الطرفين يكون أولى بتلك الماهية من الطرف الآخر ، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن هذه الممكنات وأن كانت موصوفة بالإمكان ، بمعنى أن صفاتها قابلة للعدم وللوجود ، إلا أنها غنية عن السبب وعن المؤثر ، وذلك لأجل أن الوجود أولى بها من العدم، فهذه الماهيات لأجل كونها قابلة للوجود والعدم ، تكون من جملة الممكنات ، إلا أنها لأجل أن الوجود أولى بها من العدم تكون غنية عن السبب ، فما لم تقيموا البرهان على فساد هذا الاحتمال ، لم يتم ما ذكرتموه من الحجة والبرهان ، ثم نقول : الذي يدل على أن حصول هذه الأولوية معقول جملة وجوه : الأول : الموجودات السيالة ، كالزمان والحركة والصوت ، لاشك أن
Chapter Six, on presenting another debate? that the workers are of two opinions: the possible must have … love reference? As for the formulation of the first question: it is that we say: the possible is that which accepts existence and accepts non-existence, but we say: that which is so is of two divisions: one: that the acceptance of the quiddity for existence and non-existence is equal without any difference (at all). And the second: that it accepts both, but one of the two sides is more preponderant for that quiddity than the other side. When you have understood this, we say: why is it not permissible to say that these possible things, even though they are described by possibility, meaning that their attributes are receptive of non-existence and existence, yet they are independent of the cause and the effecter, and that because existence is more preponderant for them than non-existence? So these quiddities, because they are receptive of existence and non-existence, are among the possible things, but because existence is more preponderant for them than non-existence, they are independent of the cause. So as long as you do not establish the proof for the invalidity of this probability, what you have mentioned of argument and proof is not complete. Then we say: what indicates that the occurrence of this preponderance is intelligible are several aspects: the first: flowing existents, such as time, motion, and sound, without doubt that...
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (al-fasl al-sadis) that presents another objection (irad nawab? ) concerning the need for a cause. The author first formulates the question: a possible being is defined as that which can exist or not exist. Then he divides such beings into two types: (1) those whose quiddity accepts existence and non-existence equally, without any preference; and (2) those where one side (existence or non-existence) is preponderant over the other. The objection then is: why can we not say that these possible things, though described as possible (i.e., receptive of both), are independent of a cause because existence is preponderant for them? If this were allowed, then the argument for a cause would fail. The author then promises to provide proofs that this preponderance is not self-sufficient, and begins the first proof: 'flowing existents' like time, motion, and sound. The text cuts off before completing the proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is more preponderant for a possible being, then it does not need a cause.
Correction: Preponderance itself is a property that requires explanation; being preponderant does not guarantee self-sufficiency. The author aims to prove that preponderance necessitates a cause.

Take a moment to silently acknowledge your absolute need for Him in every aspect.

This chapter calls you to see even the preponderance of your desires as something that must be caused by Allah. Will you submit to that truth today?

The Objection: Priority of Existence Without a Cause
Presents an objection that possible things may need no cause due to priority of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that some possible things may not require a cause due to the priority of existence, and how it challenges the standard causal argument.

Core Idea
Priority of existence might make a cause unnecessary.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the standard theological position that every possible being requires a cause. The objector argues that if existence is inherently more appropriate (awlā) to a possible thing, that priority itself could suffice, eliminating the need for an external cause. Understanding this objection is crucial because it forces the defender to prove that priority does not remove the need for a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the chapter heading and the structure of the question.
The passage has a clear logical flow: definition, division, objection.
2
Identify the two divisions of the possible
The passage divides the possible into two types based on whether acceptance of existence and non-existence is equal or one side is more appropriate. Find these in the text.
This division is key to the objection.
3
Understand the objection's core claim
The objector suggests that if existence is more appropriate to a possible thing, it may be independent of a cause. Grasp how this challenges the standard view.
4
Consider the challenge to the defender
The objector demands proof that this scenario is impossible, otherwise the argument for a cause fails. Reflect on why this is a strong move.
The defender must show that priority does not suffice.
5
Prepare for the proofs
The author will later provide proofs against this objection. Notice that the passage sets up the need for those proofs.
This lesson only presents the objection, not the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادسة في إيراد [نواب؟] آخر بيان أن العمال على قولين، الممكن لا بد له من سبب أو مؤثر: أما تقرير السؤال الأول: فهو أن نقول: الممكن هو الذي يقبل الوجود ويقبل العدم، إلا أنا نقول: الذي يكون كذلك على قسمين: أحدهما: أن يكون قبول الماهية للموجود والعدم على السوية من غير تفاوت أصلا. والثاني: أن يكون قابلا لهما إلا أن أحد الطرفين يكون أولى بتلك الماهية من الطرف الآخر. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذه الممكنات وإن كانت موصوفة بالإمكان بمعنى أن صفاتها قابلة للعدم وللوجود إلا أنها غنية عن السبب وعن المؤثر وذلك لأجل أن الوجود أولى بها من العدم. فهذه الماهيات لأجل كونها قابلة للوجود والعدم تكون من جملة الممكنات إلا أنها لأجل أن الوجود أولى بها من العدم تكون غنية عن السبب. فما لم تقيموا البرهان على فساد هذا الاحتمال لم يتم ما ذكرتموه من الحجة والبرهان.
Chapter Six: Presenting another [reply?] explaining that the matter is two statements: The possible must have a cause or effecter. As for the presentation of the first question: it is that we say: The possible is that which accepts existence and accepts non-existence, except that we say: that which is like that is of two divisions: one: that the acceptance of the essence for existence and non-existence is equal without any difference. And the second: that it is acceptable to both except that one of the two sides is more appropriate (awlā) to that essence than the other side. When you understand this we say: Why may it not be said that these possible things, even if they are described with possibility (imkān) meaning that their attributes are acceptive of non-existence and existence, yet they are independent of the cause (sabab) and the effecter (mu'aththir), because existence is more appropriate to them than non-existence. So these essences, because they are acceptive of existence and non-existence, are among the possible things, but because existence is more appropriate to them than non-existence, they are independent of the cause. So as long as you do not establish the proof of the corruption of this probability, what you have mentioned of argument and proof is not complete.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (sū'āl) to the standard theological position that every possible being requires a cause for its existence. It begins with a chapter heading that frames the discussion as a second reply or question. The objection divides the possible (mumkin) into two types: one where the essence accepts existence and non-existence equally, and another where one side is more appropriate (awlā) than the other. The objector then argues: why cannot one say that some possible things—those where existence is more appropriate—are independent of any cause precisely because of this priority? The existence of such things would be 'natural' to them, so they do not need an external agent. The objector demands that the defender prove the impossibility of this scenario, otherwise the original argument (that all possibles need a cause) fails. The author then promises to provide several proofs that such priority is conceivable, setting up the subsequent lessons.

Key Terms
ممكن (mumkin)
إمكان (imkān)
أولوية (awlawiyya)
سبب (sabab)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
احتمال (ihtimāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that possibility alone automatically implies dependence on a cause, but the objector argues that priority of one state could remove that need.
Correction: The objection shows that if existence is more appropriate to a possible thing, it may be independent of a cause; the defender must address this.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what condition would allow a possible thing to be independent of a cause?
A) It accepts existence and non-existence equally.
B) Existence is more appropriate to it than non-existence.
C) It is internally necessary.
D) It is externally caused.

Key Takeaway
The objection claims that if existence is more appropriate to a possible thing, it may not need a cause; the defender must prove this impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector suggests that if existence is 'more appropriate' to a thing, it can exist without a cause. Do you ever treat your own talents or blessings as 'natural' and forget they come from Allah? What would it take for you to constantly acknowledge His causality in everything you have?
?
This passage divides possible things into those that are equal and those with priority. Where do you place yourself—do you ever feel that your existence is more appropriate than non-existence, as if you deserve to be? How does that feeling affect your humility before your Creator?
?
If you accepted that something could exist without cause, what implications would that have for your reliance on Allah? Are you willing to examine your life for subtle assumptions of self-sufficiency that this passage challenges?

First Proof: Flowing Beings
Uses flowing entities like time to show priority of non-existence is conceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of flowing beings shows that priority of non-existence is a real, conceivable state, and why this makes priority of existence equally conceivable.

Core Idea
Flowing beings prove priority is real, so it can apply to existence too.

This lesson presents the first of several proofs that priority (awlawiyya) is a rational concept. The author selects a class of entities that are constantly passing away—like time, motion, and sound—to show that non-existence is more appropriate to them. By observing this, we can conceive of priority on the side of non-existence, and by analogy, priority on the side of existence is also conceivable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's words set the stage.
2
Identify the three examples
The passage mentions time, motion, and sound as flowing beings. Notice their common feature: they exist and immediately vanish.
3
Follow the logical steps
The proof shows these things are possible (they exist), yet non-existence is more appropriate. This establishes priority of non-existence.
4
Understand the analogy
If we accept priority of non-existence as conceivable, why not priority of existence? The same reasoning applies symmetrically.
This answers the objection that priority might be absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: الذي يدل على أن حصول هذه الأولوية معقول جملة وجوه: الأول: الموجودات السيالة، كالزمان والحركة والصوت، لاشك أن العدم أولى بها، والمراد من كونها سيالة أنها توجد وتنقضي ويمتنع بقاؤها بأعيانها. فنقول: الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك، أن هذا الآن الحاضر، وهذا الجزء الحاضر من الصوت لاشك أنه قابل للوجود، إذ لو لم يكن قابلاً للوجود لما دخل في الوجود، ولا شك أن العدم أولى بها، إذ لو لم تحصل هذه الأولوية لما كان واجب الانقراض والانقضاء، فثبت في هذه الأشياء أنها قابلة للوجود وقابلة للعدم، ثم إن العدم أولى بها من الوجود، فإذا عقلنا حصول هذه الأولوية في جانب العدم فلم لا نعقل حصولها في جانب الوجود؟
Then we say: What indicates that the occurrence of this priority (awlawiyya) is conceivable are several aspects: The first: The flowing beings (al-mawjūdāt al-sayyāla), such as time, motion, and sound. There is no doubt that non-existence is more appropriate to them, and the meaning of their being flowing is that they come into existence and pass away, and their persistence in their identities is impossible. We say: The proof that the matter is so is that this present 'now' and this present part of sound are without doubt acceptive of existence, for if they were not acceptive of existence, they would not have entered into existence. And there is no doubt that non-existence is more appropriate to them, for if this priority did not occur, they would not be necessarily passing away and terminating. So it is established in these things that they are acceptive of existence and acceptive of non-existence, and then non-existence is more appropriate to them than existence. So if we understand the occurrence of this priority on the side of non-existence, why should we not understand its occurrence on the side of existence?
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This lesson presents the first proof that priority (awlawiyya) is a conceivable notion. The author selects 'flowing beings' (mawjūdāt sayyāla)—entities like time, motion, and sound that come into being and immediately pass away, unable to persist. For these, non-existence is clearly more appropriate: a given moment or sound is just about to vanish. Yet they are still possible (qābil li'l-wujūd), because they do exist momentarily. The argument proceeds: since we can observe that non-existence is naturally predominant over existence in these cases, we have conceptualized priority on the side of non-existence. By analogy, priority on the side of existence is equally conceivable: there could be things where existence is the dominant state, even if they remain possible. The proof is meant to show that the objection's hypothesis—that some possibles might have existence as their predominant state without needing a cause—is not absurd.

Key Terms
موجودات سيالة (mawjūdāt sayyāla)
قابل للوجود (qābil li'l-wujūd)
أولوية العدم (awlawiyyat al-'adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority of non-existence only makes sense if it necessitates non-existence.
Correction: The author argues that priority can be without entailing necessity; flowing beings have priority of non-existence but are still possible.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first proof?
A) Time and motion are necessary beings.
B) Priority of non-existence is observed in flowing beings, so priority of existence is also conceivable.
C) All possible things need a cause.
D) Sound is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The example of flowing beings shows that priority of non-existence is a real, conceivable state, which by analogy makes priority of existence equally conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that non-existence is 'more appropriate' for flowing beings like sound and motion. Reflect on your own life: In what ways is your existence also 'flowing'—each moment passing away? How does this realization affect your urgency to use your time for what matters?
?
If non-existence is the 'more appropriate' state for all flowing beings, then your current moment of existence is a gift that overrides that priority. How does this change your attitude toward your next hour? What will you do differently today because you recognize that existence is not guaranteed?
?
The proof argues by analogy that priority on the side of existence is also conceivable. This means that there are things where existence is predominant but you still neglect them. Can you identify something in your life where existence (or goodness) is predominant but you still neglect it? What concrete step will you take tonight to prioritize that?

Second Proof: Conditional Causality
Shows that causes sometimes produce effects only under conditions, indicating priority without necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conditional causality demonstrates that priority (awlawiyya) can exist without necessity (wujūb).

Core Idea
Priority does not require necessity — conditions can block an effect.

In this lesson, we examine the second proof for the conceivability of priority without necessity. Using the example of heaviness and obstacles, the author shows that a cause may have a natural tendency (priority) toward an effect, yet the effect may not occur if a condition is absent. This illustrates that existence (wujūd) can be the priority for a possible being without being absolutely necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: statement of principle, example, conclusion.
2
Understand the example of heaviness
Identify how heaviness relates to descent. What is the condition mentioned? What happens when the condition is absent?
The condition is 'absence of an obstacle'.
3
Distinguish priority from necessity
Reflect on why the author says priority does not reach the limit of necessity. How does the example support this?
Priority is a weaker relation than necessity.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Relate this to the objector's hypothesis: priority in existence without necessity is logically possible. How does the proof strengthen that claim?
This is part of a series of proofs for the same concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: في تقرير هذا الكلام: أنه قد تكون العلة المؤثرة في حصول الأثر بحيث يتوقف تأثيرها في ذلك الأثر على حصول شرط مخصوص، مثل الثقل، فإنه إنما يوجب النزول بشرط عدم المانع. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لا شك أن الأولى بالثقل اقتضاء النزول والسقوط إلا أن عند قوات [فقدان؟] ذلك الشرط قد يتخلف عند ذلك الأثر. فهذا يدل على أن الأولوية قد تحصل في جانب الوجود مع أنها لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب.
The second aspect: In the presentation of this speech: that the effective cause ('illa mu'aththira) in the occurrence of the effect may be such that its influence on that effect depends on the occurrence of a specific condition, like heaviness (thiqal), for it only necessitates descent on condition of the absence of an obstacle. When you understand this, we say: There is no doubt that the more appropriate (awlā) for heaviness is the requirement of descent and falling, except that when that condition is absent [?], that effect may be withheld. This indicates that priority (awlawiyya) may occur on the side of existence while it does not reach the limit of necessity (wujūb).
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This lesson provides the second evidence for the conceivability of priority without necessity. The author points to causal relationships where an effect depends on a specific condition. For example, heaviness (thiqal) naturally tends to cause descent, but if an obstacle is present, the descent does not occur. Here, heaviness has a priority (awlawiyya) toward causing descent, but this priority is not a necessity (wujūb) because it falters when the condition is missing. Similarly, the objector's hypothesis posits that existence may be the priority for a possible thing without being necessary; there is no logical contradiction in such a scenario. The proof aims to show that priority is a real and intelligible concept that can exist without reaching the level of necessity.

Key Terms
علة مؤثرة ('illa mu'aththira)
أولوية (awlawiyya)
وجوب (wujūb)
شرط (shart)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might conflate priority with necessity, thinking that if something has a natural tendency toward an effect, it must always occur.
Correction: The example of heaviness shows that priority can be blocked by conditions; thus priority does not entail necessity.

Checkpoint
How does the example of heaviness support the conceivability of priority without necessity?
A) Heaviness always causes descent.
B) Heaviness has priority for descent but can be blocked by an obstacle.
C) Heaviness is a necessary being.
D) Heaviness never causes descent.

Key Takeaway
The example of heaviness demonstrates that a cause can have a genuine priority toward an effect without that priority amounting to necessity, thereby supporting the logical possibility of priority without necessity in existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you treating that expectation as if it were a necessity, assuming it must happen? How does the lesson's teaching that priority does not equal necessity challenge you to surrender that expectation to Allah's will?
?
The example of heaviness shows that even a natural tendency can be blocked by a condition you may not see. Where in your life are you holding onto a sense of entitlement or certainty about your plans? Will you accept the possibility that the outcome may be withheld for a wisdom you cannot perceive, and remain content with Allah's decree?

Philosophers' Consensus on Priority
Philosophers' division of possibles into three categories to show priority accepted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' division of possibles into majority, minority, and equal demonstrates that priority (awlawiyya) is a recognized concept without implying necessity.

Core Idea
Majority and minority are about tendency, not necessity.

The third proof draws on the philosophers' own classification of possible things. They divide possibles into those that exist most of the time, those that exist rarely, and those that are equal. This shows that the concept of 'priority' (one thing being more appropriate than another) is universally accepted, even among those who might deny causation. By understanding this division, you'll see how priority can be used in the argument without falling into the error of necessitarianism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a definition, a conclusion, and an example.
The passage begins with 'The third' indicating it is part of a series of proofs.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage states that possible things are divided into three: majority (akthariyya), minority (aqalliyya), and equal (mutasāwiya). Understand the meaning of each.
The definitions focus on what is 'more appropriate' (awlā) for existence or non-existence.
3
Analyze the example of earth's nature
The philosophers give an example: the nature of earth tends to be at the center (majority), but it can be forced upward (compulsion). This illustrates that majority does not mean always.
This example shows that priority is a real phenomenon even when no external cause is evident.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since this division is agreed upon among philosophers, it supports the objector's suggestion that priority can be conceived without necessity.
The proof aims to show that even those who deny causality accept the concept of priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على أن الممكنات على ثلاثة أقسام: منها أكثرية ومنها أقلية ومنها متساوية، ولا معنى للأكثري إلا ما يكون الوجود أولى به، مع أنه لا يمتنع أن يبقى على العدم، ولا معنى للأقلي إلا ما يكون العدم أولى به، مع أنه لا يمتنع أن يصير موجودا. وإذا كان هذا حكما متفقا عليه بين الفلاسفة كان كافيا في تقرير ذلك السؤال. ألا ترى أنهم قالوا: طبيعة الأرض الحصول في الوسط على سبيل الأكثرية لا على سبيل الدوام، لأنها قد تقتضي تصير ممنوعة عن إيجاد ذلك على سبيل القسر، مثل المدرة المرمية إلى فوق.
The third: The philosophers (falāsifa) agreed that the possible things (mumkināt) are of three divisions: some are of the majority (akthariyya), some of the minority (aqalliyya), and some are equal. And there is no meaning of 'majority' except that existence is more appropriate to it, despite that it is not prevented from remaining in non-existence; and no meaning of 'minority' except that non-existence is more appropriate to it, despite that it is not prevented from becoming existent. And if this is a judgment unanimously agreed upon among the philosophers, it is sufficient in establishing that question. Do you not see that they said: The nature of earth is to obtain in the center on the basis of majority, not on the basis of perpetuity, because it may require that it becomes prevented from causing that by way of compulsion (qasr), like a clod of earth thrown upward.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This lesson presents the third proof: the philosophers (falāsifa) themselves recognize that possibles can be divided into three categories based on the frequency of their existence: some exist most of the time (akthari), some rarely exist (aqallī), and some are equal. The 'majority' category means existence is more appropriate (awlā) to that thing, yet it is still not necessary—it can remain non-existent. The 'minority' means non-existence is more appropriate, yet it can become existent. This classification shows that priority (awlawiyya) is a standard concept in philosophical discourse. As an example, the nature of earth has a tendency to be at the center, but it can be forced upward, demonstrating that the priority is not absolute necessity. Since this division is uncontroversial among philosophers, it validates the objector's suggestion that priority can be conceived without implying necessity.

Key Terms
فلاسفة (falāsifa)
أكثرية (akthariyya)
أقلية (aqalliyya)
متساوية (mutasāwiya)
طبيعة (tabī'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is 'majority' or 'minority', it implies a necessitating cause.
Correction: The philosophers use these terms to describe natural tendencies without invoking external causes; the majority is not always, and the minority is not never.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what does 'majority' (akthariyya) mean?
A) The thing exists necessarily.
B) Existence is more appropriate to it, but it can still be non-existent.
C) It always exists.
D) It never exists.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' own division of possibles into majority, minority, and equal proves that priority (awlawiyya) is a recognized concept independent of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own habits: you may recite Qur'an daily (majority) or skip sometimes. Does knowing that even a majority tendency can be overridden (like earth forced upward) humble your self-reliance? Will you today acknowledge that your consistency is not from your own power but from Allah's permission?
?
The philosophers accepted that priority (awlā) is real without necessitating. When you sin, do you excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't help it'? Reflect: does that claim align with the lesson's teaching that majority does not force the outcome? What would it mean for your repentance if you truly believed you could have chosen otherwise?
?
If the philosophers' own division shows that non-existence is more appropriate for some things (aqalliyya), then your existence itself is a gift, not a necessity. How does that change your gratitude today? Commit to one specific action that shows you treat your life as a divine gift, not a given.

Second Proof: Priority is Like a Cause with Conditions
Razi uses the example of weight to show that priority can exist without necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's analogy of weight with conditions demonstrates that priority (awlawiyya) can exist without implying necessity (wujub).

Core Idea
Priority is like a cause with conditions: present without necessitating the effect.

In this second proof, Razi deepens the argument by introducing the analogy of causal conditions. He uses the familiar example of weight requiring the absence of impediments to show that something can be 'prior' without being 'necessary.' This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications for the debate about ontological priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own wording guide your understanding before analyzing.
Notice the structure: a general principle, an example, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the causal structure
Identify the effective cause (al-‘illa al-mu’aththira), the condition (al-shart), and the effect (al-athar). Notice that the cause's efficacy depends on the condition.
The text uses 'weight' (al-thiql) as the cause, 'descent' as the effect, and 'absence of impediment' as the condition.
3
Understand the weight analogy
Weight necessitates descent only when there is no impediment. If an impediment is present, descent is delayed or does not occur. Thus weight has a 'priority' for descent but not absolute necessity.
This shows that priority (awlawiyya) is a weaker relation than necessity (wujub).
4
Apply the analogy to the main thesis
Razi concludes that just as weight's priority does not force descent when conditions are missing, so too priority in existence does not entail necessity. This supports the possibility of priority without necessitation.
The point is to refute the claim that priority always implies necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في تقرير هذا الكلام : أنه قد تكون العلة المؤثرة في حصول الأثر بحيث يتوقف تأثيرها في ذلك الأثر على حصول شرط مخصوص، مثل الثقل، فإنه إنما يوجب النزول بشرط عدم المانع، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن الأولى بالثقل، اقتضاء (٤) النزول (٥) والسقوط ( إلا أن ) (٦) عند قوات ذلك الشرط، قد يتخلف عند ذلك الأثر . فهذا يدل على أن الأولوية قد تحصل في جانب الوجود مع أنها لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب
The second aspect: In explaining this statement: It may be that the effective cause in bringing about the effect is such that its efficacy in that effect depends on the presence of a specific condition, such as weight, for it only necessitates descent on condition that there is no impediment. If you understand this, then we say: There is no doubt that what is prioritized for weight is requiring (4) descent (5) and falling (except that) (6) when that condition is lost, the effect may be delayed. So this indicates that priority may occur on the side of existence without reaching the limit of necessity.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof by analogy with causation. The author explains that an effective cause may require a condition to produce its effect; for example, weight necessitates downward motion only in the absence of an impediment. Even though weight has a priority for descent, the effect may not occur if the condition is missing. Similarly, priority of existence can obtain without implying necessity. The point is that priority (awlawiyya) is not the same as necessity (wujūb); it is a weaker relation.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة (al-‘illa al-mu’aththira)
الشرط (al-shart)
الثقل (al-thiql)
الأولوية (al-awlawiyya)
الوجوب (al-wujub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of weight shows that weight always causes descent regardless of conditions.
Correction: In fact, the example shows that weight's effect depends on the absence of impediments; thus priority does not imply necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the example of weight show?
A) Weight always causes descent.
B) Priority can exist without necessity.
C) Causes are always effective regardless of conditions.
D) Conditions are irrelevant to causation.

Key Takeaway
Razi's weight analogy demonstrates that priority (awlawiyya) is a weaker relation than necessity (wujub); it can exist without necessitating the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed that because something was 'right' or 'obvious' it would definitely happen. How has this lesson exposed the gap between priority and necessity in your own expectations? Will you now check your assumptions for hidden conditions?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you ever treat your good intentions as if they guarantee acceptance? Where might you be confusing priority with necessity? How can the weight example help you reassess your spiritual hopes without despair?
?
Consider a deeply held conviction you have about yourself or your faith. On what basis do you consider it necessary? Has Razi's analogy revealed any unwarranted certainty? What condition might block its realization, and how does that change your approach to living it out?

Conclusion: What Has Priority of Existence is Independent of a Cause
Razi concludes that priority of existence necessitates independence from a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the priority of existence implies independence from a cause, and why this is a crucial step in proving a necessary being.

Core Idea
Priority of existence means self-sufficiency — no external cause needed.

Razi has just presented four proofs that some thing must have priority of existence over non-existence. Now he draws the critical conclusion: such a thing must be independent of any external cause. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step, showing why priority logically entails self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four proofs
The passage begins by referring to four proofs that priority of existence is established. Briefly recall what those proofs aimed to show — that something has priority.
This is the foundation for the conclusion.
2
Understand the claim
Read the first sentence: the thing with priority must be independent of a cause. Note the terms: 'effective cause' and 'originating agent'.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The evidence is a conditional analysis: assume no external cause. Either priority occurs by itself or not. Work through both scenarios.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion: priority implies independence. Since priority is established, independence is established. This identifies a necessary being.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما ثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية في الجملة فنقول : هذا الذي يكون الوجود أولى به من العدم، وجب أن يكون غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المؤثر والفاعل الموجد ، والدليل عليه : أن عند فرض عدم المؤثر المنفصل، إما أن يحصل أولا يحصل. فإن حصل الرجحان فقد كفت ماهيته في حصول هذا الرجحان، وما حصل بالذات امتنع استناده إلى الغير، وإن لم يحصل الرجحان فحينئذ لا تكون ذاته مقتضية لحصول أولوية الوجود، مع أنا فرضناه كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القول بحصول أولوية الوجود معقول، وثابت أن بتقدير حصول هذه الأولوية، فإنه يجب حصول الاستغناء عن العلة، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة .
And when it is established by these four proofs that it is necessary to acknowledge the occurrence of this priority in general, we say: That for which existence is prioritized over non-existence must be independent in its existence from the effective cause and the originating agent. The evidence for it is that when the absence of an external cause is assumed, either priority occurs or it does not. If priority occurs, then its quiddity suffices for the occurrence of this priority, and what occurs by itself cannot be attributed to another. And if priority does not occur, then its essence would not necessitate the occurrence of priority of existence, whereas we assumed it to be so. This is a contradiction. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement that priority of existence occurs is rational, and it is established that given the occurrence of this priority, independence from the cause is necessary. This is the completion of the discussion in establishing this argument.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage draws the conclusion from the four proofs. Since priority of existence is established for some things, the author argues that such a thing must be independent of an external cause. The reasoning is a conditional analysis: if we assume no external cause, either the thing has priority of existence by itself (in which case its own quiddity suffices) or it does not (which contradicts the assumption). Therefore, it must be self-sufficient. Thus, priority implies independence, and since we have established priority, we have also established that there exists something independent of a cause. This is a key argument for a necessary being.

Key Terms
الاستغناء عن العلة (al-istighna’ ‘an al-‘illa)
السبب المؤثر (al-sabab al-mu’aththir)
الفاعل (al-fa‘il)
الرجحان (al-rujhan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that everything that exists has priority and therefore is uncaused.
Correction: The argument only applies to that which has been proven to have priority; it does not claim everything has priority.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the four proofs about the thing that has priority of existence?
A) It is eternal.
B) It is dependent on a cause.
C) It is independent of a cause.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Priority of existence entails independence from any external cause, establishing the existence of a self-sufficient necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt truly independent in any aspect of your life, or do you constantly rely on others, systems, and circumstances? How does this lesson's claim that only the One with priority of existence is truly independent challenge your self-reliance?
?
If your very existence depends on God at every moment, what does that mean for the pride you take in your achievements? Will you today consciously acknowledge your absolute need for Him in every breath, or will you continue living as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Razi proves that what has priority must be uncaused. Does your heart rest in that Uncaused Cause, or are you still seeking security from created causes that can fail? What is the one created thing you trust most — and are you willing to transfer that trust entirely to God today?

Second Question: The Dependence of the Possible on an Efficient Cause
Razi begins the second objection regarding the need for an efficient cause for possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beginning of Razi's second objection to the kalam argument that the possible requires an efficient cause, specifically regarding eternal possible entities.

Core Idea
Even eternal possibles might challenge the need for a cause.

This lesson introduces Razi's second objection. He questions whether all possible beings, including those assumed eternal, truly depend on an efficient cause. The passage sets up a key debate in Islamic theology about causality and contingency.

Guided Path
1

The objection seems to target the need for a cause for entities that are perpetually existing.

وأما السؤال الثاني : على قولكم (1) الممكن مفتقر إلى المؤثر » فهو أن نقول هذه الأجرام العلوية والسفلية بتقدير أن يقوم البرهان على كونها ممكنة الوجود في أنفسها . قابلة للوجود والعدم، إلا أن بتقدير كونها دائمة أزلية (٧)

The Three Possibilities for Essences and the Proof of Priority
Classifies essences into three possibilities and proves priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational divisions of essences and the proof that contingent essences must possess a priority for either existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
Contingent essences cannot be neutral; they require a built-in priority.

In this passage, al-Sanusi begins by classifying all possible essences into three categories based on how they relate to existence and non-existence. He then eliminates the first two, establishing that each contingent essence must have a specific disposition, which he calls priority (أولوية). This foundational concept underlies the entire discussion of causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and find the three possibilities Sanusi lists for essences regarding existence and non-existence.
The divisions are given in the first sentence.
2
Understand the refutations
See why the first two divisions are impossible: the first leads to a contradiction (absence of both), the second leads to an impossible non-specific existence.
Sanusi gives a clear proof for each refutation.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Realize that the third division must be true, and that this establishes the concept of priority (al-awlawiyya) for every contingent essence.
Priority means the essence inherently favors one of the two alternatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهيات إما أن لا تكون مقتضية الحصول هذين الأمرين، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما لا بعينه، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما بعينه، والأول باطل، وإلا لزم خلو هذه الماهيات عن الوجود والعدم معا وهو محال، والثاني أيضا باطل لأن أحدهما لا بعينه يمتنع دخوله في الوجود، لأن من المحال أن يحصل في الوجود شيء، مع أنه في نفسه لا يكون شيئا معينا، بل يكون هو في نفسه إما هذا وإما ذاك، وما يكون ممتنع الوجود في نفسه، يمتنع أن يكون وجوده معلولا لوجود غيره، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ثبت أن هذه الماهيات الممكنة تقتضي إما الوجود بعينه، وإما العدم بعينه، مع أن الطرف الثاني يكون غير ممتنع الحصول فثبت بهذا البرهان: أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية. ولما ثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية في الجملة فنقول: هذا الذي يكون الوجود أولى به من العدم، وجب أن يكون غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المؤثر والفاعل الموجد، والدليل عليه: أن عند فرض عدم المؤثر المنفصل، إما أن يحصل أولا يحصل. فإن حصل الرجحان فقد كفت ماهيته في حصول هذا الرجحان، وما حصل بالذات امتنع استناده إلى الغير، وإن لم يحصل الرجحان فحينئذ لا تكون ذاته مقتضية لحصول أولوية الوجود، فرضناه، كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن القول بحصول أولوية الوجود معقول، وثابت أن بتقدير حصول هذه الأولوية، فإنه يجب حصول الاستغناء عن العلة، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة.
The essences are either such that they necessitate the attainment of these two matters [existence and non-existence], or such that they necessitate the attainment of one of them without specification, or such that they necessitate the attainment of one of them with specification. The first is false, otherwise it would entail that these essences are devoid of both existence and non-existence together, which is impossible. The second is also false, because one of them without specification cannot enter into existence, for it is impossible for something to come into existence while it is in itself not a specific thing, but rather it is in itself either this or that. And what is impossible of existence in itself is impossible for its existence to be caused by the existence of another. Since these two divisions are false, it is established that these contingent essences necessitate either existence with specification or non-existence with specification, and the second alternative is not impossible of attainment. Thus it is established by this proof that it is necessary to acknowledge the occurrence of this priority (al-awlawiyya). And since it has been established by these four ways that it is necessary to acknowledge the occurrence of this priority in general, we say: That which existence is more appropriate for than non-existence must be independent in its existence from the effective cause and the originating agent. The proof for that is that when we assume the absence of the separate effective cause, either priority occurs or it does not. If priority occurs, then its essence suffices for the occurrence of this priority, and what occurs by essence is impossible to be attributed to another. If priority does not occur, then its essence does not necessitate the occurrence of the priority of existence, as we assumed. This is a contradiction (khalaf). Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement of the occurrence of priority is intelligible, and it is established that given the occurrence of this priority, it is necessary that there be independence from the cause. This is the complete discourse in establishing this doubt.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage contains a proof establishing that contingent essences require a priority (al-awlawiyya) for existence over non-existence, and that such a priority implies independence from an external cause. The argument begins by dividing essences into three categories: (1) those that do not necessitate either existence or non-existence, (2) those that necessitate one of them without specification, and (3) those that necessitate one of them with specification. The first two are shown to be impossible. If an essence did not necessitate either, it would be devoid of both, which is impossible. If it necessitated one without specification, then the unspecified one could never come into existence, because a non-specific thing cannot exist. Therefore, the third possibility holds: each contingent essence necessitates either existence or non-existence with specification. This necessity is termed priority (al-awlawiyya). The argument then moves to the consequence of priority: what has priority for existence must be independent of an external cause. The proof is by conditional: assume the absence of any separate cause; if priority still occurs, then the essence itself suffices, so it is not dependent; if priority does not occur, then the essence does not require priority, contradicting the assumption. The contradiction shows that priority necessitates self-sufficiency. The passage ends by stating that this discourse establishes the intelligibility of the notion of priority and its consequence of independence from a cause.

Key Terms
الماهيات
الوجود
العدم
الأولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority (الأولوية) refers to temporal precedence.
Correction: Priority here is a metaphysical inherent disposition in the essence, not a temporal priority.

Checkpoint
What is the third division of essences that is shown to be true?
A) Essences that necessitate neither existence nor non-existence.
B) Essences that necessitate one of them without specification.
C) Essences that necessitate one of them with specification.
D) Essences that are independent of any necessity.

Key Takeaway
Contingent essences necessitate one of the two alternatives with specification, establishing the concept of priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a contingent essence — your existence is not necessary in itself. This proof shows that you have an inherent priority towards existence or non-existence. Are you aware that your very being testifies to a Prior Determination? How does this realization affect your sense of gratitude to the One who brought you into existence?
?
If your existence were not specified — if you were just 'one of them without specification' — you could not be. Yet you are. What does this say about the precision and purpose behind your creation? Does your life reflect that you are not a random accident but a specifically intended being?
?
The proof concludes that priority (al-awlawiyya) must be acknowledged. In your daily life, do you live as if your existence is a given, or do you constantly remember that you are contingent and upheld by another? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge your dependence on the Necessary Being?

Priority Implies Independence from a Cause
Proves that priority for existence entails independence from a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a priority for existence implies that the being is independent of an external cause.

Core Idea
If existence is preferred by an essence itself, no cause is needed.

Having established that every contingent essence has a built-in priority for either existence or non-existence, al-Sanusi now asks: what follows from this? He argues that if an essence has priority for existence, then it must be sufficient by itself, without needing an external cause. He proves this by considering what happens if we remove all external causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Sanusi claims that what has priority for existence is independent of an external cause. Find this claim in the passage.
It is stated after the reference to the four ways.
2
Understand the conditional proof
Consider the scenario where the separate cause is absent. There are two possibilities: either priority occurs or it does not. Trace each possibility.
The proof uses a conditional (khalaf) argument.
3
Grasp the conclusion
If priority occurs without the cause, the essence suffices; if not, contradiction. Thus priority necessitates independence.
The conclusion is that independence from the cause is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهيات إما أن لا تكون مقتضية الحصول هذين الأمرين، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما لا بعينه، وإما أن تكون مقتضية لحصول أحدهما بعينه، والأول باطل، وإلا لزم خلو هذه الماهيات عن الوجود والعدم معا وهو محال، والثاني أيضا باطل لأن أحدهما لا بعينه يمتنع دخوله في الوجود، لأن من المحال أن يحصل في الوجود شيء، مع أنه في نفسه لا يكون شيئا معينا، بل يكون هو في نفسه إما هذا وإما ذاك، وما يكون ممتنع الوجود في نفسه، يمتنع أن يكون وجوده معلولا لوجود غيره، ولما بطل هذان القسمان ثبت أن هذه الماهيات الممكنة تقتضي إما الوجود بعينه، وإما العدم بعينه، مع أن الطرف الثاني يكون غير ممتنع الحصول فثبت بهذا البرهان: أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية. ولما ثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بحصول هذه الأولوية في الجملة فنقول: هذا الذي يكون الوجود أولى به من العدم، وجب أن يكون غنيا في وجوده عن السبب المؤثر والفاعل الموجد، والدليل عليه: أن عند فرض عدم المؤثر المنفصل، إما أن يحصل أولا يحصل. فإن حصل الرجحان فقد كفت ماهيته في حصول هذا الرجحان، وما حصل بالذات امتنع استناده إلى الغير، وإن لم يحصل الرجحان فحينئذ لا تكون ذاته مقتضية لحصول أولوية الوجود، فرضناه، كذلك. هذا خلف. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن القول بحصول أولوية الوجود معقول، وثابت أن بتقدير حصول هذه الأولوية، فإنه يجب حصول الاستغناء عن العلة، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه الشبهة.
The essences are either such that they necessitate the attainment of these two matters [existence and non-existence], or such that they necessitate the attainment of one of them without specification, or such that they necessitate the attainment of one of them with specification. The first is false, otherwise it would entail that these essences are devoid of both existence and non-existence together, which is impossible. Thus it is established by this proof that it is necessary to acknowledge the occurrence of this priority (al-awlawiyya). The proof for that is that when we assume the absence of the separate effective cause, either priority occurs or it does not. The Objection: Persistent Things and the Need for a Cause
Presents an objection about persistent contingent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a persistent contingent being cannot be judged as needing a cause without proving temporal origination.

Core Idea
Persistence might exempt a thing from needing a continuous cause.

This lesson presents an important objection to the standard kalam proof for God's existence. The objector argues that if celestial bodies are eternal and persistent, they cannot be judged as needing a cause, because the persistent thing in its persistence is not (or is) in need of a cause. The author clarifies that to answer this, one must demonstrate that persistence does require a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation of the objection. Note the logical structure: the objector grants contingency but denies the need for a cause due to persistence.
Pay attention to the conditional 'if they are supposed to be perpetual'.
2
Understand the author's response
The author explains that if you prove temporal origination, the argument reduces to the standard proof. If not, you need to demonstrate that persistence requires a cause.
This sets up the need for the four proofs in the next lesson.
3
Identify the key claim to be proven
The author states that the exposition of this question requires proving that the enduring thing in its endurance is in need of a cause.

وأما السؤال الثاني: على قولكم الممكن مفتقر إلى المؤثر فهو أن نقول هذه الأجرام العلوية والسفلية بتقدير أن يقوم البرهان على كونها ممكنة الوجود في أنفسها، قابلة للوجود والعدم، إلا أن بتقدير كونها دائمة أزلية، باقية، فإنه يمتنع الحكم عليها بالافتقار إلى المؤثر، لأنه ثبت أن الباقي في حال بقائه يفتقر إلى المؤثر والفاعل، فعلى هذا التقدير ما لم تقيموا الدلالة على كونها محدثة، امتنع الحكم عليها بالاحتياج إلى المؤثر، إلا أنكم لو أثبتم حدوثها، فحينئذ يسقط هذا الدليل بالكلية، ويصير هذا الدليل هو الدليل الذي يعول المتكلمون عليه في إثبات العلم بالصانع، فإنهم يقيمون الدليل على أن العالم محدث، ثم يقولون: وكل محدث فله محدث، فحينئذ يحصل لهم العلم بافتقار هذا العالم إلى الصانع، فيفتقر في تقرير هذا السؤال إلى بيان أن الباقي حال بقائه يفتقر إلى المؤثر. والذي يدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إنه لو افتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر لزم إيجاد الموجود، وهو محال. الثاني: إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لزم افتقار البناء حال بنائه إلى الباني، وأيضاً من خضب يده بالحناء فإنه يبقى اللون بعد زوال الحناء، ومن رمى الحجر، فحركة الحجر باقية بعد زوال مدافعة الرامي. الثالث: إنه يلزم افتقار العدم الباقي حال استمراره إلى المؤثر. الرابع: إن كل عقل سليم يشهد بأن الشيء إذا حصل على وصفه وحاله، فإنه يبقى على تلك الصفة والحالة إلى وقت طريان المبطل المزيل: وكذلك فإنا إذا خرجنا من الدار، كان ظن بقاء تلك الأشياء التي رأيناها أقوى من ظن زوالها، ولهذا السبب فإنا نقصد للعود إليها، ولا نقصد العود إلى الدار التي يجوز حدوثها، وإذا غبنا عن بلد فإنا نكتب الكتب إلى أصحاب تلك البلدة لأجل ما حصل في عقولنا، من أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان.
As for the second question regarding your statement 'the contingent is in need of the effecting cause', it is that we say: These celestial and terrestrial bodies, given that a proof is established that they are contingent in themselves, receptive to existence and non-existence, except that if they are supposed to be perpetual, eternal, and enduring, then it is impossible to judge them as being in need of the effecting cause, because it is established that the enduring thing in its state of endurance is in need of the effecting cause and the agent. According to this supposition, as long as you do not provide evidence for their temporal origination, it is impossible to judge them as being in need of the effecting cause. However, if you establish their temporal origination, then this argument collapses entirely, and this argument becomes the argument that the theologians rely on in establishing knowledge of the Maker. For they present evidence that the world is temporally originated, then they say: Every temporally originated thing has an originator. Then they obtain knowledge of the world's need for the Maker. Therefore, the exposition of this question requires a demonstration that the enduring thing in its state of endurance is in need of the effecting cause. What indicates that are several aspects: First, if the enduring thing in its state of endurance were in need of the effecting cause, it would entail bringing into existence what already exists, which is impossible. Second, if it were so, it would entail the building's need for the builder during its being built. Also, one who dyes his hand with henna, the colour remains after the henna is gone. And one who throws a stone, the motion of the stone remains after the thrower's push ceases. Third, it would entail the enduring non-existence in its state of continuation to be in need of the effecting cause. Fourth, every sound intellect bears witness that when a thing obtains its description and state, it remains in that description and state until the time of occurrence of an annulling remover. Similarly, when we leave the house, our assumption of the persistence of those things we saw is stronger than our assumption of their removal, and for this reason we intend to return to them, and we do not intend to return to a house whose origination is possible. And when we are absent from a country, we write letters to the people of that country because of what has occurred in our minds that the original ruling in every matter is its persistence on what it was.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection against the claim that contingent beings always need an effecting cause (al-mu'aththir). The objector argues: celestial and terrestrial bodies, even if proven contingent, might be eternal and persistent; if so, they cannot be judged to need a cause, because the persistent thing in its persistence is (according to the text) in need of a cause—but the objector is actually trying to show that if they are persistent, they do not need a cause, given a variant reading. The author then explains that if one establishes temporal origination (huduth), the argument reduces to the standard kalam proof for the existence of God. To answer the objection, one must prove that the persistent thing (al-baqi) in its state of persistence does need a cause. Four proofs are given: (1) If it needed a cause, it would mean bringing into existence what already exists, which is impossible. (2) Analogies: a building does not need a builder after completion; henna colour remains after the henna is gone; a stone's motion continues after the thrower stops. (3) Non-existence, if it persisted, would also need a cause, which is absurd. (4) Common sense: things remain in their state until a remover appears; we trust that things persist when we leave them, and we act on that assumption. This shows that the default is persistence, not need for a continuous cause.

Key Terms
الباقي
المؤثر
المحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that contingent things need a cause at all.
Correction: The objection only denies the need for a cause for persistent things if temporal origination is not proven.

Checkpoint
What must be proven to answer the objection according to the passage?
A) The world is contingent.
B) The world is temporally originated.
C) The persistent thing needs a cause.
D) The persistent thing does not need a cause.

Key Takeaway
The objection forces the theologian to either prove temporal origination or show that persistence requires a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector suggests that if something is eternal, it might not need a cause. Think of something in your life you assume will always be there—your health, your faith, your relationships. Are you living as if it is self-sustaining? What would change if you saw it as contingent and needing constant divine support? Write one specific step you will take today to acknowledge your total dependence on Allah for that thing.
?
The theologian must confront the question: does persistence itself require a cause? Reflect on the spiritual habits you maintain (prayer, charity, patience). Do you assume they will persist on their own once started, or do you actively renew them? What is one habit you have neglected because you thought it would continue automatically? Commit to renewing it daily, recognizing that its continuity depends on Allah.
?
This passage forces you to decide: either your state of need for God is tied to your coming into existence or to your very persistence. Which do you truly believe? If you believe you need God every moment, how does that change your next hour? Identify one moment today when you will consciously pause and say, 'I am entirely dependent on You right now.'

Four Proofs Against the Need for a Persistent Cause
Presents four proofs that persistence does not require a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical proofs that show a persistent thing does not need a cause during its persistence.

Core Idea
Persistence does not need a cause; only origination does.

After establishing that the objection requires a demonstration that persistence needs a cause, the author presents four proofs that actually show the opposite: that persistence does not need a continuous cause. These proofs use logical impossibility, analogies, and common sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the four proofs
Read each proof in Arabic and translation. Note that the first proof is logical: if persistence needed a cause, it would entail bringing into existence what already exists.
The second proof uses analogies: building, henna, stone.
2
Analyze the analogies
Consider the building analogy: the building does not need the builder after completion. Similarly, henna colour remains after henna is gone, and the stone continues moving after being thrown.
These show that the cause is not needed for persistence.
3
Reflect on the fourth proof
The fourth proof uses common sense: things remain as they are until a remover arises. We act on this assumption in daily life.
This is an argument from the natural constitution (fitra).
