Four Objections to the Need for a Cause in Persistence
The author enumerates four objections against the claim that persisting things need a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised against the claim that a persisting thing requires a cause for its continued existence.

Core Idea
Persistence does not require a cause; claiming otherwise leads to absurdities.

This passage presents four objections that challenge the assumption that a persisting thing depends on a cause for its continued persistence. These objections are crucial for understanding debates about causality and the nature of originated things, and are often discussed in the context of proving the existence of a Maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the four objections.
2
Understand the context
Note that the author is presenting objections that will be answered later; these are not his own views but difficulties posed to the claim that persistence needs a cause.
3
Analyze each objection
Identify and analyze each of the four objections starting from 'الأول' to 'الرابع'. Understand the reasoning behind each: first entails bringing into existence what already exists, second leads to absurd consequences, third involves persistent non-existence, fourth appeals to common sense.
The first objection is the classic argument of taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil.
4
Reflect on common sense
Consider the fourth objection based on human behavior and presumption of persistence. How does it align with everyday experience?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقائه يمتنع باقية ، فإنه يمتنع الحكم عليها بالافتقار إلى المؤثر ، لأنه ثبت أن الباقي في حال افتقاره إلى المؤثر والفاعل ، فعلى هذا التقدير ما لم تقيموا الدلالة على كونها محدثة ، امتنع الحكم عليها بالاحتياج إلى المؤثر، إلا أنكم لو أثبتم حدوثها ، فحينئذ يسقط هذا الدليل بالكلية، ويصير هذا الدليل هو الدليل الذي يعول المتكلمون عليه في إثبات العلم بالصانع ، فإنهم يقيمون الدليل على أن العالم محدث ، ثم يقولون : وكل محدث فله محدث ، فحينئذ يحصل لهم العلم بافتقار هذا العالم إلى الصانع ، فيفتقر في تقرير هذا السؤال إلى بيان أن الباقي حال بقائه يفتقر إلى المؤثر. والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه لو افتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر لزم إيجاد الموجود ، وهو محال. الثاني : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لزم افتقار البناء حال بنائه إلى الباني ، وأيضاً من خضب يده بالحناء فإنه يبقى اللون بعد زوال الحناء ، ومن رمى الحجر ، فحركة الحجر باقية بعد زوال مدافعة الرامي. الثالث : إنه يلزم افتقار العدم الباقي حال استمراره إلى المؤثر. الرابع : إن كل عقل سليم يشهد بأن الشيء إذا حصل على وصفه وحاله ، فإنه يبقى على تلك الصفة والحالة إلى وقت طريان المبطل المزيل : وكذلك فإنا إذا خرجنا من الدار ، كان ظن بقاء تلك الأشياء التي رأيناها أقوى من ظن زوالها ، ولهذا السبب فإنا نقصد للعود إليها ، ولا نقصد العود إلى الدار التي يجوز حدوثها ، وإذا غبنا عن بلد فإنا نكتب الكتب إلى أصحاب تلك البلدة لأجل ما حصل في عقولنا، من أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان. فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذين السؤالين .
Its persistence prevents it from being persistent. For it is impossible to judge that it is in need of the efficient cause, because it has been established that the persistent thing in its state of need is in need of the efficient cause and agent. So on this assumption, as long as you do not provide evidence that they are originated, it is impossible to judge that they are in need of the efficient cause. Rather, if you establish their origination, then this proof falls entirely, and this proof becomes the proof that the theologians rely on in establishing knowledge of the Maker. For they provide evidence that the world is originated, then they say: Every originated thing has an originator. Then they obtain knowledge of this world's need for the Maker. So the establishment of this question requires a demonstration that the persistent thing in its state of persistence is in need of the efficient cause. That which indicates it are aspects: First: If the persistent thing in its state of persistence were in need of the efficient cause, it would entail bringing into existence what already exists, which is impossible. Second: If the matter were so, it would entail the building's need for the builder in its state of being built. Also, one who dyes his hand with henna – the color remains after the henna disappears. And one who throws a stone – the stone's motion remains after the thrower's thrust ceases. Third: It would entail the persistent non-existence's need for the efficient cause in its state of continuance. Fourth: Every sound intellect testifies that when a thing attains its description and state, it remains in that description and state until the time of the occurrence of the nullifier that removes it. Likewise, when we leave a house, the presumption of the persistence of those things we saw is stronger than the presumption of their disappearance. For this reason, we intend to return to them, and we do not intend to return to a house that may have originated. And when we are absent from a land, we write books to the people of that land because of what has settled in our minds that the original state in every matter is its persistence as it was. This completes the discussion of the two questions.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents four objections against the claim that a persisting thing requires a cause for its continued existence. The author begins by noting that if a thing's persistence does not require a cause, then without proving its origination (ḥudūth), one cannot claim it needs a cause. If origination is proven, the usual proof of the theologians (that the world is originated and every originated has an originator) suffices. However, the specific question here demands showing that the persisting thing needs a cause in its state of persistence. The author then lists four objections that undermine this demand: (1) It would entail bringing into existence what already exists ( taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil ), which is absurd. (2) It would imply absurd consequences like a building needing a builder while being built, color remaining after henna removal, or stone's motion needing continuous thrust. (3) It would entail that persistent non-existence (al-ʿadam al-bāqī) needs a cause, which is meaningless. (4) Common sense and human behavior show that things persist on their own until something removes them; we assume persistence until proven otherwise. The passage concludes by marking the end of the two initial questions.

مؤثر
إيجاد الموجود
افتقار
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is arguing that persistence does need a cause, but he is presenting these objections as difficulties that must be answered later.
Correction: The author is not endorsing these objections; he is presenting them as challenges that need to be refuted.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what would follow if a persisting thing needed a cause in its state of persistence?
A) It would entail the thing's non-existence.
B) It would entail bringing into existence what already exists, which is impossible.
C) It would entail the thing's dependence on a continuous cause, which is possible.
D) It would entail the thing's eternal existence.

Key Takeaway
The four objections challenge the necessity of a cause for persistence, highlighting absurdities and aligning with common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume your faith will persist on its own without continuous effort and reliance on Allah? What nullifiers might be removing it without you noticing?
?
The fourth objection says we presume things stay as they are until something changes it. What cherished belief or habit have you assumed will last forever? Are you prepared for when Allah removes it?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of your existence is recreated by Allah, would you live differently today? How would your gratitude and dependence show?

Answer to the Second Objection: Binary Nature of Causality
The author refutes the claim that a cause has priority of influence before its condition is met.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that a cause has priority of influence before its condition is met, and you will grasp that causality is conditional on all conditions being present.

Core Idea
Causal influence is all-or-nothing: either impossible or necessary, never in between.

In this passage, the author addresses the second objection, which argues that a cause can have priority of influence before its condition is fully satisfied. The author firmly rejects this by establishing a binary view of causality: if any condition is missing, influence is impossible; if all conditions are present, influence is necessary. This clarifies that there is no gradual or priority influence in causal relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if a condition is violated' and 'if all conditions are obtained'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author presents two clear cases: when any condition is missing (influence impossible) and when all conditions are present (influence necessary). Mark these in the text.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does the author reject the idea of priority? How does this binary view fit into the broader discussion of causality? Consider that causality is not a matter of degree.
This connects to the learning objective.

والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( العلة إذا كان تأثيرها في معلولها موقوفا على شرط فقبل حصول ذلك الشرط يكون التأثير بها أولى ، مع أنه لم ينته إلى حد الوجوب ) فنقول : لا نسلم أن التأثير بها أولى ، فإن العلة مهما اختل قيد من القيود المعتبرة في كونها علة ، امتنع كونها مؤثرة ، ومهما حصل كل القيود المعتبرة في العلية ، وجب كونها مؤثرة .
And the answer to the second objection: which is their saying: 'The cause, if its influence on its effect is conditional on a condition, then before the occurrence of that condition, the influence by it has priority, even though it does not reach the limit of necessity.' We say: We do not accept that the influence has priority. For the cause, whenever a condition from the conditions considered for being a cause is violated, its being effectual is impossible. And whenever all the conditions considered for causality are obtained, its being effectual is necessary.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection. The objectors claim that a cause, when its influence is conditional on a condition, has a priority of influence before the condition is met, even though it does not reach necessity. The author refutes this by stating that a cause is only effectual when all conditions are present. If any condition is missing, it is impossible for it to be effectual. If all conditions are present, its being effectual is necessary. There is no intermediate state of priority before the condition is met.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
شرط
تأثير
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause can have priority of influence before its condition is met.
Correction: Influence is either impossible (if any condition is missing) or necessary (if all conditions are present); there is no intermediate state of priority.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, when is a cause effectual?
A) Before the condition is met.
B) Only when all conditions are present.
C) It is always effectual.
D) It is effectual only when a condition is missing.

Key Takeaway
A cause's influence is binary: impossible when any condition is missing, necessary when all conditions are present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the things you rely on daily—your work, your health, your relationships—do you treat them as having a degree of influence, or do you recognize that they are effectual only when all conditions are present and otherwise impossible? How would acknowledging this binary logic change your reliance on them and increase your reliance on Allah as the ultimate Cause?
?
The objectors claimed that a cause has priority before its condition is met. Have you ever assumed that your efforts or intentions have some effect even before they are fully realized? How can you apply the lesson's binary view to purify your actions so that you neither overestimate nor underestimate the role of means, and instead anchor your trust in Allah alone?

The First Objection: Accidents and Persistence
Author defends preponderance against accident persistence objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the necessity of a preponderating factor against the objection that some accidents cannot persist.

Core Idea
The persistence of accidents does not undermine the necessity of preponderance.

The author has established that if one side is preponderant, the other is preponderated and impossible. Now he addresses the first objection: what if some accidents cannot persist? This lesson explains his two-pronged response, showing that the objection does not threaten the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: 'Among accidents there are those whose persistence is impossible.' Why would this be a problem for the argument?
The objection might imply that the preponderant state could disappear.
3
Follow the two answers
The author gives two possible responses. First, some say all accidents may persist. Second, if some cannot, the impossibility is only of an accidental quality, not the essence. Understand each.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how both responses preserve the necessity of preponderance. The objection is neutralized.
The core argument remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبت هذا . فنقول : بتقدير أن يكون أحد الطرفين راجحا كان الطرف الثاني مرجوحا ، ولما كان حصول الرجحان حال الاستواء ممتنعا ، كان ( حصوله ) حال حصول المرجوحية أولى بالامتناع ، وإذا كان الطرف المرجوح حال كونه مرجوحاً ممتنع الحصول، كانه الطرف الراجح واجب الحصول ، ضرورة أنه لا خروج عن طرف النقيض ، ولما أقمنا البرهان القاطع على فساد الاحتمال الذي ذكرتموه ، فلنذكر الجواب على الوجوه التي عولوا عليها :

الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : ( إن من الأعراض ما يكون « ممتنع البقاء ) .

فنقول : من الناس من قال كل عرض فإنه يجوز بقاؤه ، قال : لان تلك الماهية قابلة للوجود ، وإنها لو لم تكن قابلة للوجود لما وجدت تلك القابلية من لوازم الماهية ، ولازم الماهية يكون واجب الدوام بدوام الماهية ، فتلك القابلية باقية أبدا ، فكان ذلك الشيء ممكن البقاء والدوام، وعلى هذا المذهب فالإشكال زائل وأما الذين سلموا : أن من الأعراض ما يمتنع بقاؤه .
·
فنقول : جواب هذه الشبهة عندهم : أن كونه باقيا كيفية حادثة ، فالممتنع هو حصول تلك الكيفية ، وهي غير قابلة للوجود أصلا

والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( العلة إذا كان تأثيرها في معلولها موقوفا على شرط فقبل حصول ذلك الشرط يكون التأثير بها أولى ، مع أنه لم ينته إلى حد الوجوب »

فنقول : لا نسلم أن التأثير بها أولى ، فإن العلة مهما اختل قيد من القيود المعتبرة في كونها علة ، امتنع كونها مؤثرة ، ومهما حصل كل القيود المعتبرة في العلية ، وجب كونها مؤثرة .

والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : : اتفق الحكماء على أن الممكن قد يكون أكثريا ، وقد يكون أقليا ، فنقول : المراد من الأكثري هو الذي يكون سبب وجوده في أكثر الأحوال موجودا ، وإذا فسرنا الأكثرية بهذا الوجه سقط كلامكم

والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إن قولكم : « الماهية تقتضي بعينه مع أن طريان النقيض على ذلك الطرف ممكن » .
أحد الطرفين

فنقول : الماهية تقتضي أن تكون إما موجودة ، وإما ( أن تكون ( معدومة ، قوله : « هذا المعنى أمر مبهم ، والمبهم لا وجود له في الأعيان ، وما لا وجود له في الأعيان امتنع جعله معلولا ، لما لا يكون موجودا في الأعيان » ، قلنا : مقتضى الماهية عدم الخلو عن الوجود والعدم معا ، وعدم الخلو عنها أمر معين ، فهذا تمام الكلام في السؤال الأول .
This has been established. So we say: Supposing that one of the two sides is preponderant, then the second side is preponderated. And since the occurrence of preponderance in a state of equality is impossible, its occurrence in a state of being preponderated is more deserving of impossibility. And if the preponderated side, while being preponderated, is impossible of occurrence, then the preponderant side is necessary of occurrence, necessarily because there is no exit from the two sides of contradiction. And since we have established the decisive proof of the corruption of the likelihood that you mentioned, let us mention the answer to the aspects upon which they relied:

The answer to the first sophism: which is their statement: 'Among accidents there are those whose persistence is impossible.'

So we say: Some people say that every accident may persist. He said: Because that essence is receptive to existence, and if it were not receptive to existence, that receptivity would not exist from the necessities of the essence, and the necessary of the essence is perpetually necessary with the perpetuity of the essence, so that receptivity remains forever, so that thing is possible of persistence and perpetuity. On this view, the problem is removed. As for those who concede that among accidents there are those whose persistence is impossible, we say: The answer to this sophism according to them is that its being persistent is an accidental quality, so the impossible is the occurrence of that quality, and it is not receptive to existence at all.

And the answer to the second sophism: which is their statement: 'If the cause's effect in its effect is dependent on a condition, then before the occurrence of that condition, the effect by it is more appropriate, even though it does not reach the limit of obligation.'

So we say: We do not concede that the effect by it is more appropriate. For the cause, whenever a condition from among the conditions considered for its being a cause is missing, its being effective is impossible; and whenever all the considered conditions for causality are present, its being effective is necessary.

And the answer to the third sophism: which is their statement: 'The philosophers have agreed that the possible may be majority-occurring and may be minority-occurring.' We say: The meaning of majority-occurring is that whose cause of existence is present in most circumstances. If we interpret majority in this manner, your statement falls.

And the answer to the fourth sophism: Your statement: 'The essence in itself necessitates one of the two sides, even though the occurrence of its opposite on that side is possible.'

We say: The essence necessitates that it be either existent or non-existent. Your statement: 'This meaning is vague, and the vague has no existence in external entities, and what has no existence in external entities is impossible to be made an effect, since it is not existent in external entities.' We say: The requisite of the essence is non-vacancy from existence and non-existence together, and non-vacancy from them is a definite matter. So this is the complete discussion regarding the first question.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage provides answers to four objections (shubah) raised against the argument for the necessity of a preponderating factor. The author begins by restating the established point: if one side is preponderant, the other is preponderated, and something cannot become preponderant from a state of equality. He then systematically addresses each objection. The first objection claims that some accidents cannot persist, which would undermine the reasoning about preponderance. The author presents two responses: either all accidents can persist (based on the receptivity of the essence) or, if some cannot, their persistence is an accidental quality that is simply impossible. The second objection concerns conditional causality: they argue that before the condition is met, the cause is 'more appropriate' but not obligatory. The author denies this, stating that causality requires all conditions; if any condition is missing, the cause is not effective at all. The third objection cites the concept of 'majority' and 'minority' occurrences. The author clarifies that 'majority' simply means the cause is present in most cases, which does not affect the argument. The fourth objection claims that the essence's requirement of one side is vague and thus cannot be a cause. The author responds that the essence requires non-vacancy between existence and non-existence, which is a definite matter. The passage concludes the first question.

Key Terms
مرجوح (preponderated)
ماهية (essence)
عرض (accident)
ممكن (possible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author must provide a single answer to the objection, but he offers two valid responses.
Correction: Both responses are acceptable; they show that regardless of one's view on accident persistence, the objection is neutralized.

Checkpoint
What is the author's second response to the objection that some accidents cannot persist?
A) He denies that any accidents exist.
B) He says that the impossibility is only of the accidental quality of persistence.
C) He accepts the objection and changes his argument.
D) He says that accidents are not relevant to the discussion.

Key Takeaway
The objection about accident persistence does not refute the necessity of a preponderating factor because either all accidents can persist, or the impossibility is limited to an accidental quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a success to your own effort, do you genuinely believe that the alternative—failure—was impossible given Allah's will? How does that change your gratitude?
?
Think of a sin you repeatedly commit and consider it 'impossible to avoid.' Are you confusing the impossibility with an accidental quality? What step will you take today to rely on Allah for change?
?
The author argues that if one side is preponderant, the other is impossible. In your daily choices, when you choose comfort over obedience, do you treat the obedient option as 'impossible'? How can you shift your preponderance toward what pleases Allah?

The Second Objection: Conditional Causality
Author rejects intermediate causality in conditional causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author denies that a cause can be 'more appropriate' before its condition, asserting that causality requires all conditions.

Core Idea
A cause is only effective when all conditions are present; nothing is 'more appropriate'.

The second objection argues that before a condition is met, the cause is still 'more appropriate' for its effect. The author rejects this, stating that causality depends entirely on the presence of all conditions. This lesson clarifies his decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the objection's phrasing: the cause is 'more appropriate' even before the condition.
2
Identify the author's denial
The author says 'We do not concede that the effect by it is more appropriate.' This is a strong rejection.
3
Understand the logic
The author explains that if any condition is missing, the cause is entirely ineffective. Only when all conditions are present does the cause become necessary.
There is no intermediate state of 'more appropriate'.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this response defends the necessity of preponderance: if the cause is not effective until all conditions are present, the preponderating factor must be fully effective when conditions are met.

?

?

It eliminates the middle ground. Either you are required to do it or you are not. Are you using false humility or self-comparison to avoid what is actually obligatory for you?
?

The lesson states that if a condition is missing, the cause is ineffective—no middle state. Does this challenge your tendency to wait for perfect circumstances before acting? What is one area of your life where you need to stop saying 'more appropriate' and instead either commit fully or stop pretending?

The Third Objection: Majority and Minority Occurrence
Author redefines 'majority occurrence' to defuse objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author neutralizes the objection that possible things can be majority- or minority-occurring, by redefining 'majority' in terms of the presence of its cause.

Core Idea
Majority occurrence simply means the cause is usually present.

The third objection appeals to the fact that some possible things happen more often than others. The author counters by reinterpreting 'majority' — it refers to the frequency of the cause, not a property of the possible itself. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the short passage. The objection mentions that the possible can be majority- or minority-occurring.
2
Identify the author's redefinition
The author defines 'majority' as the cause of existence being present in most circumstances. This shifts the focus from the possible itself to its cause.
3
See how this answers the objection
If 'majority' is about the cause, then the objection does not challenge the necessity of preponderance—it simply describes frequency of causes.
The objection falls because it misinterprets 'majority'.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Understand why this reinterpretation is effective in defending the original argument.

فنقول : من الناس من قال كل عرض فإنه يجوز بقاؤه ، قال : لان تلك الماهية قابلة للوجود ، وإنها لو لم تكن قابلة للوجود لما وجدت تلك القابلية من لوازم الماهية ، ولازم الماهية يكون واجب الدوام بدوام الماهية ، فتلك القابلية باقية أبدا ، فكان ذلك الشيء ممكن البقاء والدوام، وعلى هذا المذهب فالإشكال زائل وأما الذين سلموا : أن من الأعراض ما يمتنع بقاؤه .
·
فنقول : جواب هذه الشبهة عندهم : أن كونه باقيا كيفية حادثة ، فالممتنع هو حصول تلك الكيفية ، وهي غير قابلة للوجود أصلا

والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( العلة إذا كان تأثيرها في معلولها موقوفا على شرط فقبل حصول ذلك الشرط يكون التأثير بها أولى ، مع أنه لم ينته إلى حد الوجوب »

فنقول : لا نسلم أن التأثير بها أولى ، فإن العلة مهما اختل قيد من القيود المعتبرة في كونها علة ، امتنع كونها مؤثرة ، ومهما حصل كل القيود المعتبرة في العلية ، وجب كونها مؤثرة .

والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : : اتفق الحكماء على أن الممكن قد يكون أكثريا ، وقد يكون أقليا ، فنقول : المراد من الأكثري هو الذي يكون سبب وجوده في أكثر الأحوال موجودا ، وإذا فسرنا الأكثرية بهذا الوجه سقط كلامكم

والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إن قولكم : « الماهية تقتضي بعينه مع أن طريان النقيض على ذلك الطرف ممكن » .
أحد الطرفين

فنقول : الماهية تقتضي أن تكون إما موجودة ، وإما ( أن تكون ( معدومة ، قوله : « هذا المعنى أمر مبهم ، والمبهم لا وجود له في الأعيان ، وما لا وجود له في الأعيان امتنع جعله معلولا ، لما لا يكون موجودا في الأعيان » ، قلنا : مقتضى الماهية عدم الخلو عن الوجود والعدم معا ، وعدم الخلو عنها أمر معين ، فهذا تمام الكلام في السؤال الأول .
This has been established. So we say: Supposing that one of the two sides is preponderant, then the second side is preponderated. And since the occurrence of preponderance in a state of equality is impossible, its occurrence in a state of being preponderated is more deserving of impossibility. And if the preponderated side, while being preponderated, is impossible of occurrence, then the preponderant side is necessary of occurrence, necessarily because there is no exit from the two sides of contradiction. And since we have established the decisive proof of the corruption of the likelihood that you mentioned, let us mention the answer to the aspects upon which they relied:

The answer to the first sophism: which is their statement: 'Among accidents there are those whose persistence is impossible.'

So we say: Some people say that every accident may persist. He said: Because that essence is receptive to existence, and if it were not receptive to existence, that receptivity would not exist from the necessities of the essence, and the necessary of the essence is perpetually necessary with the perpetuity of the essence, so that receptivity remains forever, so that thing is possible of persistence and perpetuity. On this view, the problem is removed. As for those who concede that among accidents there are those whose persistence is impossible, we say: The answer to this sophism according to them is that its being persistent is an accidental quality, so the impossible is the occurrence of that quality, and it is not receptive to existence at all.

And the answer to the second sophism: which is their statement: 'If the cause's effect in its effect is dependent on a condition, then before the occurrence of that condition, the effect by it is more appropriate, even though it does not reach the limit of obligation.'

So we say: We do not concede that the effect by it is more appropriate. For the cause, whenever a condition from among the conditions considered for its being a cause is missing, its being effective is impossible; and whenever all the considered conditions for causality are present, its being effective is necessary.

And the answer to the third sophism: which is their statement: 'The philosophers have agreed that the possible may be majority-occurring and may be minority-occurring.' We say: The meaning of majority-occurring is that whose cause of existence is present in most circumstances. If we interpret majority in this manner, your statement falls.

And the answer to the fourth sophism: Your statement: 'The essence in itself necessitates one of the two sides, even though the occurrence of its opposite on that side is possible.'

We say: The essence necessitates that it be either existent or non-existent. Your statement: 'This meaning is vague, and the vague has no existence in external entities, and what has no existence in external entities is impossible to be made an effect, since it is not existent in external entities.' We say: The requisite of the essence is non-vacancy from existence and non-existence together, and non-vacancy from them is a definite matter. So this is the complete discussion regarding the first question.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage provides answers to four objections (shubah) raised against the argument for the necessity of a preponderating factor. The author begins by restating the established point: if one side is preponderant, the other is preponderated, and something cannot become preponderant from a state of equality. He then systematically addresses each objection. The first objection claims that some accidents cannot persist, which would undermine the reasoning about preponderance. The author presents two responses: either all accidents can persist (based on the receptivity of the essence) or, if some cannot, their persistence is an accidental quality that is simply impossible. The second objection concerns conditional causality: they argue that before the condition is met, the cause is 'more appropriate' but not obligatory. The author denies this, stating that causality requires all conditions; if any condition is missing, the cause is not effective at all. The third objection cites the concept of 'majority' and 'minority' occurrences. The author clarifies that 'majority' simply means the cause is present in most cases, which does not affect the argument. The fourth objection claims that the essence's requirement of one side is vague and thus cannot be a cause. The author responds that the essence requires non-vacancy between existence and non-existence, which is a definite matter. The passage concludes the first question.

Key Terms
ممكن (possible)
أكثري (majority-occurring)
أقلي (minority-occurring)
سبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'majority-occurring' means the possible itself has a tendency to exist.
Correction: The author clarifies that it refers to the frequency of the cause, not an inherent property of the possible.

Checkpoint
How does the author define 'majority-occurring' possible?
A) That which exists by itself most of the time.
B) That whose cause of existence is present in most circumstances.
C) That which is more likely than not to exist.
D) That which is agreed upon by philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'majority' occurrence is explained by the frequency of the cause, not by any intrinsic property of the possible, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the 'frequent' blessings in your life—like health, safety, provision—do you ever take them as mere routine, forgetting that they exist only because their Cause continuously brings them about? How can you train yourself today to see the hand of Allah behind every 'usual' occurrence?
?
Consider something rare or miraculous you have heard or experienced. Does this lesson make you more certain that even the rarest events have a cause (Allah's will), and are not just random? How does that change your reliance on Him?

The Fourth Objection: The Essence's Requirement
Author clarifies essence's requirement as non-vacancy, answering vagueness objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the claim that the essence's requirement of one side is vague and cannot be a cause.

Core Idea
The essence's requirement of non-vacancy between existence and non-existence is definite and sufficient.

The fourth objection argues that the essence's supposed requirement of one side is too vague to serve as a cause. The author responds that the essence requires non-vacancy — which is a definite property. This lesson explains the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the objection and answer. The objection says the essence's requirement is vague.
2
Identify the author's response
The author says the essence requires non-vacancy from existence and non-existence together, which is a definite matter.
Non-vacancy means it cannot be both or neither.
3
Understand the refutation
The objector claimed that the vague cannot be an effect. The author shows that non-vacancy is not vague but definite.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this response closes the discussion on the first question.
The passage ends with 'so this is the complete discussion regarding the first question.'

Possibility Alone Suffices for Need of an Effector
The author argues that possibility alone grounds the need for an effector.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Māturīdī theologian argues that mere possibility, rather than origination, is the ground for a thing's need for an effectuating cause.

Core Idea
Possibility, not newness, is what makes a thing dependent on a cause.

In this passage, the author directly challenges a rival view that only originated (new) things require an effector. He insists that the defining feature of a possible being—its equal potential for existence and non-existence—is itself sufficient to necessitate a preponderating factor. This lesson unpacks his first argument and begins the second.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the author's target: the condition of newness (ḥudūth).
The passage ends abruptly; we have the first proof fully and the start of the second.
2
Examine the first proof
The author defines the possible as having equal relation to existence and non-existence. Reason dictates that such equality requires a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to tip the scale. This shows possibility alone establishes the need for an effector.
This is a direct appeal to rational necessity (qadā al-ʿaql).
3
Consider why newness cannot be the cause
The second proof argues that newness is a quality of existence, which itself depends on the effector's action and the effect's need. Thus newness cannot be the cause, part, or condition of the need—it is posterior.
The argument is cut off, but the logical priority is clear: need precedes newness.
4
Reflect on the implications
If only originated things needed a cause, then eternal possibles (like the world according to some) would be uncaused. The author's view ensures all possibles, regardless of temporal origin, depend on Allah.
This relates to debates about the world's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : « افتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر مشروط بكون ذلك الممكن محدثا ، فنقول : هذا الشرط غير معتبر ، بل ندعي إن مجرد ذلك الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، ويدل عليه وجوه :

الأول: إنا بينا أن الممكن هو الذي تكون نسبة الوجود والعدم إليه على السوية ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، قضى العقل بأنه لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، فعلمنا أن هذا القدر كاف في تحقق الحاجة إلى المؤثر ، فيبقى قيد الحاجة ، محذوفا عن درجة الاعتبار

الثاني : إن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة للحاجة ، ولا جزءاً من العلة ولا شرطا لها ، والدليل عليه أن الحدوث كيفية في الوجود ، فهي متوقفة على الوجود ، المتوقف على تأثير المؤثر فيه ، المتوقف على احتياج الأثر إلى
And as for the second question: which is his statement: 'The dependence of the possible upon the effector is conditioned upon that possible being originated.' So we say: This condition is not considered; rather we claim that mere possibility (al-imkan) is the cause of the need for the effector. Evidence indicates this in several ways:

First: We have shown that the possible is that whose relation to existence and non-existence is equal. And everything that is such, reason judges that one of its two sides does not preponderate except by a preponderator. So we know that this much is sufficient for the realization of the need for an effector, so the condition of newness (al-huduth) is removed from consideration.

Second: Newness (al-huduth) cannot be the cause of the need, nor a part of the cause, nor a condition for it. The evidence for that is that newness is a quality in existence, so it depends on existence, which depends on the effect of the effector, which depends on the need of the effect for...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection: the rival claim that dependence on an effector is conditioned upon the possible being originated (hadith). The author flatly rejects this condition, asserting that mere possibility (imkan) is sufficient to ground the need for a preponderating effector. He provides two proofs. The first proof: the possible is defined as having an equal relation to existence and non-existence; reason necessarily concludes that such an equal relation can only tip to one side by a preponderator. This demonstrates that possibility alone is enough to establish the need, making the condition of newness irrelevant. The second proof: newness itself cannot be the cause of need because newness is a quality that attaches to existence itself. Existence is caused by the effector, and the need of the effect for the effector is prior to newness. Thus newness depends on the need, not the other way around. The argument is cut off at the end, but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
حدوث (huduth)
مؤثر (mu'assir)
ترجيح (tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only originated (new) things require a cause; a possible being that has always existed would not need an effector.
Correction: The author argues that mere possibility, which is a characteristic of the thing itself, is sufficient to necessitate a preponderating cause, regardless of whether it is originated or not.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the cause of the possible's need for an effector?
A) Its newness (huduth).
B) Its essence not being necessary.
C) Mere possibility (imkan).
D) Its being an accident.

Key Takeaway
Mere possibility is sufficient to establish a thing's need for an effectuating cause; newness is not a necessary condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence—your very being, not just the changes in your life—do you truly believe that at every moment you are a possible being whose existence is being preponderated by Allah? Or do you sometimes feel self-sufficient, as if you exist on your own? How would your day change if you reminded yourself of this dependency each morning?
?
The author insists that newness is not required for need; even if you feel spiritually 'stagnant' or unchanging, do you still sense your absolute need for the Effector? What would it mean for your attitude in prayer and supplication to realize that you depend on Him not only for your next breath but for the very fact that you exist right now?
?
If you are the one who tips the balance in your own choices—choosing good or evil—does that give you a sense of pride or responsibility? But the argument says that every possible being, including your free will, needs a preponderator. How does this reshape your understanding of tawakkul (reliance on Allah) while still acting as a moral agent?

Answer to the Third Doubt: Meaning of 'Majority'
The author clarifies the meaning of 'majority' in the philosophers' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' doubt about the 'majority' possible by redefining the term.

Core Idea
"Majority" possible still needs a cause; it only describes frequency.

This passage tackles a key objection to the necessity of causality. Philosophers claim some possibles occur without a cause because they are 'majority'. The author shows that 'majority' merely means the cause is usually present. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the doubt, then the answer beginning with 'fa naqulu'.
The doubt is attributed to philosophers who claim consensus on 'akthari' and 'aqalli' possibles.
2
Understand the definition of 'akthari'
The author defines 'akthari' as 'that whose cause of existence is present in most circumstances'. This is the key move: redefining the term to include a cause.
The definition directly counters the implication that 'akthari' possibles are uncaused.
3
See why the objection collapses
Once majority is defined with a cause, the philosophers' doubt loses its force. Even 'majority' possibles require a cause; the term only indicates frequency of the cause's presence.
This shows that the original doubt was based on a misunderstanding of the term 'akthari'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : اتفق الحكماء على أن الممكن قد يكون أكثريا ، وقد يكون أقليا ، فنقول : المراد من الأكثري هو الذي يكون سبب وجوده في أكثر الأحوال موجودا ، وإذا فسرنا الأكثرية بهذا الوجه سقط كلامكم
And the answer to the third doubt: namely their saying: 'The philosophers agree that the possible may be majority and may be minority.' So we say: The intended meaning of 'majority' is that whose cause of existence is present in most circumstances. And if we interpret majority in this way, your argument falls.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The doubt states that philosophers agree the possible (al-mumkin) can be 'majority' (akthari) or 'minority' (aqalli), implying that some possibles occur without a cause. The author refutes this by defining 'majority' as that whose cause is usually present. Thus, even the 'majority' possible requires a cause; the term only describes frequency, not independence. Once this meaning is accepted, the objection collapses.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'akthari' means the possible exists without a cause.
Correction: The author clarifies it only describes frequency of the cause's presence.

B) That whose cause is present in most circumstances
C) That which is more likely to exist than not
D) That which requires no preponderator

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' appeal to 'majority' possibles does not undermine causality; it only describes the frequency of the cause's presence.

The Essence's Determinate Requirement
The author explains that the essence requires existence or nonexistence, and this requirement is determinate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the fourth doubt by showing that the essence's requirement of not being free of existence and nonexistence is a determinate, not vague, property.

Core Idea
The essence's requirement is not vague; it is the determinate state of not being free of both existence and nonexistence.

The fourth doubt argues that the essence requires one specific side (existence or nonexistence) but the opposite is possible, making it inconsistent. The author responds by clarifying that the essence only requires that it be either existent or nonexistent—it does not specify which. Moreover, the requirement itself is a determinate property: the inability to be free of both. This passage defends the logical basis of the essence's need for an effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and the answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the doubt and then gives his answer.
The doubt claims the essence requires one specific side, but the opposite is possible.
2
Understand the author's clarification
The author explains that the essence does not require a specific side; rather, it requires being either existent or nonexistent. This is a general requirement.
3
Grasp the final point
The objector then says this requirement is vague and cannot be a real property. The author counters that the requirement is not vague: it is the determinate state of not being free of both existence and nonexistence. This ends the objection.
This determinate property can serve as a basis for needing an effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة : إن قولكم : « الماهية تقتضي بعينه مع أن طريان النقيض على ذلك الطرف ممكن » . أحد الطرفين فنقول : الماهية تقتضي أن تكون إما موجودة ، وإما أن تكون معدومة ، قوله : « هذا المعنى أمر مبهم ، والمبهم لا وجود له في الأعيان ، وما لا وجود له في الأعيان امتنع جعله معلولا ، لما لا يكون موجودا في الأعيان » ، قلنا : مقتضى الماهية عدم الخلو عن الوجود والعدم معا ، وعدم الخلو عنها أمر معين ، فهذا تمام الكلام في السؤال الأول .
And the answer to the fourth doubt: namely your saying: 'The essence requires one of the two sides precisely, while the occurrence of its opposite upon that side is possible.' So we say: The essence requires that it be either existent or nonexistent. Regarding his saying: 'This meaning is vague, and the vague has no existence in external things, and what has no existence in external things cannot be made an effect, since it does not exist in external things,' we say: The requirement of the essence is not being free of existence and nonexistence together, and that not being free of them is a determinate matter. So this completes the discussion of the first question.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an answer to another objection. The doubt claims that the essence (al-mahiyya) requires one specific side (existence or nonexistence), yet the opposite side can still occur, implying inconsistency. The author responds: The essence only requires that it be either existent or nonexistent – it does not specify which. The objector then argues that this requirement is vague and thus cannot be a real property. The author counters that the requirement is not vague: it is the determinate property of not being free of both existence and nonexistence simultaneously. This property is specific and can serve as a basis for needing an effect (mu'aththir). This concludes the first question.

The Need for an Effect Is Not Conditional Upon Origination
The author refutes the condition of origination and provides two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for an effective cause is based on possibility itself, not on temporal origination.

Core Idea
Mere possibility, not origination, is the cause of needing a cause.

The author addresses an objection that only originated things need a cause. He argues that even enduring possibles require a cause, because need arises from possibility alone. He provides two proofs: one from the nature of possibility, and another showing that making origination a condition leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is a refutation with two proofs.
2
Understand the opponent's condition and author's denial
Identify the opponent's claim that need is conditional upon origination, and the author's counterclaim that mere possibility is the cause.
3
Analyze the first proof
Focus on how possibility (equal relation to existence/nonexistence) demands a preponderator, making origination irrelevant.
This proof uses the principle of sufficient reason.
4
Analyze the second proof
Trace the logical dependency chain showing that if origination were the cause or condition, circularity or infinite regress results.
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : « افتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر مشروط بكون ذلك الممكن محدثا ، فنقول : هذا الشرط غير معتبر ، بل ندعي إن مجرد ذلك الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول: إنا بينا أن الممكن هو الذي تكون نسبة الوجود والعدم إليه على السوية ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، قضى العقل بأنه لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، فعلمنا أن هذا القدر كاف في تحقق الحاجة إلى المؤثر ، فيبقى قيد الحاجة ، محذوفا عن درجة الاعتبار الثاني : إن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة للحاجة ، ولا جزءاً من العلة ولا شرطا لها ، والدليل عليه أن الحدوث كيفية في الوجود ، فهي متوقفة على الوجود ، المتوقف على تأثير المؤثر فيه ، المتوقف على احتياج الأثر إلى المؤثر ، المتوقف على علة تلك الحاجة، وعلى جزء تلك العلة ، وعلى شرط تلك العلة ، فلو كان الحدوث نفس العلة أو جزءها أو شرطها ، لزم تأخر الشيء الواحد عن نفسه بمراتب وهو محال ، فيثبت مما ذكرنا : أن الممكن مفتقر إلى المؤثر سواء كان ذلك الممكن حادثاً أو باقيا .
As for the second question, which is his saying: 'The need of the possible for the effect is conditioned upon that possible being originated,' so we say: This condition is not considered; rather we claim that mere possibility is the cause of the need for the effect. And there are proofs for it: The first: We have shown that the possible is that to which the relation of existence and nonexistence is equal, and everything that is so, reason judges that one of its two sides is not preponderated except by a preponderator. So we know that this much is sufficient for the realization of the need for the effect, so the condition of origination remains removed from the degree of consideration. The second: Origination cannot be the cause of the need, nor a part of the cause, nor a condition for it. The proof for it is that origination is a quality in existence, so it is dependent on existence, which is dependent on the effect of the effect upon it, which is dependent on the effect's need for the effect, which is dependent on the cause of that need, and on the part of that cause, and on the condition of that cause. If origination were the very cause or its part or its condition, then the same thing would be delayed from itself by degrees, which is impossible. So from what we have mentioned, it is established that the possible is in need of the effect whether that possible is originated or enduring.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a condition. The opponent claims that the possible's need for an effect (mu'aththir) is conditioned upon it being originated (hadith). The author denies this and asserts that mere possibility (imkan) is the cause of the need. Two proofs are given: First, since the possible is equally related to existence and nonexistence, reason demands a preponderator (murajjih) to tip the balance. This need is already established by possibility alone, so the condition of origination is irrelevant. Second, if origination were the cause or part/condition of the need, a vicious circularity would result: origination is a quality in existence, thus dependent on existence, which in turn depends on the effect's action, which depends on the need for the effect, which depends on the cause of that need – if origination were that cause, then the need would be dependent on origination, and origination dependent on need, leading to infinite regress or self-dependence, which is absurd. Therefore, need for the effect is not conditional on origination; both originated and enduring possibles require an effect.

Key Terms
ممكن
مؤثر
حدوث
إمكان
مرجح
علة
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only originated things need a cause; enduring things do not.
Correction: The author proves that enduring possibles also need a preserving cause because need arises from possibility itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the cause of the possible's need for an effect?
A) Origination (hadath)
B) Its being possible (imkan)
C) Its being eternal
D) Its being a substance

Key Takeaway
The need for an effect is not conditional on origination; possibility itself suffices.

Answer to the First Doubt: Referencing Earlier Proof
The author responds that the objection has already been answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection about re-creating the existent by referencing earlier sections, and why this is a valid response.

Core Idea
The objection's answer was already given; repetition is unnecessary.

The author faces a doubt: if an enduring thing needs an effect during its persistence, it would imply creating something that already exists, which seems absurd. Instead of repeating the full refutation, he points to earlier chapters where this has already been resolved. This lesson shows how earlier arguments preempt later objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author mentions two objections: one about 'creating the existent' and another about 'need of nonexistence'. Then he simply says the answer preceded.
The Arabic uses 'والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى' to introduce the response.
2
Identify the author's method
Ask yourself: Why doesn't the author repeat the full argument? Because he expects the reader to recall or review earlier sections. This shows the interconnected structure of the work.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Consider that if the earlier proof is valid, then this objection collapses. The author's confidence in his prior reasoning allows him to simply reference it. This is a common technique in scholarly writing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : « لو افتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر لزم إيجاد الموجود ، فنقول : الجواب عنه قد تقدم في الفصول السالفة ، وكذا الجواب عن قوله : « يلزم افتقار العدم حال بقائه الى المؤثر ) .
And the answer to the first doubt: namely their saying: 'If the enduring, in its state of endurance, were in need of the effect, then it would necessitate the creation of the existent.' So we say: The answer to it has already preceded in the previous sections, and likewise the answer to his saying: 'It would necessitate the need of nonexistence, in its state of endurance, for the effect.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a brief answer to an objection. The doubt argues that if the enduring (al-baqi) needs an effect during its persistence, that would mean 'creating what already exists' (ijad al-mawjud), which is absurd. The author does not repeat the full refutation but states that the answer has already been given in earlier chapters. Similarly, the related objection about 'the need of nonexistence' is also refuted previously. This shows the author's method of referencing earlier arguments rather than repeating them.

The Builder Analogy: Distinguishing Origination from Persistence
Sanusi answers an analogy-based doubt by distinguishing movement from stability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a thing can need a cause to come into existence but not need the same cause to continue existing, using the builder analogy.

Core Idea
Coming into being and remaining in being can have different causes.

The doubt posed by opponents is clever: if an enduring thing needs a cause to persist, then a building would always need a builder to stay standing. Sanusi responds by splitting the act of building into two distinct aspects: the movement of materials into place (which depends on the builder) and their remaining there (which does not). This distinction is crucial for understanding how created things can persist after their immediate cause departs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
Read the doubt carefully: 'It would necessitate the need of the building during its construction for the builder.' The opponent assumes that if a thing needs a cause for its existence, it must also need that cause for its continuation.
2
Identify the two aspects
Look at how the author separates the movement of bodies to their places from their stability in those places. These are two different things with two different causes.
Movement = transitional, stability = staying.
3
Trace the causal link
The author says the builder causes only the movement. The stability is not caused by the builder but by the natures (tabai') of the bodies themselves. So after the builder leaves, the movement ends but the stability continues.
This shows that the cause of origination need not be the cause of persistence.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this distinction applies to the broader theological question: the enduring possible (al-mumkin al-baqi) can persist either by its own nature or by a different cause, without needing the original creator every instant.
This diffuses the doubt and supports the possibility of an enduring created world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( يلزم افتقار البناء حال بنائه إلى البناء، فنقول : انتقال تلك الأجسام إلى تلك الأحياز غير ، وبقاء تلك الأحياز غير ، فالمفتقر إلى البناء هو انتقالها إلى تلك الأحياز ، فلا جرم لم يبق ذلك الانتقال بعد مفارقة البناء، أما استقرارها في تلك الأحياز ، فليس معلول تحرك البناء بل معلول طبائع تلك الأجرام .
And the answer to the second doubt: namely their saying: 'It would necessitate the need of the building during its construction for the builder.' So we say: The movement of those bodies to those places is one thing, and their remaining in those places is another. So what is in need of the builder is their movement to those places, and necessarily that movement does not remain after the builder leaves. As for their stability in those places, it is not an effect of the builder's movement, but rather an effect of the natures of those bodies.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an analogy-based objection. The doubt argues that if the enduring needed an effect, a building, once built, would always need a builder to persist – which is false. The author replies by distinguishing two aspects: the movement of bricks to their positions (which requires the builder) and their remaining in place after construction (which does not). The builder only causes the movement; the stability is due to the natural properties (tabai') of the bodies themselves. Thus, the enduring possible's persistence is not maintained by the same cause that brought it into existence; it may be sustained by its own nature or another cause.

Key Terms
انتقال
استقرار
طبيعة
بناء
أجسام
أحياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something needs a cause to begin existing, it must also need that same cause to continue existing.
Correction: The author shows that origination and persistence can have different causes; the movement of bodies into place requires a builder, but their staying in place is due to their own natures.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the building (construction) actually depend on the builder for?
A) The remaining of the bodies in their places
B) The movement of the bodies to their places
C) The stability of the building
D) The destruction of the building

Key Takeaway
The key insight is that the cause of coming into existence is not necessarily the cause of remaining in existence; a thing can persist by its own nature or a different cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a spiritual practice you once started with great motivation. That initial drive may have faded. What now sustains it—your own cultivated nature, or a constant need for external reinforcement? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the root of your consistency?

Answering the Third Doubt: Expectation of Persistence Does Not Prove Independence
The author challenges the inference from expectation of persistence to independence from a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the expectation of an object's persistence does not logically prove that it requires no sustaining cause.

Core Idea
The assumption of persistence does not imply independence from a cause – the link is unproven.

We now examine the author's response to the third and final doubt about the enduring. The doubt claims that our natural expectation of an object's future persistence shows it is independent of a cause. The author grants the expectation but challenges the inference. Let's read his concise rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the structure: a doubt is presented, then the author responds.
The author uses a very precise logical style.
2
Identify the doubt and its claim
The doubt states: knowledge of present existence leads to an assumption (zann) of future persistence, and that implies the enduring is independent of any effect. Identify these two steps.
3
Understand the author's concession
The author concedes that the assumption (zann) exists. He says 'There is no dispute that this assumption exists'. Why do you think he concedes this?
He is not denying the psychological fact, only its inferred conclusion.
4
Locate the author's objection
The author then says: 'But we do not concede that this assumption occurred only because the enduring is independent of the effect.' He demands the proof: 'What is the proof that the matter is so?' This shifts the burden of proof.
The author does not need to prove the opposite; he only questions the link.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this response is effective. The objectors cannot simply assume that expectation equals logical necessity. The discussion ends here, leaving the doubt unanswered without proof.
This is a classic kalam move: accept the premise but deny the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : و العلم بوجوده ( في الحال ) يوجب ظن بقائه في المستقبل، وذلك يقتضي استغناء الباقي عن المؤثر .. فنقول : لا نزاع في أن هذا الظن حاصل ، لكن لا نسلم أن هذا الظن إنما حصل لأجل أن الباقي حال بقائه غني عن المؤثر ، فما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ فهذا جملة الكلام المعلوم في هذا الموضوع وبالله التوفيق
And the answer to the third doubt: namely their saying: 'Knowledge of its existence in the present necessitates the assumption of its persistence in the future, and that implies that the enduring is independent of the effect.' So we say: There is no dispute that this assumption exists, but we do not concede that this assumption occurred only because the enduring, in its state of endurance, is independent of the effect. So what is the proof that the matter is so? This is the complete known discussion on this subject, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the last objection regarding the enduring. The doubt argues that since we naturally assume an existing thing will persist, it must not need an effect to maintain it. The author grants that the assumption (zann) of persistence exists, but he denies that this assumption arises from the thing's independence from an effect. The burden of proof remains on the objector to show that the assumption is caused by the thing's independence, rather than by other factors such as habit or divine custom. The author does not provide further refutation here, indicating that the point is sufficiently clear. The passage ends with 'With God is success' (bi-llahi al-tawfiq), closing the discussion.

Key Terms
علم
ظن
بقاء
باقي
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that our natural expectation of an object's persistence logically proves that it requires no cause to continue existing.
Correction: The author shows that this is a non-sequitur; the existence of an assumption does not prove its cause, and the burden is on the objector to demonstrate that the assumption stems from the thing's independence.

Checkpoint
What does the author concede regarding the assumption of persistence?
A) That it is false
B) That it exists, but does not prove independence from an effect
C) That it implies the enduring needs an effect
D) That it is based on rational proof

Key Takeaway
The assumption of an object's persistence does not prove it is independent of a cause; the burden of proof lies with the objector.

Why Contingency Always Demands a Cause
The author proves that the contingent always needs an effect-producing cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent beings always need an effect-producing cause, regardless of being originated or continuing.

Core Idea
Contingency itself demands a cause, not just origination.

This passage tackles a common assumption that only newly originated things need a cause. The author proves that the need for a cause is intrinsic to contingency itself, using a logical argument that exposes an impossibility if origination were the cause. Let's examine the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: the conditional statement and its implication.
The passage begins mid-sentence with 'المؤثر', but forms a complete argument.
2
Identify the refuted assumption
Notice that the author is arguing against the idea that origination (huduth) is the cause, part of the cause, or condition for the need of a cause. Locate that conditional in the text.
3
Understand the impossibility of self-delay
If origination itself were the cause of the need, then a single thing (the contingent) would be delayed from itself by degrees. Think about why that is impossible—it implies a circularity or infinite regress in causation.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: the consequence is absurd, so the premise must be false.
4
Grasp the conclusion
From the impossibility, the author concludes that the contingent always needs an effect-producing cause (mu'aththir), whether it is originated or continuing (baqin). This means the need is not due to temporality but to contingency itself.

So if origination (al-huduth) were the cause itself or part of it or its condition, then a single thing would be delayed from itself by degrees, and that is impossible. So it is established from what we have mentioned that the contingent (al-mumkin) is in need of the effect-producing cause, whether that contingent is originated (hadith) or continuing (baqin).

Commentary
This is a proof that the contingent being always requires a cause, regardless of whether it is newly originated or persists in existence. The author argues that if origination (huduth) itself were the cause or part of the cause or a condition of the cause for the need of a cause, then a single thing would be delayed relative to itself by multiple degrees, which is impossible. Therefore, the need for a cause cannot be attributed solely to origination. The conclusion is that the contingent is always in need of an effect-producing cause (mu'aththir), whether it is originated or continuing.

Refuting Three Objections to Continuing Things Needing a Cause
The author answers three objections raised against the view that continuing things need a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the view that continuing things require a cause and how each is refuted.

Core Idea
Continuing things require a cause, and common objections are answered by distinguishing persistence from origination.

Before reading the author's responses, it is helpful to know the three objections skeptics raise. The passage presents each doubt followed by a concise refutation. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes between different aspects of existence to counter each argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow.
The passage begins and ends with framing statements.
2
Identify the first objection and its refutation
The first objection claims that if a continuing thing needed a cause, it would imply bringing the existent into existence again. The author says the answer was already given earlier.
Notice that the author refers to previous chapters.
3
Identify the second objection and its refutation
The second objection analogizes to a building needing a builder while it stands. The refutation distinguishes the movement of materials from their stability.
Key distinction: movement requires builder, stability is due to natures of bodies.
4
Identify the third objection and its refutation
The third objection uses our expectation of future existence as evidence of independence. The author denies that the presumption proves the point and demands evidence.
The refutation shifts the burden of proof.
5
Reflect on the pattern
Each refutation either refers to prior discussion, distinguishes between different aspects, or questions the logical connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر الآن الجواب عن الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في إثبات أن الباقي لا يحتاج إلى المؤثر : فالجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : « لو افتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر لزم إيجاد الموجود ، فنقول : الجواب عنه قد تقدم في الفصول السالفة ، وكذا الجواب عن قوله : « يلزم افتقار العدم حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ) . والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : ( يلزم افتقار البناء حال بنائه إلى البناء، فنقول : انتقال تلك الأجسام إلى تلك الأحياز غير ، وبقاء تلك الأحياز غير ، فالمفتقر إلى البناء هو انتقالها إلى تلك الأحياز ، فلا جرم لم يبق ذلك الانتقال بعد مفارقة البناء، أما استقرارها في تلك الأحياز ، فليس معلول تحرك البناء بل معلول طبائع تلك الأجرام . والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : وهي قولهم : و العلم بوجوده ( في الحال ) يوجب ظن بقائه في المستقبل، وذلك يقتضي استغناء الباقي عن المؤثر .. فنقول : لا نزاع في أن هذا الظن حاصل ، لكن لا نسلم أن هذا الظن إنما حصل لأجل أن الباقي حال بقائه غني عن المؤثر ، فما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ فهذا جملة الكلام المعلوم في هذا الموضوع وبالله التوفيق.
And let us now mention the answer to the arguments which they held to in proving that the continuing (al-baqi) does not need the effect-producing cause (al-mu'aththir): The answer to the first doubt: which is their statement: 'If the continuing in its state of continuance were in need of the effect-producing cause, that would necessitate bringing the existent into existence (ijad al-mawjud).' So we say: The answer to it has already preceded in previous chapters, and likewise the answer to his statement: 'It necessitates the non-existent in its state of continuance being in need of the effect-producing cause.' And the answer to the second doubt: which is their statement: 'It necessitates that a building during its construction is in need of the builder.' So we say: The movement of those bodies into those spaces is one thing, and the permanence of those spaces is another. So what is in need of the builder is their movement into those spaces; inevitably that movement does not remain after the builder departs. As for their stability in those spaces, it is not the effect of the builder's movement but rather the effect of the natures of those bodies. And the answer to the third doubt: which is their statement: 'Knowledge of its existence at present necessitates the presumption of its continuance in the future, and that implies the independence of the continuing from the effect-producing cause.' So we say: There is no dispute that this presumption occurs, but we do not accept that this presumption only occurs because the continuing in its state of continuance is independent of the effect-producing cause. So what is the evidence that it is so? This is the summary of the known discourse on this topic, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage answers three objections. The first objection claims that if a continuing entity needed a cause, that would be bringing the existent into existence again. The author replies that the answer was already given earlier. The second objection analogizes a building needing a builder while it stands; the answer distinguishes the movement of the building materials from their stability after construction. The third objection argues that our expectation of future existence shows independence; the author denies that the presumption proves the point and demands evidence. The passage concludes with a closing remark.
