Proof of Cause-Effect Simultaneity
Author presents a logical proof that the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect, and why separating them in time leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Cause and effect cannot be separated in time; to exist, they must be simultaneous.

This passage presents a rigorous logical argument for the simultaneity of cause and effect. The author systematically eliminates the possibilities that the cause could exist before or after the effect, and even addresses a potential objection. By the end, you will see why the only consistent scenario is that when the effect occurs, the cause must be present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two initial options
Read the first part of the passage where the author sets up the conditional: if the cause is not simultaneous with the effect, then either the cause existed before the effect or after it became non-existent. Notice how these two options are introduced.
The author uses a classic reductio ad absurdum structure.
2
Understand why each option is invalid
The first option is invalid because if the cause existed but no effect occurred at that time, it cannot be called a cause. The second is invalid because a non-existent thing cannot cause existence. Grasp these two contradictions.
This is the core of the proof: cause must be present when effect occurs.
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
An objector suggests the cause might produce the effect in a later time while itself existing earlier. The author refutes this by showing that in the later time, the cause is either non-existent (impossible) or no effect occurs (contradiction).
The refutation reinforces that cause and effect must coexist.
4
Conclude with the only valid scenario
The passage concludes that the only consistent possibility is that the cause exists simultaneously with its effect. This is exactly what the argument set out to prove.
This conclusion is fundamental to Islamic theology regarding divine attributes.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof matters: if cause and effect could be separated, then God could create without being present, but the proof shows that the Creator must be continuously present with creation.
This connects to discussions about God's attributes of power and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو لم تكن العلة واجبة الحصول حال حصول المعلول ، لكانت إما أن تكون علة لهذا المعلول حال ما كانت تلك العلة موجودة ، أو بعد أن صارت معدومة ، والأول باطل لأن في تلك الحالة المتقدمة لم يصدر عنها شيء أصلا ، فامتنع كونها علة ومؤثرة . والثاني باطل ، لأنها في الزمان الثاني قد صارت معدومة ، والمعدوم لا يكون علة للموجود . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلة حال وجودها أوجبت وجود المعلول في الزمان الثاني منه ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل ، لأن في الزمان الثاني إن لم يصدر عنه أثر البتة ، لم يكن في ذلك الزمان علة ولا مؤثراً البتة . وإذا لم يكن في ذلك الزمان علة ولا مؤثراً فلو صار علة في الزمان الثاني ، مع أنه في الزمان الثاني صار معدوما ، لزم كون المعدوم علة للموجود ، وهو محال ، وإن لم يصر علة أيضا في الزمان الثاني ، كان هذا تصريحا بأنه ليس علة ولا مؤثرا البتة ، وأما إن قلنا : إنه في الزمان الأول قد صدر عنه أثر ، وكان هذا الأثر حاصلا في ذلك الزمان ، فحئنذ يكون المؤثر موجودا حال حصول الأثر وذلك عين المطلوب.
And when you know this, we say: if the cause were not necessary to exist at the time the effect exists, then it would either be a cause for this effect while that cause existed, or after it became non-existent. The first is invalid because in that prior state nothing proceeded from it at all, so its being a cause and efficient is impossible. The second is invalid because in the second time it has become non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be a cause for the existent. If it is said: why may it not be said that the cause during its existence caused the existence of the effect in a second time after it? We say: this is invalid, because in the second time if no effect proceeds from it at all, then it is not in that time a cause or efficient at all. And if it is not in that time a cause or efficient, then if it were a cause in the second time, while in the second time it became non-existent, then the non-existent being a cause for the existent would follow, which is impossible. And if it is not a cause either in the second time, then this is a declaration that it is not a cause or efficient at all. But if we say: in the first time an effect proceeded from it, and this effect existed in that time, then the efficient exists at the time the effect occurs, and that is exactly what is sought.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof for the necessity of cause-effect simultaneity. The author presents a conditional argument: if the cause does not have to exist simultaneously with the effect, then two possibilities exist: either the cause caused the effect when it existed (but then no effect occurred at that time, so it cannot be a cause), or it caused the effect after it ceased to exist (impossible because non-existence cannot cause existence). Then he anticipates an objection: could the cause, while existing, produce the effect at a later time? He refutes this: if no effect occurs in that later time, the cause is not a cause then; if the cause is non-existent in that later time, effect cannot arise from nothing; if it neither causes then, it is simply not a cause. The only consistent scenario is that the cause exists simultaneously with its effect, which is precisely the conclusion sought (al-matlub).

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
المعدوم
المؤثر
الزمان الأول
الزمان الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that cause and effect can be separated in time, e.g., a cause produces an effect at a later time.
Correction: The author shows that such separation leads to logical contradictions: either the cause was not a cause when it existed, or non-existence causes existence, both impossible. Therefore, cause and effect must be simultaneous.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the problem with saying the cause caused the effect after it ceased to exist?
A) The cause might still exist in another form.
B) The non-existent cannot be a cause for the existent.
C) The effect would have to be eternal.
D) The cause would have to be simultaneous with itself.

Key Takeaway
The simultaneity of cause and effect is logically necessary; any temporal gap leads to absurdity because a non-existent cause cannot produce an effect, and a cause without an effect is not a cause.

What would it look like to align your practice with the truth that the Efficient Cause must be present now?
?
If a non-existent cause cannot produce an effect, then your own existence right now requires a cause that exists right now. Who is that Cause? Are you living as if you depend on Him every instant, or have you fallen into the illusion of self-sufficiency? How would your day be different if you consciously recalled this moment by moment?

Proof of Simultaneity of Cause and Effect
Razi proves that the effective cause must exist simultaneously with its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the effective cause must coexist with its effect at the same moment.

Core Idea
A cause cannot act before or after its effect—only simultaneously.

This passage presents a rigorous proof from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that the effective cause must be present at the same time as its effect. Razi considers two alternatives—cause acting while existing or after non-existence—and shows both lead to absurdity. He also refutes an objection that the cause could produce the effect later. The lesson walks through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's thesis
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to see the overall claim.
The thesis is stated in the first sentence.
2
Follow the reductio
Notice how Razi reduces the alternative to two impossible cases.
The key move is showing that non-existence cannot cause existence.
3
Examine the objection
Read the objection that the cause could act at its time to produce an effect later, and Razi's response.
Razi shows this leads to contradiction.
4
Identify the conclusion
The only consistent position is that cause and effect are simultaneous.
This is the desired proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة المؤثرة يجب أن تكون موجودة ، حال وجود المعلول ، أما في كتاب الإشارات وسائر الكتب فلم يذكر هذه المقدمة ، والحق ما ذكره في كتاب النجاة بالبيان الذي ذكرناه . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو لم تكن العلة واجبة الحصول حال حصول المعلول ، لكانت إما أن تكون علة لهذا المعلول ، حال ما كانت تلك العلة موجودة ، أو بعد أن صارت معدومة ، والأول باطل لأن في تلك الحالة المتقدمة لم يصدر عنها شيء أصلا ، فامتنع كونها علة ومؤثرة . والثاني باطل ، لأنها في الزمان الثاني قد صارت معدومة ، والمعدوم لا يكون علة للموجود . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العلة حال وجودها أوجبت وجود المعلول في الزمان الثاني منه ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل ، لأن في الزمان الثاني إن لم يصدر عنه أثر البتة ، لم يكن في ذلك الزمان علة ولا مؤثراً البتة . وإذا لم يكن في ذلك الزمان علة ولا مؤثراً فلو صار علة في الزمان الثاني ، مع أنه في الزمان الثاني صار معدوما ، لزم كون المعدوم علة للموجود ، وهو محال ، وإن لم يصر علة أيضا في الزمان الثاني ، كان هذا تصريحا بأنه ليس علة ولا مؤثرا البتة ، وأما إن قلنا : إنه في الزمان الأول قد صدر عنه أثر ، وكان هذا الأثر حاصلا في ذلك الزمان ، فحينئذ يكون المؤثر موجودا حال حصول الأثر وذلك عين المطلوب.
The effective cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect. As for the Book of Indications and other books, they did not mention this premise; the truth is what he mentioned in the Book of Deliverance according to the explanation we gave. And if you know this, we say: If the cause were not necessarily present at the time the effect occurs, then either it is a cause for this effect while the cause exists, or after it becomes non-existent. The first is invalid because in that prior state nothing emanated from it at all, so it is impossible for it to be a cause and an effect. The second is invalid because in the second moment it has become non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be a cause for the existent. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the cause at the time of its existence necessitated the existence of the effect in a second moment from it? We say: This is invalid, because in the second moment, if no effect emanated from it at all, then it was in that moment neither a cause nor an effective agent at all. And if it was not in that moment a cause nor an effective agent, then if it became a cause in the second moment, while it became non-existent in the second moment, it would necessitate that the non-existent be a cause for the existent, which is impossible. And if it also did not become a cause in the second moment, this is a declaration that it is not a cause nor an effective agent at all. As for if we say that in the first moment an effect emanated from it, and this effect occurred in that moment, then the effective agent exists at the time of the occurrence of the effect, and that is exactly what is required.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of simultaneity between cause and effect. The author first states the thesis: the effective cause must exist at the same time as its effect. Then he notes that some books omitted this premise. He then provides a conditional proof: if the cause were not simultaneous, then either it causes the effect while it exists (but then the effect is not yet present, so nothing is caused) or after it ceases to exist (but the non-existent cannot cause). An objection is raised: could the cause at its time bring about the effect later? The author refutes this: in the later time, if no effect emanated from it, it was not a cause; if it did become a cause while being non-existent, that is impossible; if it did not become a cause, then it is not a cause at all. The only coherent option is that the cause produced the effect in the first moment, so the cause exists when the effect occurs. This proves the desired simultaneity.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة (effective cause)
المعلول (effect)
المعية (simultaneity)
المعدوم (non-existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause can bring about its effect in a later moment even if it ceases to exist.
Correction: The cause must exist at the same moment as its effect; otherwise, the non-existent would be causing, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if the cause is not simultaneous with the effect, what are the two possibilities considered?
A) The cause exists before the effect, or the cause exists after the effect.
B) The cause is a cause while it exists, or after it becomes non-existent.
C) The cause is a cause by itself, or by time.
D) The cause is a cause for the effect in the first moment, or in the second moment.

Key Takeaway
The effective cause must exist simultaneously with its effect; any delay would require the impossible scenario of a non-existent cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing outcomes in your life to secondary causes—like your own effort, luck, or other people—without simultaneously recognizing Allah as the true cause in that very moment? How does Razi's proof that a cause must be present with its effect challenge you to constantly connect every event directly to Allah's presence and power?
?
If you truly believed that nothing happens without Allah being the immediate cause, how would it change the way you rely on people, plans, or material means? Would you still panic when those secondary supports fail?
?
This lesson shows that a non-existent thing cannot cause an existent. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if past causes (like a past sin or a past success) still have power in the present, forgetting that only Allah, who is ever-present, is the real cause of your current state? What would it mean for you to 'return' each moment to Him as the only effective cause?

Objections 1-2: Essential/Temporal Simultaneity and Redundancy
Razi presents the first two objections: essential vs temporal simultaneity and the redundancy argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two objections against the simultaneity of cause and effect, focusing on the distinction between essential and temporal simultaneity and the argument that simultaneous causation would be redundant.

Core Idea
If cause and effect are simultaneous, either they share essence or time — both impossible.

The opponents of causal simultaneity raise several logical objections. The first two objections attack the very concept of simultaneity, arguing that it must be either essential (by essence) or temporal (by time). The first shows essential simultaneity is impossible for cause and effect, while the second argues temporal simultaneity leads to absurdities like creating what already exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for objections 1-2
Read the Arabic text for the first two objections carefully. Note how each objection is structured: a conditional statement followed by a refutation.
The passage uses the template 'لو كانت ... لكانت إما ...' for the first objection.
2
Understand the distinction between essential and temporal simultaneity
Identify the two types of simultaneity mentioned. Essential simultaneity means sharing the same essence, which is impossible for cause and effect. Temporal simultaneity means existing at the same time, but that only applies to temporal things, not to eternal causes.
The cause may be eternal, so temporal simultaneity doesn't apply.
3
Analyze the second objection's logic
The second objection argues that if the cause acts when the effect is already present, it would be creating something that already exists — an absurdity. Therefore, the cause must precede the effect.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on why these objections matter
Consider how these objections challenge the idea that cause and effect are simultaneous. They form the foundation for the opponents' view of temporal precedence.
These are just two of seven objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من خالف في وجود هذه المعية بأمور : الأول : إن هذه المعية لو كانت معتبرة ، لكانت إما أن تكون عبارة عن المعية بالذات، أو عن المعية بالزمان ، والأول باطل ، باتفاق العقلاء على أن العلة لا تكون مع العلول بالذات، والثاني باطل لأن المعية الزمانية لا تصدق إلا على ما كان زمانيا ، وكل زماني فهو متحرك متغير ، وما لا يكون كذلك كانت المعية الزمانية ممتنعة ، في حقه . الثاني : إن العلة لو أثرت في المعلول حال كون المعلول حاضرا ، كان ذلك إيجادا للموجود وهو محال ، فوجب أن يقال : العلة متقدمة على المعلول حتى يعقل كونها مؤثرة في نقل ذلك المعلول ، من العدم إلى الوجود . الثالث : إنا إذا رمينا الهم فلا يزال يكون كل واحد من المدافعات السابقة علة لاندفاع آخر، يحصل بعده على الترتيب والولاء ، ولولا ذلك ، لوجب سقوط السهم عند انفصاله عن اليد . الرابع : إن الفكر يوجب العلم بالمطلوب ، مع أن الفكر يجب تقدمه على العلم بالنتيجة ، لأن الفكر في الشيء حال حصول العلم به محال . الخامس : إن الثقيل النازل يكون كل جزء من أجزاء حركته علة الحصول الجزء الذي يليه إما بأن يكون علة مؤثرة كما هو عند بعض المعتزلة ، أو يكون علة معدة ، كما هو عند الفلاسفة . السادس : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن المؤثر ما لم يوجد بتمامه ، استحال أن يصدر عنه الأثر ، وتمام وجود المؤثر متقدم على وجود الأثر ، فالمؤثر التام يجب أن يكون متقدما على الأثر . السابع : إنه يقال حركت يدي فتحرك المفتاح ، أو ثم تحرك المفتاح ، وصريح العقل شاهد بحصول هذا الترتيب ، وذلك يدل على أن العلة يجب تقدمها على المعلول .
Those who opposed the existence of this simultaneity argued by several matters: First: If this simultaneity were considered, it would either be an expression of essential simultaneity or temporal simultaneity. The first is invalid by the consensus of rational people that the cause is not essentially simultaneous with the effect. The second is invalid because temporal simultaneity only applies to what is temporal, and everything temporal is moving and changing, and what is not so, temporal simultaneity is impossible with respect to it. Second: If the cause were to affect the effect while the effect is present, that would be bringing the existent into existence, which is impossible. So it must be said that the cause precedes the effect so that it can be understood as effective in transferring that effect from non-existence to existence. Third: When we shoot an arrow, each one of the preceding draughts [air currents] continues to be a cause for a subsequent repulsion, occurring after it in order and succession; otherwise, the arrow would have to fall when it leaves the hand. Fourth: Thought necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion, while thought must precede knowledge of the result, because thought about a thing at the time of obtaining knowledge of it is impossible. Fifth: A heavy descending body, each part of its motion is a cause for the occurrence of the part that follows it, either as an effective cause as with some Mu'tazila, or as a preparatory cause as with the philosophers. Sixth: We know by necessity that as long as the effective agent does not exist in its entirety, it is impossible for the effect to emanate from it, and the complete existence of the effective agent precedes the existence of the effect; thus the complete effective agent must precede the effect. Seventh: It is said, 'I moved my hand, then the key moved,' or 'then the key moved,' and the discernment of reason witnesses the occurrence of this order, and that indicates that the cause must precede the effect.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage enumerates seven objections raised by those who deny that the cause must be simultaneous with its effect. Each objection provides a counterexample or a logical argument: (1) Simultaneity can only be by essence or by time, but essential simultaneity is impossible for cause and effect, and temporal simultaneity only applies to changing things, not to eternal causes. (2) If the cause acts when the effect is already present, it would be creating what already exists, which is absurd; therefore, the cause must precede. (3) In shooting an arrow, successive air currents cause the arrow's motion in sequence, showing cause precedes effect. (4) Thought must precede knowledge, not be simultaneous with it. (5) A falling body's motion occurs in successive parts, each caused by the previous. (6) The complete cause must exist entirely before its effect. (7) Empirical examples like moving a hand and then a key show temporal order. These objections collectively argue that causality requires temporal precedence, not simultaneity.

Key Terms
المعية بالذات
المعية بالزمان
إيجاد الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that simultaneity of cause and effect is obvious and requires no analysis.
Correction: The passage shows that even defining simultaneity leads to difficulties, and opponents argue it leads to logical absurdities.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, which type of simultaneity is rejected because the cause is not essentially the same as the effect?
A) Temporal simultaneity
B) Essential simultaneity
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The first two objections argue that simultaneity between cause and effect is either impossible to define or leads to logical absurdity, thus supporting the view that cause must precede effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If cause and effect cannot be simultaneous, what does that mean for your experience of praying then seeing results? Do you ever feel that God's action is 'delayed'? Could it be that the cause (God's will) is eternal and not bound by time, so the effect appears in time as a fresh creation?
?
Consider a blessing you enjoy right now—your health, a relationship, or provision. Are you tempted to think of it as a natural result of your own actions? How does the redundancy argument remind you that every moment of existence is a direct gift, not a mechanical consequence?
?
The second objection shows that if the effect were already present, the cause would be superfluous. Have you ever acted as if you could achieve something without acknowledging God as the ultimate cause? What would change if you truly believed that every good thing is created anew each instant?

Objections 3-5: Sequential Causation in Arrows, Thought, and Falling Bodies
Razi presents objections 3-5 using examples of sequential causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three objections that use empirical examples of sequential causation to argue that cause must precede effect.

Core Idea
In arrows, thoughts, and falling bodies, each step is caused by the previous — showing cause precedes effect.

The opponents continue with concrete examples from everyday experience and mental processes. The third objection uses the example of an arrow shot in the air, where successive air currents cause its continued motion. The fourth objection points to reasoning: thought must precede knowledge. The fifth objection analyzes a falling body, where each segment of motion is caused by the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for objections 3-5
Read the Arabic text for the third, fourth, and fifth objections. Notice how each uses a specific phenomenon to illustrate temporal precedence.
These objections are empirical in nature.
2
Analyze the arrow example (objection 3)
In archery, the arrow's flight depends on successive air currents. Each current causes the next, showing a chain of causes and effects in time.
If cause and effect were simultaneous, the arrow would fall immediately.
3
Understand the thought example (objection 4)
Thought about a topic produces knowledge of the result. The thinking process must occur before the knowledge is obtained, not at the same time.
This is a psychological argument.
4
Study the falling body example (objection 5)
A heavy object falling: each part of its motion is a cause for the next part. This can be seen as an effective cause (Mu'tazila view) or preparatory cause (philosophers view).
Both schools agree on the sequential nature.
5
Synthesize the common theme
All three objections demonstrate that in natural and mental processes, causes precede their effects sequentially.
This challenges the idea of simultaneous causation.

Objections 6-7: Complete Cause Precedence and Empirical Order
Razi presents the final two objections: complete cause precedence and empirical order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final two objections: the necessity of the complete cause preceding the effect, and the empirical evidence from hand-key motion showing temporal order.

Core Idea
A cause must exist in its entirety before its effect, and common experience confirms this order.

The opponents conclude with two more arguments. The sixth objection is based on the principle that a cause must be fully present before its effect can occur. The seventh objection points to simple empirical observation: when we move our hand, the key moves only after the hand, not at the same instant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for objections 6-7
Read the Arabic text for the sixth and seventh objections. Notice the logical necessity argument in the sixth and the common-sense example in the seventh.
The seventh objection relies on 'the discernment of reason' (صريح العقل).
2
Understand the sixth objection
The cause must exist completely (تام) for the effect to occur. The complete existence of the cause precedes the effect. This is a necessary truth.
This is a metaphysical principle.
3
Analyze the seventh objection
When we say 'I moved my hand, then the key moved,' reason recognizes a temporal order. The cause (hand movement) is followed by the effect (key movement).
This is a direct empirical observation.
4
Consider the cumulative force of all seven objections
Each objection adds a different perspective: logical, empirical, psychological, metaphysical. Together they form a strong case against simultaneous causation.
The next section will likely respond to these objections.

Six Proofs for the Temporal Priority of Cause over Effect
Six arguments demonstrating that cause must precede effect in time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand six independent proofs from everyday observation and rational necessity that establish the temporal priority of cause over effect.

Core Idea
Cause must precede effect in time; otherwise, causality is absurd.

This passage presents six concise arguments drawn from physics, psychology, and common experience, each demonstrating that a cause cannot be simultaneous with its effect. These proofs form a critical foundation for understanding causal relationships in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the six proofs
The Arabic text numbers proofs from second to seventh. Read the translation to grasp each argument before diving into analysis.
Notice that the first proof is not given here; it was covered earlier.
2
Analyze the 'impossible creation' argument
The second proof: if cause and effect were simultaneous, the cause would be 'creating an already existent' thing, which is impossible. So cause must precede effect.
This is a direct logical argument from impossibility.
3
Examine the empirical arguments
The third, fourth, and fifth proofs use examples of an arrow, thought, and a falling object to show temporal sequence in causality.
These examples cover different domains: physics, psychology, and motion.
4
Understand the necessity argument
The sixth proof states that the cause must exist completely before the effect can issue. The seventh proof uses common experience ('I moved my hand, so the key moved') to affirm temporal order.
These final proofs ground the conclusion in necessary and observed reality.
5
Reflect on the unified conclusion
All six proofs converge on the same point: cause is temporally prior to effect. This refutes any notion of simultaneity.
Consider how this principle underpins causal reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن العلة لو أثرت في المعلول حال كون المعلول حاضرا ، كان ذلك إيجادا للموجود وهو محال ، فوجب أن يقال : العلة متقدمة على المعلول حتى يعقل كونها مؤثرة في نقل ذلك المعلول ، من العدم إلى الوجود .

الثالث : إنا إذا رمينا السهم فلا يزال يكون كل واحد من المدافعات السابقة علة لاندفاع آخر، يحصل بعده على الترتيب والولاء ، ولولا ذلك ، لوجب سقوط السهم عند انفصاله عن اليد .

الرابع : إن الفكر يوجب العلم بالمطلوب ، مع أن الفكر يجب تقدمه على العلم بالنتيجة ، لأن الفكر في الشيء حال حصول العلم به محال .

الخامس : إن الثقيل النازل يكون كل جزء من أجزاء حركته علة الحصول الجزء الذي يليه إما بأن يكون علة مؤثرة كما هو عند بعض المعتزلة ، أو يكون علة معدة ، كما هو عند الفلاسفة .

السادس : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن المؤثر ما لم يوجد بتمامه ، استحال أن يصدر عنه الأثر ، وتمام وجود المؤثر متقدم على وجود الأثر ، فالمؤثر التام يجب أن يكون متقدما على الأثر .

السابع : إنه يقال حركت يدي فتحرك المفتاح ، أو ثم تحرك المفتاح وصريح العقل شاهد بحصول هذا الترتيب ، وذلك يدل على أن العلة يجب تقدمها على المعلول .
Second: If the cause were to act upon the effect while the effect is present, that would be creating an existent thing, which is impossible. So it must be said: the cause is prior to the effect so that it can be understood to be effective in transferring that effect from non-existence to existence.

Third: When we shoot an arrow, each one of the preceding [air] resistances continues to be a cause for the repulsion of the next, occurring after it in sequence and succession. Were it not for that, the arrow would necessarily fall upon its separation from the hand.

Fourth: Thought (al-fikr) necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion, while thought must precede knowledge of the result, because thinking about a thing at the moment of obtaining knowledge of it is impossible.

Fifth: The descending heavy object: each part of its motion is a cause for the occurrence of the part that follows it, either as an efficient cause (illa mu'aththira) as according to some Mu'tazila, or as a preparatory cause (illa mu'idda) as according to the philosophers.

Sixth: We know necessarily that as long as the cause (al-mu'aththir) does not exist in its entirety, it is impossible for the effect to issue from it; and the complete existence of the cause is prior to the existence of the effect. So the complete cause must be prior to the effect.

Seventh: It is said: 'I moved my hand, so the key moved', or 'then the key moved', and the clear intellect witnesses the occurrence of this order, and that indicates that the cause must be prior to the effect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents six independent proof-arguments for the necessary temporal priority of the cause over the effect. Each proof draws on a different kind of observable or rationally necessary causal relation.

1. The second proof: If the cause were to act on the effect while the effect is already present (i.e., simultaneous), that would amount to 'creating the already existent' (ijad al-mawjud), which is impossible. Therefore the cause must be prior so that its action can be understood as bringing the effect from non-existence to existence.

2. The third proof: When an arrow is shot, the successive resistances (al-mudafi'at) from the air each become a cause for the next repulsion in temporal succession. If the cause and effect were simultaneous, the arrow would drop immediately upon leaving the hand, since there would be no temporal sequence of causes.

3. The fourth proof: Thought (al-fikr) produces knowledge of the desired conclusion, but the thought must be prior to that knowledge, because thinking about something at the very moment of knowing it is impossible. Thus cause (thought) precedes effect (knowledge).

4. The fifth proof: A heavy object descending has parts of its motion; each part is a cause for the next part, whether as an efficient cause (according to some Mu'tazila) or as a preparatory cause (according to the philosophers). In either case, temporal priority is required.

5. The sixth proof: We know necessarily that unless the cause exists in its complete state, the effect cannot issue. The complete existence of the cause is prior to the effect, so the complete cause must be prior.

6. The seventh proof: Empirical observation ('I moved my hand, so the key moved') shows a clear order: the cause (hand movement) precedes the effect (key movement). Reason attests to this order, so the cause must be prior.

All six proofs converge on the same conclusion: the cause must be temporally prior to the effect.

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
إيجاد الموجود
التقدم
المؤثر
الفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect can be simultaneous.
Correction: Each proof shows simultaneity leads to absurdity or contradicts observed reality. For example, if cause and effect were simultaneous, thought would occur at the same time as knowledge, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, why must the complete cause (al-mu'aththir al-tamm) be prior to the effect?
A) Because the effect cannot exist without the cause being temporally simultaneous.
B) Because the effect must be created out of nothing.
C) Because the effect cannot issue from the cause unless the cause exists in its entirety, and that prior existence is required.
D) Because the cause and effect are identical in time.

Key Takeaway
Cause must be temporally prior to effect; otherwise, causality is impossible or absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that cause must precede effect. Your repentance (tawba) is a cause—what effect are you expecting? Are you delaying the cause while hoping for the effect to appear? Will you take a concrete step today to initiate that sequence?
?
The arrow falls if it is not pushed forward by successive causes. In your spiritual journey, what are the 'successive causes'—daily prayers, good company, consistent dhikr—that keep you from falling? Which one have you neglected this week, and will you restart it today?
?
The fourth proof shows that you cannot think about a thing while knowing it. When you stand in prayer, are you truly reflecting on the words, or are you reciting from memory without thought? This lesson demands that you engage your mind before the meaning settles—will you force yourself to ponder each verse before moving on?

5 min
Refuting the Objection on the Withness of Cause and Effect
Refutation of the first objection regarding withness (ma'iyya)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the simultaneity of cause and effect is based on a false premise about time.

Core Idea
Time is not limited to motion; God's existence with each event proves it.

The opponent argues that if cause and effect are simultaneous, that simultaneity must be either essential or temporal. The author shows this objection assumes time is a property of motion, which is false. The decisive proof is that God exists at the same time as every originated thing, which refutes the opponent's foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the first objection carefully.
Notice the concise structure of objection and answer.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says 'withness' is either essential or temporal. Understand this dichotomy.
3
Understand the author's reply
The author says this objection is built on a false premise that time is a concomitant of motion.
This is key to undermining the objection.
4
Grasp the decisive proof
The author gives a necessary knowledge: God exists with every originated event, showing time is not restricted to motion.
This refutes the opponent's premise.
5
Reflect on the implication
How does this objection relate to causal priority? The opponent aims to deny temporal priority, but the author shows the argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : و إن هذه المعية ، إما أن تكون معتبرة بالذات أو بالزمان ، فنقول : هذا بناء على قولكم : إن المدة من لواحق الحركة والتغير ، وهذا عندنا باطل ، بل نقول : الدليل القاطع على بطلانه : أنا تعلم بالضرورة أن الله تعالى في هذا الوقت موجود ، مع حدوث كل حادث ، وذلك يبطل قولكم .

والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : لو كانت العلة موجودة مع المعلول لزم إيجاد الموجود ، فنقول : إن كنت تريد بهذا الكلام إيجاد الموجود مرة أخرى ، فهذا باطل ، وإن كنت تريد كونه موجداً لذلك الموجود بمعنى أنه لولا ذلك الإيجاد ، لما حصل ذلك الموجود ، فهذا حق عندنا ، فلم قلتم : إنه محال ؟

والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الرامي يفعل في السهم قوة باقية هي الموجبة لتلك الحركات، إلا أن إيهابها لكل جزء من أجزاء الحركة ، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم. وهذا هو الجواب عن نزول الثقيل . وأما قوله : و الفكر يوجب العلم بالمطلوب ، مع أن الفكر يجب حصوله قبل حصول العلم بالنتيجة ) فنقول : الفكر عبارة عن مجموع العلم بالمقدمتين ، وهما حاصلان مع العلم بالنتيجة .

أما قوله : المؤثر ما لم يوجد بتمامه ، لم يصدر عنه الأثر : فنقول : إن كان المراد من قوله وجدت العلة بتمامها : التقدم الزماني ، فهذا هو عين المطلوب ، وإن كان المراد به التقدم بالذات ، فهو مسلم لكنه لا يقدح في غرضنا . وأما التمسك بما يقال في العرف : حركت يدي فتحرك الكم ، أو ثم تحرك الكم فنقول : هذا تمسك في المضائق العقلية بالألفاظ ، وهو ضعف ، ثم بتقدير الصحة فإنا نحمله على التقدم بالذات .

والعلية والله ولي التوفيق .
The answer to the first objection: Their saying: 'This withness (al-ma'iyya) is either considered in essence or in time.' We say: This is based on your claim that time (al-mudda) is a concomitant of motion and change, which is false in our view. Rather, we say: The decisive proof of its falsity is that we know necessarily that God, exalted, exists at this time, along with the occurrence of every originated thing (hadith), and that invalidates your statement.

The answer to the second objection: Their saying: 'If the cause existed with the effect, creating the existent (ijad al-mawjud) would be necessary.' We say: If by this speech you mean creating the existent a second time, that is false. If you mean that it is an origination (ijad) of that existent in the sense that were it not for that origination, that existent would not exist, then this is true in our view. So why do you say it is impossible?

The answer to the third objection: The archer (al-rami) places in the arrow a remaining power (quwwa baqiya) that is the necessitator of those motions, except that its bestowal upon each part of the motion is conditional upon the passing away of the preceding part. And this is the answer concerning the descent of the heavy object. As for his saying: 'Thought (al-fikr) necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion, while thought must occur before the attainment of knowledge of the result,' we say: Thought is the totality of knowledge of the two premises (muqaddimatayn), and they are both present with the knowledge of the result. As for his saying: 'The cause (al-mu'aththir) as long as it does not exist completely, the effect does not issue from it,' we say: If by 'the cause exists completely' is meant temporal priority (taqaddum zamani), then this is precisely what is sought. If what is meant is priority in essence (taqaddum bi al-dhat), this is granted but does not harm our objective. As for clinging to what is said in common usage: 'I moved my hand, so the sleeve moved,' or 'then the sleeve moved,' we say: This is clinging to words in intellectual difficulties, and it is weak. Then, assuming its validity, we interpret it as priority in essence.

And causality [is established]. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes three objections (shubhat) that opponents might raise against the doctrine that causes must be temporally prior to their effects. The author responds to each in turn.

1. First objection: The opponent argues that the simultaneity ('withness') of cause and effect can be conceived either in essence or in time. If in essence, then temporal priority is not required. The author replies that this objection presupposes that time is a property of motion and change, which is false. The decisive proof is that God exists at the same time as each originated event (hadith) — this shows that time is not restricted to motion, thus undermining the objection's foundation.

2. Second objection: If cause and effect coexist, then creating an already-existing thing would be necessary, which is impossible. The author distinguishes two meanings: if 'creating the existent' means producing it a second time, that is absurd; but if it means that the cause brings the effect from non-existence to existence (even if simultaneous), that is actually true. The opponent's charge of impossibility is baseless.

3. Third objection: This response reframes several arguments. For the archer's arrow: the arrow possesses a remaining power that causes successive motions, but this power is imparted successively conditioned on the previous part's expiration — thus priority is temporal. The same applies to the heavy object's descent. For thought: thought is not a separate entity but the knowledge of the two premises, which coexist with the knowledge of the result — so no temporal priority is needed here. For the complete cause: if 'complete existence' means temporal priority, it confirms the author's position; if it means essential priority, it is admitted but irrelevant. Finally, conventional expressions like 'I moved my hand, so the key moved' are weak proofs in rational matters; even if accepted, they indicate essential priority, not necessarily temporal.

The passage ends with a closing formula affirming the doctrine of causality with divine help.

Key Terms
الشبهة
المعية
التقدم الزماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that time is only a property of motion, so if cause and effect are simultaneous, it must be essential.
Correction: The author shows that time applies to God's existence with originated events, so simultaneity can be temporal without motion.

Checkpoint
What premise does the author identify in the first objection?
A) That time is a concomitant of motion and change
B) That cause and effect are simultaneous in essence
C) That God exists outside time
D) That the opponent denies causality

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because time is not limited to motion; God's existence with each event is a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God exists with every single event in your life, even the moments of sin or neglect, does that thought make you uncomfortable? Will you allow that awareness to change how you act in your next unguarded moment?
?
If time is not merely a property of motion but a reality in which God is present, how does that affect your understanding of His closeness to you right now? What is one thought or action you will change today because of this truth?
?
The objection assumed time is only about change; the author proved it is broader. In your spiritual life, do you tend to think of God as present only in 'religious' actions (prayer, dhikr) or in every moment? Will you consciously invite Him into the ordinary, changeless moments of your day?

Causality and Created Things

4 min
Refuting the Objection of Creating the Existent
Refutation of the second objection regarding creating the existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that cause-effect simultaneity would entail creating an already-existing thing.

Core Idea
Creating the existent only means causing it to exist, not re-creating it.

The opponent argues that if cause and effect coexist, then creating the effect would be 'creating the existent' (ijad al-mawjud), which is impossible. The author carefully distinguishes two meanings: producing something a second time (impossible) versus bringing it into existence (possible). The latter is exactly what causal simultaneity entails, so the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection.
Notice the direct question at the end.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
They say cause-effect simultaneity leads to 'creating the existent' which is impossible.
3
See the author's distinction
He separates two interpretations of 'creating the existent'.
One is re-creation, the other is origination.
4
Evaluate the two interpretations
The author accepts the true meaning (origination) and rejects the absurd one (re-creation).
5
Conclude
The objection does not prove impossibility because the only plausible meaning is unproblematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The objector assumed that cause-effect simultaneity implies a logical impossibility. Are there areas of your faith where you unknowingly assume contradictions without exploring deeper meanings? What step can you take this week to investigate such assumptions?

Refuting the Third Objection: Archer, Thought, and Complete Cause
Refutation of the third objection with multiple sub-arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the third objection by analyzing the examples of the archer, thought, the complete cause, and conventional usage.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is preserved by conditioned powers and correct interpretation.

The third objection comprises several sub-arguments: the archer's arrow, the heavy object's descent, the role of thought in knowledge, the requirement of the complete cause, and conventional expressions. The author addresses each, showing that they do not undermine temporal priority but often affirm it when properly understood. This lesson unpacks each sub-answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third objection including the closing formula.
Note the multiple sub-arguments.
2
Identify the sub-arguments
List them: archer, heavy object, thought, complete cause, common usage.
3
Analyze the archer answer
The arrow has a remaining power conditioned on preceding parts, showing temporal priority.
The same applies to heavy object descent.
4
Analyze the thought answer
Thought is the knowledge of two premises which coexist with the result.
No temporal priority needed here.
5
Analyze the complete cause and common usage
Clarify meanings: temporal priority supports our view; common usage is weak and re-interpreted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى : وهي قولهم : و إن هذه المعية ، إما أن تكون معتبرة بالذات أو بالزمان ، فنقول : هذا بناء على قولكم : إن المدة من لواحق الحركة والتغير ، وهذا عندنا باطل ، بل نقول : الدليل القاطع على بطلانه : أنا تعلم بالضرورة أن الله تعالى في هذا الوقت موجود ، مع حدوث كل حادث ، وذلك يبطل قولكم .

والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : لو كانت العلة موجودة مع المعلول لزم إيجاد الموجود ، فنقول : إن كنت تريد بهذا الكلام إيجاد الموجود مرة أخرى ، فهذا باطل ، وإن كنت تريد كونه موجداً لذلك الموجود بمعنى أنه لولا ذلك الإيجاد ، لما حصل ذلك الموجود ، فهذا حق عندنا ، فلم قلتم : إنه محال ؟

والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إن الرامي يفعل في السهم قوة باقية هي الموجبة لتلك الحركات، إلا أن إيهابها لكل جزء من أجزاء الحركة ، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم. وهذا هو الجواب عن نزول الثقيل . وأما قوله : و الفكر يوجب العلم بالمطلوب ، مع أن الفكر يجب حصوله قبل حصول العلم بالنتيجة ) فنقول : الفكر عبارة عن مجموع العلم بالمقدمتين ، وهما حاصلان مع العلم بالنتيجة .

أما قوله : المؤثر ما لم يوجد بتمامه ، لم يصدر عنه الأثر : فنقول : إن كان المراد من قوله وجدت العلة بتمامها : التقدم الزماني ، فهذا هو عين المطلوب ، وإن كان المراد به التقدم بالذات ، فهو مسلم لكنه لا يقدح في غرضنا . وأما التمسك بما يقال في العرف : حركت يدي فتحرك الكم ، أو ثم تحرك الكم فنقول : هذا تمسك في المضائق العقلية بالألفاظ ، وهو ضعف ، ثم بتقدير الصحة فإنا نحمله على التقدم بالذات .

والعلية والله ولي التوفيق .
The answer to the first objection: Their saying: 'This withness (al-ma'iyya) is either considered in essence or in time.' We say: This is based on your claim that time (al-mudda) is a concomitant of motion and change, which is false in our view. Rather, we say: The decisive proof of its falsity is that we know necessarily that God, exalted, exists at this time, along with the occurrence of every originated thing (hadith), and that invalidates your statement.

The answer to the second objection: Their saying: 'If the cause existed with the effect, creating the existent (ijad al-mawjud) would be necessary.' We say: If by this speech you mean creating the existent a second time, that is false. If you mean that it is an origination (ijad) of that existent in the sense that were it not for that origination, that existent would not exist, then this is true in our view. So why do you say it is impossible?

The answer to the third objection: The archer (al-rami) places in the arrow a remaining power (quwwa baqiya) that is the necessitator of those motions, except that its bestowal upon each part of the motion is conditional upon the passing away of the preceding part. And this is the answer concerning the descent of the heavy object. As for his saying: 'Thought (al-fikr) necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion, while thought must occur before the attainment of knowledge of the result,' we say: Thought is the totality of knowledge of the two premises (muqaddimatayn), and they are both present with the knowledge of the result. As for his saying: 'The cause (al-mu'aththir) as long as it does not exist completely, the effect does not issue from it,' we say: If by 'the cause exists completely' is meant temporal priority (taqaddum zamani), then this is precisely what is sought. If what is meant is priority in essence (taqaddum bi al-dhat), this is granted but does not harm our objective. As for clinging to what is said in common usage: 'I moved my hand, so the sleeve moved,' or 'then the sleeve moved,' we say: This is clinging to words in intellectual difficulties, and it is weak. Then, assuming its validity, we interpret it as priority in essence.

And causality [is established]. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes three objections (shubhat) that opponents might raise against the doctrine that causes must be temporally prior to their effects. The author responds to each in turn.

1. First objection: The opponent argues that the simultaneity ('withness') of cause and effect can be conceived either in essence or in time. If in essence, then temporal priority is not required. The author replies that this objection presupposes that time is a property of motion and change, which is false. The decisive proof is that God exists at the same time as each originated event (hadith) — this shows that time is not restricted to motion, thus undermining the objection's foundation.

2. Second objection: If cause and effect coexist, then creating an already-existing thing would be necessary, which is impossible. The author distinguishes two meanings: if 'creating the existent' means producing it a second time, that is absurd; but if it means that the cause brings the effect from non-existence to existence (even if simultaneous), that is actually true. The opponent's charge of impossibility is baseless.

3. Third objection: This response reframes several arguments. For the archer's arrow: the arrow possesses a remaining power that causes successive motions, but this power is imparted successively conditioned on the previous part's expiration — thus priority is temporal. The same applies to the heavy object's descent. For thought: thought is not a separate entity but the knowledge of the two premises, which coexist with the knowledge of the result — so no temporal priority is needed here. For the complete cause: if 'complete existence' means temporal priority, it confirms the author's position; if it means essential priority, it is admitted but irrelevant. Finally, conventional expressions like 'I moved my hand, so the key moved' are weak proofs in rational matters; even if accepted, they indicate essential priority, not necessarily temporal.

The passage ends with a closing formula affirming the doctrine of causality with divine help.

Key Terms
الشبهة
القوة الباقية
المقدمتين
التقدم الزماني
التقدم بالذات

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the archer's arrow example?
A) The power in the arrow is simultaneous with all motions
B) The power is bestowed conditionally, with temporal precedence
C) The arrow moves without any cause
D) The archer is irrelevant to causality

Key Takeaway
The third objection's sub-arguments are all answered either by showing temporal priority or reinterpreting to essential priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans, do you truly acknowledge that every outcome depends on conditions beyond your control, or do you assume your decisions are the complete cause? How would your reliance on Allah change if you internalized that even your 'remaining powers' are bestowed and conditioned?
?
You often claim 'I did this' or 'I made that happen' — but after reading this passage, can you honestly point to a single action of yours that isn't dependent on prior conditions ceasing and new conditions arising? Will you pause today to thank the One who sustains those conditions moment by moment?
?
The author distinguishes temporal priority from essential priority. Where in your life have you confused the two, attributing to yourself an essential priority that belongs only to Allah? What concrete step can you take today to realign that belief?

Answer to the Second Objection
Refutation of the second objection concerning cause-effect coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that cause and effect cannot coexist by distinguishing between two meanings of 'bringing into existence.'

Core Idea
Cause and effect coexist; the cause does not recreate but originates.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection against the coexistence of cause and effect. Critics argue that if the cause is present with the effect, it would be bringing into existence something already existing, which is absurd. The author clarifies the ambiguity and defends the rational possibility of causal coexistence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'If the cause existed together with the effect, that would necessitate bringing into existence what already exists.' What is the apparent problem?
2
Analyze the author's response
Notice how the author distinguishes between two possible meanings of 'bringing into existence what already exists.' One is re-creation (impossible), the other is original causation (true).
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why this distinction matters for the overall argument about causality. The author is preserving the causal relationship without temporal separation.
This refutes the objection without denying causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: وهي قولهم: (لو كانت العلة موجودة مع المعلول لزم إيجاد الموجود)، فنقول: إن كنت تريد بهذا الكلام إيجاد الموجود مرة أخرى، فهذا باطل، وإن كنت تريد كونه موجداً لذلك الموجود بمعنى أنه لولا ذلك الإيجاد، لما حصل ذلك الموجود، فهذا حق عندنا، فلم قلتم: إنه محال؟
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: إن الرامي يفعل في السهم قوة باقية هي الموجبة لتلك الحركات، إلا أن إيهابها لكل جزء من أجزاء الحركة، مشروط بانقضاء الجزء المتقدم. وهذا هو الجواب عن نزول الثقيل. وأما قوله: (الفكر يوجب العلم بالمطلوب، مع أن الفكر يجب حصوله قبل حصول العلم بالنتيجة) فنقول: الفكر عبارة عن مجموع العلم بالمقدمتين، وهما حاصلان مع العلم بالنتيجة.
أما قوله: (المؤثر ما لم يوجد بتمامه، لم يصدر عنه الأثر): فنقول: إن كان المراد من قوله (وجدت العلة بتمامها): التقدم الزماني، فهذا هو عين المطلوب، وإن كان المراد به التقدم بالذات، فهو مسلم لكنه لا يقدح في غرضنا. وأما التمسك بما يقال في العرف: حركت يدي فتحرك الكم، أو ثم تحرك الكم، فنقول: هذا تمسك في المضائق العقلية بالألفاظ، وهو ضعف، ثم بتقدير الصحة فإنا نحمله على التقدم بالذات.
And the answer to the second objection: which is their saying: 'If the cause existed together with the effect, that would necessitate bringing into existence what already exists,' so we say: If you mean by this speech bringing into existence what already exists a second time, then this is false. And if you mean that it is a bringer into existence of that existent in the sense that were it not for that bringing into existence, that existent would not have come about, then this is true according to us. So why do you say it is impossible?
And the answer to the third objection: The archer produces in the arrow a remaining force that necessitates those motions, except that its bestowal upon each part of the motion is conditioned upon the passing away of the preceding part. And this is the answer concerning the falling of the heavy object. And as for his saying: 'Thought necessitates knowledge of the desired conclusion, even though thought must occur before knowledge of the result occurs,' we say: Thought is an expression for the totality of knowledge of the two premises, and they are present with knowledge of the result.
As for his saying: 'The efficient cause, as long as it is not found in its entirety, does not produce its effect,' we say: If what is meant by his saying 'the cause is found in its entirety' is temporal priority, then this is exactly what is claimed. And if what is meant is priority by essence, then it is conceded but it does not harm our purpose. And as for appealing to what is said customarily: 'I moved my hand and the sleeve moved, or then the sleeve moved,' we say: This is appealing to words in intellectual straits, and it is weak. Then, assuming its validity, we interpret it as priority by essence.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three objections (shubah) related to causality. The author addresses each separately. The first objection claims that if cause and effect coexist, then the cause would be bringing into existence something already existing, which is impossible. The author clarifies: if one means re-creation, it is false; but if one means original creation (that the effect would not exist without the cause), it is true. The second objection concerns continuous motion: an archer imparts a force to the arrow that persists and necessitates motion, contingent upon the moment preceding each part. This answers the example of a falling heavy body. The third objection involves thought and knowledge: thought is the sum of knowledge of the premises, which exist simultaneously with knowledge of the conclusion. Finally, the author dismisses a customary argument about moving a hand and a sleeve, stating that such appeals to language are weak in rational matters; if accepted, they are interpreted as priority by essence.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
إيجاد الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that cause and effect cannot coexist in any sense, leading to a denial of simultaneous causation.
Correction: The passage shows that cause and effect can coexist if 'bringing into existence' is understood as original causation, not re-creation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which interpretation of 'bringing into existence what already exists' is true?
A) Re-creating an existing thing.
B) Original causation that without it the effect would not exist.
C) Both interpretations are true.
D) Neither interpretation is true.

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of cause and effect is rationally sound if 'bringing into existence' means original causation, not re-creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection seemed logically sound until the author distinguished two meanings. Are there thoughts or doubts you have about God's actions that might rest on a similar confusion of terms? What would it take for you to examine the meaning behind your objections rather than dismissing a truth as impossible?
?
The author accepts that cause and effect coexist, meaning every moment of your existence depends on God's original creative act. Do you truly live as if your existence at every instant is a direct gift from God, or do you take it for granted as something that simply 'is'? How would your prayer and gratitude change if you internalized this?

Answer to the Third Objection: Continuous Motion
Explanation of continuous motion through a persisting force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author explains continuous motion by positing a persisting force bestowed in parts.

Core Idea
Continuous motion results from a persisting force applied to each part conditionally.

The third objection challenges the possibility of continuous motion without a continuous cause. The author uses the example of an archer shooting an arrow to illustrate how a single force can produce a series of motions, each part conditioned on the previous one's passing. This also explains the falling of a heavy object.

Guided Path
1
Read the archer example
The author says the archer produces in the arrow a remaining force that necessitates the motions. This force is bestowed on each part of the motion conditioned on the passing of the preceding part.
This is the key idea.
2
Connect to the falling heavy object
The author explicitly says this is the answer for the falling heavy object as well. Think about how gravity might be analogous.
3
Analyze the objection being answered
What exactly is the objection? Likely that each part of motion requires a new cause, but the author shows one force suffices if applied in parts.

Source Text

Answer to the Third Objection: Thought, Cause, and Custom
Refutation of three sub-objections under the third objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes objections concerning thought causing knowledge, the condition of the cause's completeness, and the hand-sleeve custom.

Core Idea
Thought is the sum of premises present with the conclusion; the cause's entirety means essential priority, not temporal.

The author continues answering the third objection by addressing three additional issues. First, the relationship between thought and knowledge: thought is the integration of premises that coexist with the conclusion, not a separate cause. Second, the statement about the cause not being found in its entirety: it either means temporal priority (irrelevant) or essential priority (conceded). Third, appealing to customary phrases like 'I moved my hand and the sleeve moved' is weak in rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the thought-knowledge argument
The objector says thought must precede knowledge. The author responds that thought is the knowledge of premises, which exist with the conclusion.
2
Examine the cause's entirety
The objector says the cause must exist in its entirety before the effect. The author distinguishes temporal priority from essential priority.
3
Critique the hand-sleeve custom
The author dismisses appealing to customary language as weak in rational matters, but if accepted, interprets it as essential priority.

Misconception & Correction

Refutation of Circularity (Dawr)
The author refutes circularity via self-priority absurdity and begins addressing an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that refutes circular causation by showing it leads to a thing being prior to itself.

Core Idea
Circular causation forces a thing to be prior to itself, which is impossible.

This passage presents a classic Kalam argument against the doctrine of circular causation (dawr). The argument establishes that causality entails priority, and circularity would entail a thing being prior to itself, an absurdity. An objection is then raised about the meaning of 'priority' — whether temporal or essential/causal — which the author begins to address.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence in the answer to the objection.
2
Trace the proof of impossibility
Identify the premise that cause is prior to effect. Then follow the reduction: if each is cause of the other, each is prior to the other, leading to A being prior to what is prior to A, hence A prior to itself.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3

The objector questions what 'priority' means. Distinguish between temporal priority (impossible) and essential/causal priority (which reduces to causality itself).
The author's full response is not available due to the cut-off.

The ninth section on refuting the doctrine of circularity (dawr). They argued for the corruption of the doctrine of circularity by saying: it is established that the cause (al-‘illa) is prior to the effect (al-ma‘lūl). So if each one were a cause of the other, then each one would be prior to the other. And if this is prior to that, and that is prior to this, then it would follow for this that it is prior to what is prior to itself. And what is prior to what is prior to a thing must be prior to that thing. So it follows that a single thing is prior to itself, which is impossible. Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of circular causation (dawr). It begins by labeling itself as the ninth section on refuting circularity. The proof is presented: it is established that the cause (al-‘illa) is prior to the effect (al-ma‘lūl). If two things were causes of each other, each would be prior to the other. Then if A is prior to B and B is prior to A, then A would be prior to what is prior to itself (B being prior to A). Since whatever is prior to what is prior to a thing must be prior to that thing itself, A would be prior to itself, which is absurd. Therefore circularity is impossible. An objection is then raised: what is meant by 'priority'? If temporal priority, the objection claims the proof fails because temporal priority is impossible or invalid. If essential or causal priority, the objection notes that this is a philosophical concept that needs examination. The objector asks whether 'causal priority' means the cause being an efficient cause and necessitator, or something else. If it means simply that the cause is a cause of the effect, then the notion of priority reduces to causality itself, and the argument's conclusion might be affected. The text cuts off during this response.

Proof that Circular Causality is Impossible
The author presents a proof that circular causation leads to a logical contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof against circular causality (al-dawr) and why it is considered impossible in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Circular causality leads to a logical contradiction: a thing cannot be prior to itself.

The concept of circular causality (al-dawr) is often debated in theological discussions about creation and causation. In this passage, the author presents a concise logical proof to demonstrate why circular causality is impossible. By following the steps of this argument, you will see how a seemingly plausible idea collapses under logical scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key premise
The proof begins by stating a fundamental principle: the cause is prior to the effect. This priority is logical, not merely temporal.
2
Assume circular causality
Suppose two things, A and B, are each the cause of the other. This is the scenario of al-dawr.
3
Apply the premise to both directions
Since A is cause of B, A is prior to B. Since B is cause of A, B is prior to A.
4
Use transitivity
If A is prior to B, and B is prior to A, then by transitivity A is prior to itself.
5
Recognize the impossibility
A thing cannot be prior to itself. Thus the assumption of circular causality leads to a contradiction, proving it impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على فساد القول بالدور، بأن قالوا: ثبت أن العلة متقدمة على المعلول، فلو كان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على الآخر، وإذا كان هذا متقدما على ذاك، وكان ذلك متقدما على هذا، لزم في هذا كونه متقدما على المتقدم على نفسه، والمتقدم على المتقدم على الشيء يجب كونه متقدما على ذلك الشيء، فيلزم كون الشيء الواحد متقدما على نفسه وهو محال.
They argued for the corruption of the claim of circularity by saying: It is established that the cause is prior to the effect. So if each of them were a cause of the other, then each of them would be prior to the other. And if this is prior to that, and that is prior to this, then it follows for this that it is prior to that which is prior to itself. And that which is prior to that which is prior to a thing must be prior to that thing. Thus it follows that one and the same thing is prior to itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the possibility of circular causality (al-dawr). The author begins by stating the premise that the cause is prior to the effect. If we have two things, A and B, and each is a cause of the other, then A must be prior to B and B must be prior to A. But if A is prior to B, and B is prior to A, then by transitivity A is prior to itself. Since self-priority is impossible, circular causality is impossible. The argument is a classic logical reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
دور
علة
معلول
تقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular causality is impossible because of temporal contradiction.
Correction: The proof relies on logical priority, not temporal. Even if time is involved, the logical priority of cause over effect is the key premise.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise used in the proof against circular causality?
A) The cause must be simultaneous with the effect.
B) The cause is prior to the effect.
C) The effect is prior to the cause.
D) Time is illusory.

Key Takeaway
Circular causality is impossible because it leads to the self-contradictory conclusion that a thing is prior to itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that a thing cannot be prior to itself. Consider your own life: Do you ever treat a created thing as if it were the ultimate cause of your happiness or success, forgetting that only Allah is the First Cause? How does this logical principle expose hidden shirk in your heart? Commit to identifying one dependency you have that is circular—something you rely on that ultimately relies on you—and break that pattern by redirecting your reliance to Allah alone.

The proof shows that assuming circular causality leads to a contradiction. In your intellectual life, do you ever accept arguments that are circular, maybe in justifying your own actions? Are you willing to apply the same logical scrutiny to your own beliefs as the author applies here? Pause and examine one area where you might be reasoning in a circle. Resolve to seek evidence outside yourself, just as the proof demands an external cause.

Analyzing the Meaning of Causal Priority
Objection and response on the meaning of causal priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical analysis of causal priority and why the author concludes that 'priority by causality' is identical to causality itself.

Core Idea
Causal priority is not a separate concept; it is nothing but the causal relation itself.

This passage tackles a subtle objection against the proof of impossibility of circular causation. The objector questions what 'priority' means. The author dissects the concept, showing that either priority reduces to causality or it is unintelligible. Through this, the logical foundation of the proof is reaffirmed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objector's dilemma
The objector asks whether 'the cause is prior to the effect' means temporal priority or essential/causal priority. If temporal, it's impossible; if essential, the meaning needs clarification.
Notice how the objector tries to trap the author in a dichotomy.
2
Examine the objector's clarification
The objector argues that priority by causality is distinct from both temporal priority and causality itself, using the example of a moving finger and a ring moving simultaneously but with causal order.
This is the key move to distinguish priority as a separate concept.
3
Grasp the author's response
The author replies that the intellectual order (priority) is exactly the causal relation itself. There is no additional concept of 'priority' beyond the cause being a cause.
The author uses the same example to show that reason sees only causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For it is said: What do you mean by your statement: 'The cause is prior to the effect'? If you mean temporal priority, this is invalid, for we have shown that such priority is impossible. Also, we have shown that if temporal priority were valid, it would invalidate this proof. But if you mean priority by essence and causality. Then we say: The claim of establishing priority by essence and causality is a statement that occurred among the philosophers, so it must be examined. Then we say: Do you mean by priority by causality that this is effective in that and a cause for it and necessitating it, or do you mean something other than this effect? If the first is intended, then your statement 'The cause is prior to the effect' means that the cause is a cause for the effect, and then there remains no concept for this priority other than causality. On this estimation, your statement: 'If each of them were a cause of the other, then each of them would be a cause of the other' would be such that there is no difference at all between the premise of this conditional and its consequent, so it is corrupt speech. But if you mean by priority a concept other than causality and effectivity, then it must be explained, for it is unintelligible. For they said: 'Priority by causality has a concept distinct from temporal priority and from causality itself. As for being distinct from temporal priority, because when you moved your finger, the ring moved, and it is impossible for the movement of the finger to be temporally prior to the movement of the ring, otherwise interpenetration of two bodies would be necessary, which is impossible. So here priority by causality occurred without temporal priority, so the difference is manifest. As for showing that priority by causality is a concept distinct from causality itself: We say: Reason is decisive that unless the existence of the cause is completed in itself, it is impossible for the effect to issue from it. And it is known that the attainment of the cause in its entirety is distinct from its being a cause for that effect. This indicates that priority by causality is distinct from that causality itself.' We say: In answer to this question: There is no dispute that the movement of the finger and the movement of the ring existed together, and no dispute that reason judges an order of one to the other, but we say: Why is it not permissible for that order to be exactly causality and effect? For reason judged that the movement of the finger is effective in the movement of the ring, and that the movement of the ring results from the movement of the finger. However, this meaning we have mentioned is nothing but causality, dependency, and effect, and there is no concept beyond that. So it becomes clear from this explanation we have summarized: That in reason there is nothing obtained from priority by causality except that very causality. And when this is established, we say: Your statement: 'If each of them were a cause for the existence of the other, then each of them would be a cause for the other' means: If each were...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response concerning the meaning of 'priority' (al-taqaddum) in the proof against circular causality. The objector asks: Does 'the cause is prior to the effect' mean temporal priority or priority by essence/causality? If temporal, the objector claims it is impossible and would undermine the proof. If essential, the objector challenges the concept. The author responds by analyzing priority by causality. First, he argues that if priority by causality is nothing but causality itself, then the conditional in the proof becomes tautological ('if A is cause of B, then A is cause of B'), which is trivial. Alternatively, if it is a separate concept, it is unintelligible. The objector then tries to show that priority by causality is distinct from both temporal priority and causality itself, using the example of finger and ring moving together but with causal order. The author finally refutes this, asserting that the intellectual order is nothing but the causal relation itself, so no extra concept of priority exists. Thus, the original proof against circularity is upheld.

Causal Priority is Identical to Causality
Opponent argues for distinct causal priority; author refutes by identifying it with causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that causal priority is not a distinct concept from causality itself, but rather the same relation of effecting and dependence.

Core Idea
Causal priority is nothing but the causality relation itself.

The opponent attempts to distinguish causal priority from both temporal priority and causality, using the example of a finger moving a ring simultaneously. However, the author argues that this supposed priority is simply the causality and effecting that the intellect perceives. This lesson examines the opponent's distinction and the author's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
The opponent first says causal priority is different from temporal priority (example of finger and ring), then argues it is different from causality itself (completeness of cause). Read these carefully.
2
Identify the author's response
The author agrees on the simultaneity but questions why the priority cannot be simply the causality and effecting. He says the intellect grasps nothing beyond causality, dependence, and effecting.
3
Conclude with the author's result
The author concludes that nothing is obtained from causal priority except the very causality. This refutes the opponent's claim of a distinct concept.
