فإن قالوا : التقدم بالعلية له مفهوم مغاير للتقدم الزماني ولنفس العلية، أما أنه مغاير للتقدم بالزمان، فلأنك حين حركت إصبعك فقد تحرك الخاتم، ويمتنع أن تكون حركة الإصبع متقدمة بالزمان على حركة الخاتم، وإلا لزم تداخل الجسمين، وهو محال، فههنا قد حصل التقدم بالعلية، ولم يحصل التقدم بالزمان، فقد ظهر التغاير، وأما بيان أن التقدم بالعلية مفهوم مغاير لنفس العلية. فنقول : العقل قاطع بأنه ما لم يتم وجود العلة في نفسها، فإنه يستحيل أن يصدر عنها المعلول. ومعلوم : أن حصول العلة بتمامها أمر مغاير لكونها علة لذلك المعلول، وهذا يدل على أن التقدم بالعلية أمر مغاير لنفس تلك العلية. فنقول : في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : لا نزاع في أن حركة الإصبع وحركة الخاتم وجدتا معا، ولا نزاع في أن العقل يقضي بترتيب أحدهما على الآخر، إلا أنا نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الترتيب هو نفس العلية والتأثير ؟ فإن العقل حكم بأن حركة الإصبع مؤثرة في حركة الخاتم، وأن حركة الخاتم حاصلة عن حركة الإصبع، إلا أن هذا المعنى الذي ذكرناه ليس إلا لنفس العلية والافتقار والتأثير، وليس ههنا مفهوم سوى ذلك. فظهر بهذا البيان الذي لخصناه : أنه لا يحصل عند العقل من التقدم بالعلية إلا نفس تلك العلية، وإذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : قولكم : لو كان كل واحد منهما علة لوجود الآخر، لكان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر، فيصير معناه : لو كان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر، لكان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر، فيصير هذا إلزاماً للشيء على نفسه، وهو فاسد. وأيضاً : فلأن القول يكون كل واحد منهما علة للآخر، إن كان معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة، فحينئذ لا حاجة إلى ذكر هذا الدليل، وإن كان محتاجا إلى الإثبات بالحجة والبينة لم يكن الكلام الذي ذكروه مفيدا فائدة، لأنه لم يحصل فيه إلا تبديل اللفظ باللفظ، ومعلوم أنه لا يسمن ولا يغني من جوع.
If they say: 'Causal priority (al-taqaddum bi-al-‘illiyyah) has a meaning that differs from temporal priority and from causality itself. As for its being different from temporal priority, it is because when you moved your finger, the ring moved, and it is impossible that the movement of the finger be temporally prior to the movement of the ring, otherwise the interpenetration of the two bodies would follow, which is impossible. So here causal priority occurred without temporal priority, so the difference appears. As for the demonstration that causal priority is a meaning different from causality itself, we say: The intellect is certain that unless the cause’s existence is complete in itself, it is impossible for the effect to proceed from it. And it is known that the attainment of the cause in its completeness is a matter different from its being a cause for that effect, and this indicates that causal priority is a matter different from that causality itself.' We say in answer to this question: There is no dispute that the movement of the finger and the movement of the ring occurred together, and there is no dispute that the intellect judges a priority of one over the other. However, we say: Why is it not permissible for that priority to be the very causality and effecting (al-ta’thīr)? For the intellect judges that the movement of the finger is effective in the movement of the ring, and that the movement of the ring results from the movement of the finger. But this meaning we have mentioned is nothing other than the very causality, dependence (al-iftiqār), and effecting, and there is no meaning beyond that. So this explanation we have summarized shows that nothing is obtained in the intellect from causal priority except that very causality. And when this is established, we say: Your statement 'If each of them were a cause for the existence of the other, then each of them would be a cause for the other' – its meaning becomes: 'If each of them were a cause for the other, then each of them would be a cause for the other', so this is an imposition of the thing upon itself, which is invalid. Also, because the statement that each of them is a cause for the other – if its impossibility is known by intuition, then there is no need to mention this argument; and if it needs proof and evidence, then the statement they mentioned is of no benefit, for it only replaces a word with a word, and it is well known that it does not satisfy or avail against hunger.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a conclusion. The opponent first argues that causal priority (al-taqaddum bi-al-‘illiyyah) is a concept distinct from both temporal priority and the very causality (al-‘illiyyah). To prove its distinction from temporal priority, they give the example of moving a finger that moves a ring: the finger movement is causally prior but not temporally prior, since both occur simultaneously. Then they argue that causal priority is distinct from causality itself because the cause must be complete in itself before the effect follows, and completeness is different from being a cause. The author replies that there is no dispute about simultaneity or the intellect’s judgment of priority, but the question is: why can that priority not be simply the causality and effecting? The intellect judges that the finger movement is effective and causes the ring movement, and this is nothing beyond causality, dependence, and effecting. Therefore, causal priority is nothing but causality itself. Building on that, the author then analyses the opponent’s claim that circular causation (dawr) is invalid because each would be a cause for the other. He shows that the argument reduces to an empty tautology (repeating the same thing), which is logically pointless. Moreover, if the impossibility is self-evident, no argument is needed; if not, the argument merely swaps words. Thus the opponent’s reasoning is futile.

Key Terms
التقدم بالعلية (al-taqaddum bi-al-‘illiyyah: causal priority)
العلية (al-‘illiyyah: causality)
التأثير (al-ta’thīr: effecting)
الافتقار (al-iftiqār: dependence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think causal priority is a real additional relation beyond the causality relation.
Correction: The author demonstrates that causal priority is identical to the causality, dependence, and effecting relation, not a separate concept.

Checkpoint
Which example does the opponent use to show that causal priority is not temporal priority?
A) A key turning a lock
B) A finger moving a ring
C) A fire burning wood
D) A father and son

Key Takeaway
Causal priority is not a distinct concept; it is identical to the causality relation itself.

The Tautology in the Circular Causation Argument
Author criticizes opponent's circular causation argument as tautological and useless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the opponent's argument for the impossibility of circular causation as an empty tautology.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument reduces to saying 'if X then X'—a useless repetition.

Having established that causal priority is nothing but causality, the author turns to the opponent's claim that circular causation (dawr) is impossible because each would be a cause for the other. The author shows that this argument merely repeats itself and is either self-evident or useless. This lesson analyzes that logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says: if each of two things were a cause for the other, then each would be a cause for the other—implying impossibility. Read the author's citation.
2
See the tautology exposed
The author points out that the statement is exactly the same: 'If each is a cause of the other, then each is a cause of the other.' This is an identity, not a proof.
3
Evaluate the alternative
The author adds that if the impossibility is self-evident, no argument is needed; if not, the argument just swaps words, providing no real evidence.

Refuting the 'Cause of a Cause' Argument
Second argument against circular causation via 'cause of a cause' and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that 'the cause of a cause is a cause' does not prove circular causation impossible, because it is either contradictory or trivial.

Core Idea
A seemingly logical premise can be either false or useless in argument.

This passage dissects a second argument against circular causation (dawr). The argument hinges on a commonly accepted premise: 'the cause of a cause of a thing is a cause for that thing.' The author shows that this premise, when applied to a circular causal chain, leads to either a contradiction or a meaningless tautology. By carefully distinguishing two possible interpretations, he exposes the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument presented
First, read the initial argument: if A causes B and B causes A, then A causes itself because A is the cause of its own cause. Notice how it uses the premise 'cause of a cause is a cause.'
This is a common but flawed line of reasoning.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author splits the premise into two meanings: (1) the cause of a cause is an immediate efficient cause of the thing, and (2) it is merely a cause for the intermediate cause. Keep these distinct.
The first leads to contradiction, the second to triviality.
3
Understand the contradiction
If the cause of a cause is an immediate cause, then it cannot also be a cause for the intermediate cause, since an immediate cause does not cause another cause. Thus the premise becomes self-contradictory.
4
Understand the triviality
If the premise only means the cause of a cause is a cause for the cause itself, then it merely repeats the idea without proving anything. It is idle talk.
No real inference can be drawn.
5
Draw the conclusion
The argument fails: either its premise is contradictory (so invalid) or trivially true (so useless). Therefore circular causation is not refuted by this argument.
This passage sharpens skills in analyzing logical premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على فساد الدور: قالوا: لو كان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر، لكان كل واحد منهما علة لعلة نفسه، وعلة العلة علة، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما علة لنفسه، وذلك محال. ولقائل أن يقول: مدار هذه الحجة على قولهم: إن علة علة الشيء علة لذلك الشيء [وهذا كلام مبهم يجب البحث عنه] فيقال: إن أردتم بقولكم: علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة لذلك الشيء [وعجب؟] كونها مؤثرة في ذلك الشيء وموجدة له، فهذا باطل قطعا، لأن على هذا التقدير تكون علة العلة علة قريبة للشيء، وكونها علة قريبة لذلك الشيء [يمنع من كونها علة للعلة]، فقولكم: علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة قريبة لذلك الشيء كلام متناقض. وإن أردتم به أنها علة لعلة الشيء، فهذا مسلم، إلا أنه يرجع حاصل الكلام فيه إلى أن علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة لعلة ذلك الشيء، ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام عبث ولا فائدة فيه.
The second argument against the invalidity of circular causation: They said: If each of them were a cause for the other, then each of them would be a cause for the cause of itself, and the cause of a cause is a cause, so it would follow that each of them is a cause for itself, and that is impossible. And it is possible for someone to say: This argument revolves around their statement: 'The cause of a cause of a thing is a cause for that thing' – and this is an ambiguous statement that must be investigated. So it is said: If you mean by your statement 'The cause of a cause of a thing must be a cause for that thing' – and it is [questionable?] that it is effective in that thing and brings it into existence – then this is absolutely false, because on this assumption the cause of a cause would be an immediate cause (illah qarībah) for the thing, and its being an immediate cause for that thing prevents it from being a cause for the cause. So your statement 'The cause of a cause of a thing must be an immediate cause for that thing' is contradictory. And if you mean by it that it is a cause for the cause of the thing, then this is granted, but the net result of the statement is that 'the cause of a cause of a thing must be a cause for the cause of that thing', and it is obvious that this statement is idle talk and of no benefit.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a second argument against circular causation (dawr). The argument claims that if A causes B and B causes A, then A would be the cause of its own cause, and since the cause of a cause is a cause, A would be the cause of itself, which is impossible. The author first notes that this argument depends on the premise that 'the cause of a cause of a thing is a cause for that thing'. He then examines two possible interpretations of this premise. First, if they mean that the cause of a cause is an efficient cause (immediate cause) of the thing, this is false because then the cause of a cause would be an immediate cause of the thing, which actually prevents it from being a cause for the intermediate cause (since an immediate cause does not cause another cause). Thus the statement becomes contradictory. Second, if they mean it is a cause for the cause itself, the statement is trivially true but merely repeats itself, providing no real proof. Hence the argument is either false or useless.

Key Terms
الدور (al-dawr: circular causation)
علة العلة (illat al-illah: cause of a cause)
علة قريبة (illah qarībah: immediate cause)
متناقض (mutanāqiḍ: contradictory)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'cause of a cause is a cause' is always straightforwardly true and sufficient to prove impossibility of circular causation.
Correction: The premise is ambiguous: if interpreted as immediate efficient causation it is contradictory, if interpreted as mere causation of the cause it is tautological and proves nothing.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the first interpretation (cause of a cause as immediate cause) is contradictory?
A) Because it would make the cause of a cause an immediate cause of the thing, preventing it from being a cause for the intermediate cause.
B) Because immediate causes cannot exist in causal chains.
C) Because the opponent explicitly rejects immediate causation.
D) Because the argument assumes temporal priority.

Key Takeaway
The claim 'cause of a cause is a cause' is ambiguous; when applied to circular causation it leads either to contradiction or triviality, so it does not refute dawr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief using a phrase that sounded convincing but you never examined its exact meaning? Like 'cause of a cause is a cause'? This lesson shows how ambiguity can hide falsehood. Will you commit to defining your terms clearly before arguing for your faith, starting today?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that seems to disprove an Islamic belief, do you accept it quickly or do you patiently scrutinize its premises? This lesson reveals that even a clever argument can be built on an ambiguous foundation. Pray that Allah grants you the discernment to detect such flaws.
?
The opponent's argument failed because it tried to use a general rule in an improper context. In your own life, are there general statements you apply without considering the context? Reflect on one area where you need to be more precise in your reasoning about Allah's attributes.

Second Proof Against Circular Causation: Objection and Resolution
Presents a proof against circular causation and responds to a potential objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of a second proof against circular causation and how a potential objection is resolved by clarifying the ambiguous premise.

Core Idea
Circular causation makes each thing the cause of itself — an absurdity.

This passage presents a second, more refined proof against circular causation in causal chains. After stating the proof, the author anticipates an objection that targets its key premise, and then offers a careful clarification that both refutes the objection and reinforces the proof's validity. Understanding this dialectic sharpens one's grasp of causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
The proof argues that if A causes B and B causes A, then A becomes the cause of its own cause, leading to self-causation. Identify the logical steps.
Notice how the argument reduces circular causation to self-causation.
2
Identify the ambiguous premise
The proof relies on the claim that 'the cause of a cause is a cause.' The objector argues this premise is ambiguous and needs clarification.
This ambiguity is the crux of the objection.
3
Understand the two interpretations
The response distinguishes two meanings: (1) immediate cause (directly producing the effect), and (2) cause of the intermediate cause (remote cause). Each leads to a different conclusion.
The first interpretation leads to contradiction; the second is trivial.
4
See how the objection is resolved
Under the immediate cause interpretation, the premise becomes contradictory; under the trivial interpretation, it is useless. Thus the objection fails and the proof stands.
The resolution shows the proof's premise is valid only when properly understood.

الحجة الثانية على فساد الدور : قالوا : لو كان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر ، لكان كل واحد منهما علة لعلة نفسه ، وعلة العلة علة ، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما علة لنفسه ، وذلك محال . ولقائل أن يقول : مدار هذه الحجة على قولهم : إن علة علة الشيء علة لذلك الشيء ، وهذا كلام مبهم يجب البحث عنه . فيقال : إن أردتم بقولكم : علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة لذلك الشيء وعجب كونها مؤثرة في ذلك الشيء وموجدة له ، فهذا باطل قطعا ، لأن على هذا التقدير تكون علة العلة علة قريبة للشيء ، وكونها علة قريبة لذلك الشيء يمنع من كونها علة للعلة ، فقولكم : علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة قريبة لذلك الشيء كلام متناقض . وإن أردتم به أنها علة لعلة الشيء ، فهذا مسلم ، إلا أنه يرجع حاصل الكلام فيه إلى أن علة علة الشيء يجب كونها علة لعلة ذلك الشيء ، ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام عبث ولا فائدة فيه
The second proof against the invalidity of circularity: They said: If each of the two were a cause of the other, then each of the two would be a cause of the cause of itself, and the cause of the cause is a cause, so it would follow that each of the two is a cause of itself, and that is impossible. And someone might say: The basis of this argument is their statement that the cause of the cause of a thing is a cause of that thing, and this is ambiguous speech that must be investigated. So it is said: If by your statement 'the cause of the cause of a thing must be a cause of that thing' you mean that it is effective in that thing and brings it into existence, then this is absolutely false, because on this assumption the cause of the cause would be an immediate cause of the thing, and its being an immediate cause of that thing prevents it from being a cause of the cause, so your statement 'the cause of the cause of a thing must be an immediate cause of that thing' is contradictory. And if by it you mean that it is a cause of the cause of the thing, then this is granted, except that the upshot of the speech is that the cause of the cause of a thing must be a cause of the cause of that thing, and it is known that this speech is frivolous and has no benefit.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof against the logical impossibility of circular causation (dawr). The proof argues that if each of two things is the cause of the other, then each becomes the cause of its own cause, and since the cause of a cause is a cause, each is the cause of itself—an obvious absurdity. Then an objection is raised: the proof relies on the premise that the cause of the cause of something is a cause of that thing, but this premise is ambiguous. The response clarifies: if the premise means the cause of the cause is an immediate cause of the thing, then it is false because then the cause of the cause would be both an immediate cause of the thing (preventing it from being a cause of the cause) and also a cause of the cause—a contradiction. If the premise merely means the cause of the cause is a cause of the cause (i.e., of the intermediate cause), then it is trivially true but useless. Thus the objection fails and the proof stands.

Key Terms
دور
علة
علة العلة
علة قريبة
متناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise 'the cause of a cause is a cause' is always self-evident and unambiguous, so the proof is unassailable.
Correction: The premise is ambiguous: it can mean an immediate cause (which would be contradictory) or a remote cause (which is trivial). The proof works only under the proper interpretation (remote cause).

Checkpoint
What does the second proof claim follows from two things being causes of each other?
A) Each is the cause of the other's effect.
B) Each is the cause of itself.
C) Each is the immediate cause of the other.
D) The causal chain is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The second proof against circular causation is strengthened by clarifying that the cause of a cause cannot be an immediate cause of the original effect without contradiction.

The objector tried to dismantle the proof by highlighting a potential flaw, but the author showed that careful analysis only strengthens the argument. ?
If you truly accept that nothing can be the cause of itself, then your very existence is a gift from another. How would your life change if you lived every moment conscious that you are not self-sufficient? What would it mean for your daily choices to reflect total dependence on the Creator who brought you from nothing?

Circular Causation Refuted by Self-Need Contradiction
Presents a stronger proof against circularity based on need (iftiqar) with two sub-proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mutual causal dependence between two things leads to the logical impossibility of self-need.

Core Idea
Circular causation collapses because it forces a thing to be in need of itself.

This passage presents what the author considers a stronger argument against circular causation. Instead of relying on infinite regress, it focuses on the concept of need (iftiqar). By analyzing what it means for one thing to be the cause of another, the argument shows that mutual causation leads to an absurdity: a thing needing itself. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Trace the logical chain to self-need
Follow the argument: if each of two things is cause of the other, then each needs the other. By transitivity of need, each would need itself.
This step uses the principle: if A needs B and B needs C, then A needs C.
3
Examine the first impossibility
The first reason self-need is impossible: it combines need and not-need in the same entity in the same respect, a contradiction.
Needed things are not in need of the needy; self-need violates this.
4
Examine the second impossibility
The second reason: need is a relation that requires two distinct relata. One thing cannot have a relation with itself.
A relation requires at least two distinct things.
5
Conclude the proof
Since self-need is impossible, the initial assumption of circular causation must be false.
This proof is considered stronger than infinite regress arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهي الأقوى ، أن يقال : لو كان كل واحد منهما علة للآخر ، لكان كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى الآخر ، والمفتقر إلى المفتقر إلى الشيء ، يجب كونه مفتقرا إلى ذلك الشيء فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، وذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : إن المفتقر إلى الشيء محتاج إليه ، والمفتقر إليه غير محتاج إلى المفتقر ، فلو كان الشيء الواحد مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة الواحدة ، محتاجا وغنيا ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . الثاني : وهو أن الافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة مخصوصة ، بين المفتقر والمفتقر إليه ، والنسبة لا يمكن حصولها إلا بين أمرين ، فالأمر الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ، يمتنع حصول النسبة فيه
The second proof, and it is the stronger: It is said: If each of the two were a cause of the other, then each of the two would be in need of the other, and that which is in need of that which is in need of a thing must be in need of that thing, so it would follow that each of the two is in need of itself, and that is impossible for two reasons: First: That which is in need of a thing is in need of it, and that which is needed is not in need of that which is in need, so if one thing were in need of itself, it would follow that the same thing, in the same respect, is both in need and wealthy [not in need], and that is a combination of two contradictories, which is impossible. Second: Need of a thing is a specific relation between the one in need and the thing needed, and a relation cannot occur except between two things, so for one thing under one consideration, it is impossible for the relation to occur.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a stronger proof against circular causation, based on the concept of need (iftiqar). The argument: if each of two things is the cause of the other, then each is in need of the other. Moreover, if A is in need of B, and B is in need of C, then A is in need of C. Applying this to circularity: if X is in need of Y and Y is in need of X, then X is in need of X (since X is in need of Y and Y is in need of X, so X is in need of X). This self-need is impossible for two reasons. First, need implies that the needed is not in need of the one in need; but self-need would require the same entity to be both in need (requiring) and not in need (required), a contradiction. Second, need is a relation between two distinct entities; one entity cannot have a relation with itself. Thus circular causation is absurd.

Key Terms
مفتقر
محتاج
غني
نسبة
نقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular causation is only problematic because it leads to infinite regress.
Correction: This proof shows a more direct absurdity: self-need is logically impossible regardless of regress.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason self-need is impossible?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It combines need and not-need in the same entity.
C) It requires a cause external to the series.
D) It violates the law of non-contradiction in a different way.

Key Takeaway
Circular causation is refuted because it leads to the absurdity of a thing being in need of itself, which is impossible both logically and relationally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this proof expose the impossibility of such contradictions in your own life? What step will you take today to align your actions with the truth of your complete need for Him?
?
The proof shows that self-need is impossible because a thing cannot be both needy and not needy in the same respect. In your relationship with Allah, do you recognize that you are entirely needy in every respect? Is there any area—your wealth, your intelligence, your status—where you subtly claim independence? How will you let go of that illusion today?
?
This argument uses pure logic to dismantle circular reasoning. Are you willing to apply the same logical scrutiny to your own assumptions about God, even when they feel comfortable? What is one belief you hold about Allah that you have never subjected to rigorous logical examination? Will you examine it now using the tools of reason He gave you?

Objections to the Impossibility of Circularity and the Answer to the First
Lists three objections to circularity's impossibility and answers the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments for the possibility of circularity and how the author refutes the first argument by rejecting the existence of matter and form as separate principles.

Core Idea
Circularity arguments often rely on faulty premises; rejecting those premises dissolves the circularity.

This passage presents three objections from those who claim circularity is not impossible. The objections are based on hylomorphism, relational terms, and the nature of causality. The author answers the first by denying the existence of matter and form as understood by the opponents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and note the three distinct objections being raised. They are marked by 'الأول', 'الثاني', and 'الثالث'.
The objections are: matter/form and substance/accident; relatives; causality and being-caused.
2
Understand the answer's scope
Notice that the answer only addresses the first objection. The author says 'We do not affirm matter and form.' This is a direct rebuttal of the premise.
The other two objections are not answered here; they may be addressed later.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why the author chooses to deny the ontological framework rather than accept it and argue differently. This shows a common strategy in kalam: rejecting background assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال : الدور غير ممتنع بوجوه : الأول : إن الهيولي والصورة كل واحد منهما محتاج إلى الآخر ، وهو دور ، والجوهر والعرض يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، وهو دور . الثاني : إن المضافين يتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر وهو دور ، ولا يقال : إن وجوب مقارنتهما لأجل أن العلة الواحدة موجبة لهما معا ، لأنا نقول : هذا لا يصح على قول الفلاسفة ، لأن عندهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد . الثالث : إن العلية والمعلولية من باب المضاف، والمضافان يوجدان معا ، فالعلية والمعلولية يوجبان المعية ، والمعية تنافى حصول التقدم ، فوجب أن يمتنع حصول التقدم بين العلة وبين المعلول . والجواب عن الأول : إنا لا نقول بالهيولي والصورة. أما الجسم فإنه مستلزم للحصول في الحيز ، إلا أن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالملازمة بينها إنما حصلت بينهما لأن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق
And those who said that circularity is not impossible argued with [several] points: First: Matter and form are each in need of the other, and that is circular; and substance and accident are each in need of the other, and that is circular. Second: Relative terms depend on each other, and that is circular, and it cannot be said that the necessity of their concomitance is because one cause necessitates both together, for we say: This is not valid according to the philosophers, because for them one does not proceed from the One except one. Third: Causality and being caused belong to the category of relatives, and relatives exist together, so causality and being caused necessitate concomitance, and concomitance contradicts the existence of priority, so it is necessary that the existence of priority between cause and effect is impossible. And the answer to the first: We do not affirm matter and form. As for body, it necessitates obtaining in space, except that the essence of body necessitates obtaining in absolute space, and on this estimation the concomitance between them occurred only because the essence of body necessitates obtaining in absolute space.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents three objections raised by proponents who claim circularity is not impossible. First objection: In hylomorphism, matter and form are mutually dependent—a circular relation; similarly, substance and accident. Second objection: Relative terms (e.g., father and son) are interdependent, constituting a circle; the objection anticipates the reply that they are caused by a single cause, but counters that for philosophers the One cause only produces one effect. Third objection: Causality and being-caused are relatives that coexist simultaneously, so priority between cause and effect is impossible. The answer focuses on the first objection: the author denies the existence of matter and form (hylomorphism) as understood by the opponents. For body, it simply necessitates occupying space, and the concomitance between body and space is due to the essence of body itself, not a circular dependency. The other two objections are not answered in this passage.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
جوهر
عرض
مضاف
علية
معلولية
معية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that everyday examples of interdependence (like father-son) prove circularity is possible.
Correction: The passage does not accept that these examples imply circularity; it rejects the ontological framework (hylomorphism) and the philosophical principle that from one only one proceeds.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the first objection about matter and form?
A) He accepts the premise and argues that circularity is still impossible.
B) He denies the existence of matter and form as separate principles.
C) He claims that matter and form are not mutually dependent.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant to causality.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of circularity often depends on denying the ontological assumptions underlying the alleged circular relations.

The Two Proofs Against Self-Dependence
Proof that circularity implies self-dependence, impossible for two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical arguments that prove a thing cannot depend on itself, which is the basis for rejecting circular causation.

Core Idea
A single thing cannot be both dependent and independent in the same respect.

Before addressing any objections, the author sets out the core proof against circular causation. He shows that if circularity existed, each thing would depend on itself, and that is rationally impossible for two distinct reasons. This lesson examines those two arguments in detail.

Guided Path
1
Understand circular causation
Circular causation (دور) occurs when A depends on B and B depends on A. The author argues this implies each depends on itself.
2
Analyze the first reason
The first reason is that self-dependence would require a single thing to be both dependent (محتاج) and independent (غني) in the same respect, which is a contradiction (جمع بين النقيضين).
This is based on the law of non-contradiction.
3
Analyze the second reason
The second reason is that dependence (افتقار) is a relation (نسبة) between two distinct things. A single thing cannot have a relation to itself.
Relations require at least two terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء ، يجب كونه مفتقرا إلى ذلك الشيء فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، وذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : إن المفتقر إلى الشيء محتاج إليه ، والمفتقر إليه غير محتاج إلى المفتقر ، فلو كان الشيء الواحد مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة الواحدة ، محتاجا وغنيا ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال الثاني : وهو أن الافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة مخصوصة ، بين المفتقر والمفتقر إليه ، والنسبة لا يمكن حصولها إلا بين أمرين ، فالأمر الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ، يمتنع حصول النسبة فيه . واحتج من قال : الدور غير ممتنع بوجوه : الأول : إن الهيولي والصورة كل واحد منهما محتاج إلى الآخر ، وهو دور ، والجوهر والعرض يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، وهو دور . الثاني : إن المضافين يتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر وهو دور ، ولا يقال : إن وجوب مقارنتهما لأجل أن العلة الواحدة موجبة لهما معا ، لأنا نقول : هذا لا يصح على قول الفلاسفة ، لأن عندهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد الثالث : إن العلية والمعلولية من باب المضاف، والمضافان يوجدان معا ، فالعلية والمعلولية يوجبان المعية والمعية تنافى حصول التقدم ، فوجب أن يمتنع حصول التقدم بين العلة وبين المعلول . والجواب عن الأول : إنا لا نقول بالهيولي والصورة. أما الجسم فإنه مستلزم للحصول في الحيز ، إلا أن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالملازمة بينهما إنما حصلت لأن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق والجواب عن الثاني : إن المضافين يجب حصولهما معا ، وكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى الآخر أمر محال . والجواب عن الثالث : إن ذات العلة وذات المعلول شيء ، وكون هذا علة لذاك ، وكون ذاك معلولا لهذا ، شيء آخر ، فالتضايف والمعية إنما حصلت بينهما باعتبار كونهما علة ومعلولا، وإذا اعتبرنا الحقيقة المخصوصة التي لكل واحد منهما ، فبهذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم والتأخر والله ولي التوفيق
The thing must be in need of that thing, so it follows that each one of them is in need of itself. That is impossible for two reasons: First: The one in need of something is dependent upon it, and the one needed is not dependent upon the one in need. So if one thing were in need of itself, it would follow that the single thing in a single respect would be both dependent and independent, and that is a conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible. Second: Need of something is a specific relation between the one in need and the one needed, and a relation cannot obtain except between two things. So with one thing in one respect, it is impossible for a relation to obtain in it. And one who says that circularity is not impossible argued with several points: First: Matter and form, each one of them is in need of the other, and that is circularity; and substance and accident, each one of them is in need of the other, and that is circularity. Second: The two correlatives depend on each other, and that is circularity. And it cannot be said that their necessary concomitance is because a single cause necessitates them both together, for we say: This is not valid according to the philosophers, because with them the one does not issue from it except the one. Third: Causality and effectuality belong to the category of correlation, and the two correlatives exist together, so causality and effectuality necessitate concomitance, and concomitance precludes priority, so it follows that priority between cause and effect is impossible to obtain. The answer to the first: We do not affirm matter and form. As for body, it entails existence in location, except that the essence of body necessitates existence in absolute location, and on this estimate, the necessary concomitance between them only occurred because the essence of body necessitates existence in absolute location. The answer to the second: The two correlatives must exist together, but each one of them being in need of the other is impossible. The answer to the third: The essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are one thing, and this being a cause of that, and that being an effect of this, is another thing. So the mutual relation and concomitance only occur between them in consideration of their being cause and effect. But if we consider the specific reality that belongs to each of them, then in this consideration priority and posteriority occur. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that circular causation (دور) is impossible and then answers three objections raised by those who claim it is possible. The author begins by stating that if there were circular dependence (A depends on B and B depends on A), then each thing would depend on itself, which is impossible for two reasons. The first reason is that such self-dependence would require a single thing to be both in need (محتاج) and self-sufficient (غني) in the same respect, a contradiction. The second reason is that dependence (افتقار) is a relation between two distinct things; a single thing cannot have a relation to itself. The author then presents three objections from proponents of circularity. The first objection cites examples from natural philosophy: matter (هيولي) and form (صورة) depend on each other, as do substance (جوهر) and accident (عرض). The second objection states that correlatives (مضافان) like father and son depend on each other. The third objection notes that cause (علة) and effect (معلول) are correlatives and must exist together, so priority of cause is negated. The author answers each objection. To the first, he rejects the premise of matter and form, and explains that body (جسم) entails location, but the essence of body necessitates absolute location, and the mutual dependence is only due to that. To the second, he concedes that correlatives exist together, but denies that each depends on the other; rather, they are just mutually concomitant. To the third, he distinguishes between the essences (ذوات) of cause and effect and their relation of causality: the essences are distinct and allow priority and posteriority, while the correlation (تضايف) and concomitance (معية) are only in terms of the relational concepts.

Key Terms
افتقار
محتاج
غني
نقيض
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first reason that a thing cannot depend on itself?
A) It leads to an infinite regress of causes.
B) It would require a thing to be both dependent and independent in the same respect.
C) It violates the principle of sufficient reason.
D) It is rejected by all philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Circular causation is impossible because it implies self-dependence, which is logically contradictory and relationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, where do you subconsciously think you are self-sufficient (غني) while actually being dependent (محتاج)? How does this lesson expose that contradiction?
?
Circular reasoning in relationships—friends, family, or even your relationship with Allah—can create illusions of independence. Can you identify a cycle in your life that relies on self-dependence? What would breaking that cycle look like?
?
If every relation of need requires two distinct entities, how does that change your understanding of your connection to Allah? Are you treating Him as if He needs you, or are you embracing your complete need for Him alone?

Objections and Refutations to Circularity
Three objections to the impossibility of circularity and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the impossibility of circular causation and how the author refutes each one.

Core Idea
Apparent cases of mutual dependence, like matter and form or correlatives, do not imply logical circularity.

After establishing that circular causation is impossible, the author addresses three objections from those who claim it occurs in nature or logic. These objections involve matter and form, correlatives, and cause-effect coexistence. Each objection is met with a precise response that preserves the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first objection
The first objection uses examples from natural philosophy: matter (هيولي) and form (صورة), and substance (جوهر) and accident (عرض), each pair depending on the other.
2
Examine the second objection
The second objection points to correlatives (مضافان), like father and son, which are defined in relation to each other.
3
Examine the third objection
The third objection argues that cause (علة) and effect (معلول) are correlatives and must exist together, so cause cannot be prior.
4
Learn the author's responses
The author refutes each objection: he rejects the reality of matter and form, distinguishes between dependence and mere correlation, and separates essence from relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشيء ، يجب كونه مفتقرا إلى ذلك الشيء فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، وذلك محال لوجهين : الأول : إن المفتقر إلى الشيء محتاج إليه ، والمفتقر إليه غير محتاج إلى المفتقر ، فلو كان الشيء الواحد مفتقرا إلى نفسه ، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة الواحدة ، محتاجا وغنيا ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال الثاني : وهو أن الافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة مخصوصة ، بين المفتقر والمفتقر إليه ، والنسبة لا يمكن حصولها إلا بين أمرين ، فالأمر الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد ، يمتنع حصول النسبة فيه . واحتج من قال : الدور غير ممتنع بوجوه : الأول : إن الهيولي والصورة كل واحد منهما محتاج إلى الآخر ، وهو دور ، والجوهر والعرض يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، وهو دور . الثاني : إن المضافين يتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر وهو دور ، ولا يقال : إن وجوب مقارنتهما لأجل أن العلة الواحدة موجبة لهما معا ، لأنا نقول : هذا لا يصح على قول الفلاسفة ، لأن عندهم الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد الثالث : إن العلية والمعلولية من باب المضاف، والمضافان يوجدان معا ، فالعلية والمعلولية يوجبان المعية والمعية تنافى حصول التقدم ، فوجب أن يمتنع حصول التقدم بين العلة وبين المعلول . والجواب عن الأول : إنا لا نقول بالهيولي والصورة. أما الجسم فإنه مستلزم للحصول في الحيز ، إلا أن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالملازمة بينهما إنما حصلت لأن ذات الجسم توجب الحصول في الحيز المطلق والجواب عن الثاني : إن المضافين يجب حصولهما معا ، وكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى الآخر أمر محال . والجواب عن الثالث : إن ذات العلة وذات المعلول شيء ، وكون هذا علة لذاك ، وكون ذاك معلولا لهذا ، شيء آخر ، فالتضايف والمعية إنما حصلت بينهما باعتبار كونهما علة ومعلولا، وإذا اعتبرنا الحقيقة المخصوصة التي لكل واحد منهما ، فبهذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم والتأخر والله ولي التوفيق
The thing must be in need of that thing, so it follows that each one of them is in need of itself. That is impossible for two reasons: First: The one in need of something is dependent upon it, and the one needed is not dependent upon the one in need. So if one thing were in need of itself, it would follow that the single thing in a single respect would be both dependent and independent, and that is a conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible. Second: Need of something is a specific relation between the one in need and the one needed, and a relation cannot obtain except between two things. So with one thing in one respect, it is impossible for a relation to obtain in it. And one who says that circularity is not impossible argued with several points: First: Matter and form, each one of them is in need of the other, and that is circularity; and substance and accident, each one of them is in need of the other, and that is circularity. Second: The two correlatives depend on each other, and that is circularity. And it cannot be said that their necessary concomitance is because a single cause necessitates them both together, for we say: This is not valid according to the philosophers, because with them the one does not issue from it except the one. Third: Causality and effectuality belong to the category of correlation, and the two correlatives exist together, so causality and effectuality necessitate concomitance, and concomitance precludes priority, so it follows that priority between cause and effect is impossible to obtain. The answer to the first: We do not affirm matter and form. As for body, it entails existence in location, except that the essence of body necessitates existence in absolute location, and on this estimate, the necessary concomitance between them only occurred because the essence of body necessitates existence in absolute location. The answer to the second: The two correlatives must exist together, but each one of them being in need of the other is impossible. The answer to the third: The essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are one thing, and this being a cause of that, and that being an effect of this, is another thing. So the mutual relation and concomitance only occur between them in consideration of their being cause and effect. But if we consider the specific reality that belongs to each of them, then in this consideration priority and posteriority occur. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that circular causation (دور) is impossible and then answers three objections raised by those who claim it is possible. The author begins by stating that if there were circular dependence (A depends on B and B depends on A), then each thing would depend on itself, which is impossible for two reasons. The first reason is that such self-dependence would require a single thing to be both in need (محتاج) and self-sufficient (غني) in the same respect, a contradiction. The second reason is that dependence (افتقار) is a relation between two distinct things; a single thing cannot have a relation to itself. The author then presents three objections from proponents of circularity. The first objection cites examples from natural philosophy: matter (هيولي) and form (صورة) depend on each other, as do substance (جوهر) and accident (عرض). The second objection states that correlatives (مضافان) like father and son depend on each other. The third objection notes that cause (علة) and effect (معلول) are correlatives and must exist together, so priority of cause is negated. The author answers each objection. To the first, he rejects the premise of matter and form, and explains that body (جسم) entails location, but the essence of body necessitates absolute location, and the mutual dependence is only due to that. To the second, he concedes that correlatives exist together, but denies that each depends on the other; rather, they are just mutually concomitant. To the third, he distinguishes between the essences (ذوات) of cause and effect and their relation of causality: the essences are distinct and allow priority and posteriority, while the correlation (تضايف) and concomitance (معية) are only in terms of the relational concepts.

Key Terms

صورة
جوهر
عرض
مضاف
علية
معلولية
تضايف
معية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the refutation of circularity denies the coexistence of correlatives, or that cause and effect cannot be simultaneous.
Correction: The author does not deny coexistence; he distinguishes between existential dependence and mere correlation. Correlatives exist together, but this does not entail dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author respond to the objection that matter and form depend on each other?
A) He affirms matter and form but denies dependence.
B) He rejects the existence of matter and form as separate entities.
C) He says matter depends on form but not vice versa.
D) He says the dependence is only apparent.

Key Takeaway
Apparent mutual dependence in examples like matter-form or correlatives does not constitute logical circularity because it involves either a single essence or a correlation without dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship or dependency you feel trapped in—perhaps with a person, a habit, or a belief. The author insists that true mutual dependence is impossible. If that is true, what does that say about the actual nature of your attachment? What would it mean to see yourself as independent in essence, even while coexisting? Can you commit to distinguishing your core self from the relation you are in?
?
The objectors pointed to examples like matter and form to justify circularity, but the author simply rejected their reality. Are there assumptions in your own worldview that you hold without question, like the objectors held matter and form? How would you respond if someone challenged their existence? Will you examine one uncritical assumption today?
?
The author's final move is to separate the essence of cause and effect from their relation. In your life, do you confuse the identity of someone or something with your relationship to them? For instance, do you define yourself by your role (parent, child, professional) while neglecting your own essential reality? How might this distinction free you to see both priority and connection clearly? What one step can you take to reclaim your essence?

Correlatives Must Be Simultaneous
The author answers an objection about the simultaneity of correlatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlatives must exist together and why they cannot be mutually dependent.

Core Idea
Correlatives exist together but not by depending on each other.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the relationship between cause and effect: if cause and effect are correlatives, they must be simultaneous. But does that lead to absurd circularity? The author clarifies the nature of correlation to avoid this pitfall.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence carefully then the translation. Notice the key terms: المضافان (the two correlatives), معا (together), مفتقراً (in need).
2
Understand the claim
The author asserts that correlatives must occur together (معا). That means if one exists, the other must exist at the same time. This is a well-known logical rule.
3
Identify the impossibility
Then he says it is impossible for each one to be in need of the other. This refers to a mutual dependency in existence, which would lead to circularity. So correlatives are simultaneous but not causally dependent on each other.
This distinction is key to avoiding the objection.
4
Apply to causality
Think about cause and effect as correlatives: they coexist, but the cause is not dependent on the effect for its existence. The effect depends on the cause, not vice versa.
This resolves the worry that cause and effect would have to be mutually dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : إن المضافين يجب حصولهما معا ، وكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى الآخر أمر محال .
والجواب عن الثالث : إن ذات العلة وذات المعلول شيء ، وكون هذا علة لذاك ، وكون ذاك معلولا لهذا ، شيء آخر ، فالتضايف والمعية إنما حصلت بينهما باعتبار كونهما علة ومعلولا ، أما إذا اعتبرنا الحقيقة المخصوصة التي لكل واحد منهما ، فبهذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم والتأخر . [ والله ولي التوفيق ]
And the answer to the second [objection]: The correlatives must both occur together, and for each one to be in need of the other is impossible. And the answer to the third [objection]: The essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are one thing, and for this to be a cause of that and that to be an effect of this is another thing. So the correlation and simultaneity only obtain between them in consideration of their being cause and effect. As for when we consider the specific reality that belongs to each one of them, then in this consideration priority and posteriority occur. And God is the one who grants success.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents answers to two objections, likely in the context of a discussion about causality and simultaneous existence. The author first addresses the objection regarding correlatives (like cause and effect). He states that correlatives must exist together—that is, if one exists, the other must also exist. He then declares that for each one to be in need of the other (in the sense of dependence for existence) is impossible, implying that while they are correlatives, they are not mutually dependent in a way that leads to circularity. The second objection concerns priority and posteriority between cause and effect. The author clarifies that the essence (dhat) of the cause and the essence of the effect are distinct entities (the cause's essence is one thing, the effect's essence is another). However, the relation of being a cause and being an effect is something additional to the essences. The correlation and simultaneity (mu'iyya) occur only in terms of this relational aspect. If we consider the specific reality of each essence, then priority and posteriority are obtained: the cause is prior in existence, the effect is posterior. This distinction resolves the apparent paradox that cause and effect must coexist but also have order.

Key Terms
المضافان
التضايف
المعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correlatives must be mutually dependent for existence.
Correction: The author clarifies that correlatives must be simultaneous, but not necessarily dependent on each other. Mutual dependence is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the two correlatives?
A) They must be simultaneous and mutually dependent.
B) They must be simultaneous but not mutually dependent.
C) They cannot be simultaneous.
D) They are independent of each other in all respects.

Key Takeaway
Correlatives must coexist but cannot be mutually dependent; this principle underlies the relation of cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two things happen together, one must be the cause of the other? How does this lesson challenge you to reexamine your assumptions about what you think causes your happiness or success? Will you now pause to consider whether you are attributing need where there is only simultaneity?
?
This passage teaches that mutual dependence between correlatives is impossible. Is there a relationship in your life where you have felt that both you and the other person are completely dependent on each other? How does this truth free you from that flawed thinking and redirect your ultimate dependence to Allah alone? Make a commitment today to identify one such relationship and consciously shift your reliance.

Resolving Priority and Simultaneity in Causality
The author resolves the objection about priority and simultaneity of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cause and effect can be both simultaneous (as correlatives) and have priority/posteriority in reality.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are simultaneous as relations but ordered in their essences.

A common objection: if cause and effect are correlatives, they must be simultaneous, so how can the cause be prior? The author resolves this by distinguishing between the essences of cause and effect and their relational aspect. This lesson unpacks that solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic answer to the third objection and its translation. Note the key terms: ذات (essence), علة (cause), معلول (effect), التضايف (correlation), المعية (simultaneity), التقدم (priority), التأخر (posteriority).
2
Distinguish essence from relation
The author says: the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are one thing (i.e., each is a distinct entity), and the relation of being a cause and being an effect is something else. This separation is crucial.
The relation is an additional consideration.
3
Understand where simultaneity applies
Correlation and simultaneity (المعية) only apply to the relation of cause and effect, not to the essences themselves. As correlatives, they must be simultaneous in time.
4
Understand where priority applies
When we consider the specific realities (الحقيقة المخصوصة) of each essence, we observe priority (التقدم) for the cause and posteriority (التأخر) for the effect. The cause is prior in existence, the effect posterior. This is compatible with their simultaneous relation.
The priority here is not temporal but existential or essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني : إن المضافين يجب حصولهما معا ، وكون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا إلى الآخر أمر محال .
والجواب عن الثالث : إن ذات العلة وذات المعلول شيء ، وكون هذا علة لذاك ، وكون ذاك معلولا لهذا ، شيء آخر ، فالتضايف والمعية إنما حصلت بينهما باعتبار كونهما علة ومعلولا ، أما إذا اعتبرنا الحقيقة المخصوصة التي لكل واحد منهما ، فبهذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم والتأخر . [ والله ولي التوفيق ]

And the answer to the third [objection]: The essence of the cause and the essence of the effect are one thing, and for this to be a cause of that and that to be an effect of this is another thing. So the correlation and simultaneity only obtain between them in consideration of their being cause and effect. As for when we consider the specific reality that belongs to each one of them, then in this consideration priority and posteriority occur. And God is the one who grants success.

Commentary
This passage presents answers to two objections, likely in the context of a discussion about causality and simultaneous existence. The author first addresses the objection regarding correlatives (like cause and effect). He states that correlatives must exist together—that is, if one exists, the other must also exist. He then declares that for each one to be in need of the other (in the sense of dependence for existence) is impossible, implying that while they are correlatives, they are not mutually dependent in a way that leads to circularity. The second objection concerns priority and posteriority between cause and effect. The author clarifies that the essence (dhat) of the cause and the essence of the effect are distinct entities (the cause's essence is one thing, the effect's essence is another). However, the relation of being a cause and being an effect is something additional to the essences. The correlation and simultaneity (mu'iyya) occur only in terms of this relational aspect. If we consider the specific reality of each essence, then priority and posteriority are obtained: the cause is prior in existence, the effect is posterior. This distinction resolves the apparent paradox that cause and effect must coexist but also have order.

Misconception: Cause and effect cannot be both simultaneous and have priority.
Correction: The author shows they are simultaneous as correlative terms but have priority/posteriority in their essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing or a trial in your life, do you tend to focus on the immediate apparent cause and forget the ontological priority of Allah’s will? How can the distinction between the essence of the cause and the relation of causality help you shift your perspective?
?
Think of a relationship or situation where you confused the simultaneity of events with equality of dependence. How does recognizing that the cause is prior in essence even when effects are simultaneous challenge your assumptions about that situation?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have treated a secondary cause as if it were independent, forgetting that its very essence is dependent on the First Cause? Commit to affirming verbally or in writing that the true priority belongs to Allah alone.

The First Proof Against Infinite Regress
First proof against infinite regress: analysis of the whole chain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against infinite regress of causes, which shows that the entire chain of causes and effects must itself be possible and thus requires an external cause.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possible causes is itself possible and needs a cause outside the chain.

In classical Islamic philosophy, one of the most important arguments for the existence of a necessary being is the refutation of an infinite regress of causes. This lesson presents the first proof from Al-Matalibul Aliyah, which carefully analyzes the status of the entire chain of causes and effects. By reasoning about the possibility of the whole, it establishes that an infinite regress cannot be self-sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of simultaneous causation
The proof depends on the idea that a cause must exist at the same time as its effect. Ensure you grasp this before reading the argument.
This premise is crucial for the whole proof.
2
Follow the reasoning that the totality is possible
The author argues that the entire infinite series, being made of possible individuals, is itself possible. Pay attention to the dependence argument.
The key is that each individual is possible, so the whole is possible.
3
Identify the three options for the cause of the totality
After establishing that the totality needs a cause, three possibilities are listed: the totality itself, something internal, or something external.
The proof stops here; later lessons eliminate the first two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر
في المالسب التسلسلات
إبطال السلسال
اعلم أنه حصل في هذه المسألة أنواع من الدلائل :
البرهان الأول : إنا لو فرضنا كون كل ممكن ، معلولا الممكن آخر، لا إلى نهاية لزم كون تلك الأسباب والمسببات موجودة دفعة واحدة بأسرها ، بناء على المقدمة التي بيناها ، وهي أن السبب لا بد وأن يكون موجودا حال وجود المسبب ، وإن ثبت هذا ، فنقول : مجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات : ممكن الوجود، والدليل عليه : إن ذلك المجموع مفتقر في تحققه إلى تحقق كل واحد من تلك الأحاد [ وكل واحد من تلك الأحاد ] ممكن ، فالمجموع مفتقر إلى الأسباب الممكنة ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان ، فثبت أن ذلك المجموع ممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل ممكن فله مؤثر ، فذلك المجموع له مؤثر .
فنقول : المؤثر في ذلك المجموع، إما يكون نفس ذلك المجموع ، أو أمرا داخلا فيه ، أو أمرا خارجا عنه ، فهذه أقسام ثلاثة لا مزيد عليها .
Chapter Ten
On the refutation of infinite regresses
Refutation of the chain
Know that there have occurred in this issue types of proofs:
The first proof: If we suppose that every possible thing is an effect of another possible thing without end, then necessarily those causes and effects exist all at once as a whole, based on the premise we have explained, which is that the cause must exist at the time the effect exists. And if this is established, then we say: The totality of those causes and effects is possible in existence. The evidence for it is that that totality is dependent for its actualization on the actualization of every one of those individuals, and every one of those individuals is possible. So the totality is dependent on possible causes, and whatever is dependent on the possible is more entitled to possibility. Thus it is established that that totality is possible in itself. And every possible has an influence (mu'aththir), so that totality has an influence.
Then we say: The influence on that totality is either that same totality, or something internal to it, or something external to it. These are three divisions with no more.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Ten, which aims to refute the concept of infinite regress (tasalsul) in causal chains. The author introduces the first proof. The proof proceeds as follows: Assume an infinite chain where every possible thing is the effect of another possible thing, ad infinitum. Based on the previously established premise that a cause must coexist with its effect at the moment the effect exists, the entire chain of causes and effects must exist simultaneously as a single whole. Next, the author argues that this whole composite of all causes and effects is possible in existence. Why? Because it is dependent (muftaqir) on the actualization of each individual possible cause within it. Since each individual is possible, the entire whole, being dependent on possible things, is itself possible. By the principle that every possible thing requires an external cause (mu'aththir), the whole must have a cause. The author then presents a threefold division regarding that cause: either the cause is the whole itself, or something inside it, or something outside it. The proof stops here, and subsequent discussion would presumably eliminate the first two possibilities to conclude that the cause must be external (i.e., a necessary being). This argument is a classic kalam proof against infinite regress.

Key Terms
التسلسل
الممكن
السبب
المسبب
المجموع
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of causes can be self-sufficient and needs no external cause.
Correction: The proof shows that the entire infinite chain, being composed of possible beings, is itself possible and therefore requires a cause external to it.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof against infinite regress, the totality of the infinite chain of causes and effects is considered to be:
A) Necessary in itself
B) Possible in itself and requires an external cause
C) Impossible because of infinite regress
D) The cause of itself

Key Takeaway
The infinite chain of possible causes cannot be self-sufficient; it requires an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trace your own existence back through parents, food, air, and so on. If that chain of causes never ended, would you be satisfied with an endless dependence? Or does this argument force you to admit that something self-sufficient must ultimately uphold you?
?
The proof shows that even an infinite chain of possible things cannot cause itself. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, ignoring your dependency on the Necessary Being? What would change if you truly lived knowing you are always in need?
?
If the cause of all possible beings is outside the chain—transcendent and necessary—what does that demand from you today? Are you willing to orient your life toward that cause, not just intellectually accept it?

The First Proof Against Infinite Regress
Establishes that the infinite series of possible causes is possible and needs a cause, and lists three alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against infinite regress and why the sum of all possible causes must have an external cause.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possible things cannot be self-sufficient; it demands an external cause.

This passage opens the chapter on refuting infinite regress. The author presents a powerful argument: assuming an infinite chain of possible causes and effects, the entire sum of them is itself possible and thus needs a cause. He then considers where that cause could come from—internally or externally. Follow along to see how internal possibilities are eliminated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind before analyzing it.
Notice the clear structure: assumption, premise, conclusion, proof, and enumeration.
2
Identify the key premise: why the sum is possible
The author argues that the sum depends on each individual possible; since each individual is possible, the sum is also possible. Understanding this dependence is crucial.
This step uses the logical principle that what depends on the possible is itself possible.
3
Notice the three alternatives for the cause
After proving the sum needs a cause, the author lists three logical possibilities: the sum itself, a part of it, or something external. These exhaust all options.
The next lesson will examine why the first two are impossible, leaving only an external cause.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why internal candidates (the sum or a part) would lead to circularity or regress. This prepares you for the subsequent reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر في المطالب التسلسلية إبطال التسلسل

اعلم أنه حصل في هذه المسألة أنواع من الدلائل :

البرهان الأول : إنا لو فرضنا كون كل ممكن معلولا لممكن آخر لا إلى نهاية لزم كون تلك الأسباب والمسببات موجودة دفعة واحدة بأسرها، بناء على المقدمة التي بيناها، وهي أن السبب لا بد وأن يكون موجودا حال وجود المسبب، وإن ثبت هذا، فنقول : مجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات : ممكن الوجود، والدليل عليه : إن ذلك المجموع مفتقر في تحققه إلى تحقق كل واحد من تلك الأحاد [وكل واحد من تلك الأحاد] ممكن، فالمجموع مفتقر إلى الأسباب الممكنة، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان، فثبت أن ذلك المجموع ممكن الوجود لذاته، وكل ممكن فله مؤثر، فذلك المجموع له مؤثر.

فنقول : المؤثر في ذلك المجموع، إما يكون نفس ذلك المجموع، أو أمرا داخلا فيه، أو أمرا خارجا عنه، فهذه أقسام ثلاثة لا مزيد عليها.
Chapter Ten on Serial Matters: Refutation of Infinite Regress. Know that there are types of proofs in this issue: The first proof: if we suppose that every possible is an effect of another possible ad infinitum, then it necessarily follows that all those causes and effects exist at once as a whole, based on the premise we explained, namely that the cause must exist at the time of the effect. And if this is established, we say: the sum of those causes and effects is possible of existence. The evidence for it is that that sum depends in its realization on the realization of every single one of those individuals, and every single one of those individuals is possible, so the sum depends on possible causes, and that which depends on the possible is more deserving of possibility. So it is established that that sum is possible of existence in itself, and every possible has an efficient cause, so that sum has an efficient cause. We say: the efficient cause of that sum is either that sum itself, or something internal to it, or something external to it. These are three divisions with no more.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against infinite regress (إبطال التسلسل). It begins by assuming an infinite chain of possible causes and effects (كل ممكن معلول لممكن آخر لا إلى نهاية). The author argues that all these causes and effects would exist simultaneously at the moment of any effect, because the cause must exist at the time of the effect. Then he argues that the entire sum (مجموع) of these causes and effects is itself possible (ممكن الوجود). Why? Because it depends on each individual, each of which is possible. So the sum, being dependent on possible things, is also possible. Then he applies the principle that every possible has an efficient cause (مؤثر). Therefore, the sum must have a cause. He then lists three possibilities for this cause: either (1) the sum itself is its cause, (2) a part of the sum is its cause, or (3) an external entity is its cause. These three exhaust all logical possibilities.

علة

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the sum's possibility is trivial or that the argument assumes the sum exists without proof.
Correction: The author carefully proves the sum's possibility by showing its dependence on possible individuals. The sum's existence is not assumed; it follows from the initial supposition and the premise of cause-effect coexistence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is the sum of all possible causes and effects itself possible?
A) Because every individual in the sum is possible, and the sum depends on them.
B) Because the sum is self-sufficient and independent.
C) Because the sum is eternal and uncreated.
D) Because the sum is the cause of itself.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against infinite regress shows that an infinite chain of possible causes, taken as a whole, is itself possible and must be caused by something external to it.

?
The proof shows that an infinite regress cannot be self-sufficient. In your own life, where do you tend to rely on finite things as if they were ultimate causes? How can this proof reorient your trust?
?
If the sum of all possible causes needs an external cause, what does that imply about your own ultimate reliance? Are you willing to let go of the illusion of self-sufficiency and submit to the One who caused everything?
