The First Argument: Self-Causation Implies Independence from a Cause
First argument from contradiction of possibility requiring a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the sum being its own cause: that it would imply that the possible is independent of a cause, contradicting the established principle that every possible needs a cause.

Core Idea
To be its own cause means to need nothing outside itself—but we already know the possible needs a cause.

Imam al-Razi begins his refutation of the first alternative by presenting a concise logical argument. If the sum of all possible causes were its own cause, it would have to be self-sufficient. But we have already proven that this sum is possible in itself, and every possible thing requires a cause. Thus, the claim collapses. This lesson examines that argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the author states the claim, then presents the first argument.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument relies on the premise that ‘being a cause of itself’ means ‘not needing another’. Reflect on why this is taken as a definition.
3
Connect to the earlier proof
The author references an earlier proof that the sum is possible in itself. Consider how that proof relates to the argument.
The earlier proof is assumed; we are now in the stage of refuting alternatives.
4
Understand the contradiction
If the possible is independent, that contradicts the necessity of a cause for all possibles. That contradiction shows the claim is false.
This is a classic argument from established principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من وجوه :

أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال إن ذلك المجموع علة لنفسه، فهذا

الأول : إنه لا معنى لقولنا إنه علة لوجود نفسه، إلا أنه غير محتاج إلى الغير، وقد دللنا على أن ذلك المجموع ممكن لذاته، فيرجع حاصل هذا الكلام إلى أن الممكن غني عن السبب، فيكون هذا رجوعا إلى المقدمات السالفة، من أن الممكنات، هل تتوقف على السبب أم لا ؟ ونحن إنما نتكلم في هذا المقام بعد إثبات أن الممكن لا بد له من سبب .

الثاني : إن المحتاج إلى الشيء ممكن بالنسبة إلى المحتاج إليه، والمحتاج إليه غني بالنسبة إلى المحتاج فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد محتاجا وغنيا، وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين.

الثالث : إن المعلول مفتقر إلى العلة، فلو كان الشيء الواحد علة لنفسه لزم كونه مفتقراً إلى نفسه. والافتقار إلى الشيء نسبة، والنسبة لا تحصل إلا بين الأمرين، فأما الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد، فيمتنع كونه منسوبا إلى نفسه.
From aspects: As for the first division: that that sum is a cause of itself, then this is false from aspects: First: There is no meaning to our saying that it is a cause of its own existence except that it is not in need of another. And we have proven that that sum is possible in itself. So the upshot of this statement is that the possible is independent of the cause, and this is a return to the previous premises about whether possibles depend on a cause or not. We are speaking in this context after establishing that the possible must have a cause. Second: That which is in need of something is possible relative to that which it needs, and that which is needed is independent relative to the needy. So if a single thing were a cause of itself, it would follow that a single thing in one respect is both needy and independent, and that necessitates combining two contradictories. Third: The effect depends on the cause. So if a single thing were a cause of itself, it would follow that it depends on itself. Dependence on something is a relation, and a relation only occurs between two things. As for a single thing in one respect, it is impossible for it to be related to itself.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage refutes the first of the three alternatives: that the sum of all possible causes is its own cause. The author presents three arguments. (1) If the sum were its own cause, that would mean it is not in need of another (غير محتاج إلى الغير). But we already proved the sum is possible, and possible things need a cause. So this contradicts that necessity. The argument essentially says: calling it its own cause just means it is self-sufficient, but we already know it is not. (2) A cause is self-sufficient relative to its effect; an effect is needy relative to its cause. If the same thing were both cause and effect of itself, it would be both needy and self-sufficient in the same respect, which is a contradiction (الجمع بين النقيضين). (3) Dependence is a relation (نسبة) that requires two distinct terms. A thing cannot be in relation to itself, because a relation requires two relata. So it is impossible for anything to be the cause of itself.

Key Terms
علة
محتاج
غني
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what does 'being a cause of itself' mean?
A) That it is not in need of another (غير محتاج إلى الغير)
B) That it is the ultimate cause of everything
C) That it is independent of all causes
D) That it is possible in itself

Key Takeaway
The claim that something is its own cause collapses because it would make a possible thing independent of a cause, contradicting the necessity of a cause for all possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often attribute outcomes to your own efforts or choices. But this lesson teaches that anything that is possible in itself cannot be its own cause. Is there an area of your life where you are acting as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that you are a possible being in constant need of a cause? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not the cause of your own existence?
?
The author says that to say something is a cause of itself is to say it needs nothing outside itself. Can you identify a belief or habit that you hold onto as if it were self-caused, needing no external support or correction? How does this lesson challenge that?
?
If every possible being must have a cause, then you are a possible being. What is the ultimate cause that explains your existence? Have you ever truly contemplated that dependence, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What would it mean to live in constant acknowledgment of your need for that cause?

The Second Argument: Self-Causation Forces Combining Contradictories
Second argument from contradiction of being both needy and independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against self-causation: that it would require a single thing to be both needy and independent in the same respect, which is contradictory.

Core Idea
A cause is independent, an effect is needy; the same thing cannot be both in one respect.

The second argument against the sum being its own cause is a logical contradiction: if something is its own cause, it must be both cause and effect of itself. But a cause is independent relative to its effect, and an effect is needy relative to its cause. Therefore, the same thing would be both needy and independent in the same respect—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it builds on the relation between cause and effect.
2
Identify the two roles
Recognize that cause and effect have opposite properties: the cause is independent (غني) and the effect is needy (محتاج).
3
Apply to self-causation
If X is cause of itself, then X is both cause and effect, so both properties apply simultaneously.
This leads to a contradiction (الجمع بين النقيضين).
4
Understand the key qualifier
The contradiction arises 'in one respect' (بالاعتبار الواحد). This prevents the objection that it might be different respects.
The author explicitly adds this qualifier.

The Third Argument: Dependence Is a Relation Requiring Two Distinct Entities
Third argument from impossibility of self-relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against self-causation: that dependence is a relational property that requires two distinct relata, and a single thing cannot be related to itself.

Core Idea
Dependence is a relation between two things; a thing cannot be in a relation with itself.

The third and final argument against self-causation is ontological: dependence (الافتقار) is a relation (نسبة) that can only exist between two distinct entities. If something were its own cause, it would depend on itself, requiring a relation between a thing and itself, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The argument is short but profound.
2
Understand the nature of dependence
The passage states that the effect depends on the cause, and this dependence is a relation (نسبة).
3
Consider the requirements of a relation
A relation requires two distinct terms; otherwise it is not a genuine relation. For example, the relation 'father of' requires two persons.
4
Apply to self-causation
If something depends on itself, it would be a relation with only one relatum, which is impossible. Therefore, self-causation is impossible.

Source Text

Refutation of the Second Alternative: One Individual as Cause of the Whole
Refutes the possibility that one individual within the sum of causes is the cause of the whole, leading to self-causation and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an individual within a sum of causes cannot be the cause of the entire sum, due to self-causation and circularity.

Core Idea
A part cannot cause the whole without causing itself, leading to impossibility.

In this passage, the author tackles the second possible alternative for causal relationships: that one specific member of a group of causes could be the cause of the whole group. He shows that this leads to two fatal contradictions: self-causation and circular causation. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the broader argument about the nature of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure and the key terms like فرد, مجموع, دور.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is complete.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that the cause of the whole sum is an individual within that sum. Recognize this as the 'second division'.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the reasoning: if an individual causes the whole sum, it must cause every part, including itself. This leads to self-causation, which is impossible. Also, it causes its own cause, leading to circularity.
Self-causation is impossible because nothing can be prior to itself.
4
Understand why self-causation and circularity are impossible
Self-causation violates the principle that a cause must be distinct from its effect. Circularity (dawr) is also impossible because it would require an infinite regress of causes.
These principles have been established earlier in the text.
5
Conclude the refutation's validity
Since the claim leads to two impossibilities, it must be false. Thus the second alternative is invalid.
The passage ends with 'فثبت أن هذا القسم' implying the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : علة ذلك المجموع فرد من أفراد ذلك المجموع، فهذا أيضا باطل، لأن كل ما كان علة للمجموع، وجب كونه علة لجميع آحاد ذلك المجموع، ولا شك أن أحد آحاد ذلك المجموع ذلك الواحد، الذي فرض كونه علة لذلك المجموع، فحينئذ يلزم في ذلك الواحد كونه علة لنفسه، وقد بينا أن ذلك محال، ويلزم منه أيضاً أن يكون علة لعلة نفسه وذلك يوجب الدور، وقد بينا أنه محال، فثبت أن هذا القسم
As for the second division: that one says: the cause of that sum is an individual among the individuals of that sum. This is also false, because whatever is a cause of the sum must be a cause of all individuals of that sum. There is no doubt that one of the individuals of that sum is that very individual which is supposed to be the cause of that sum. Then it necessarily follows for that individual that it is a cause of itself, and we have shown that is impossible. And it also follows from it that it is a cause of its own cause, and that necessitates circularity, and we have shown that is impossible. So it is established that this division...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage refutes the second alternative: that the cause of the whole sum is one particular individual within that sum. The argument: If that individual is the cause of the entire sum, then it must be the cause of every part of the sum, including itself. That would make it the cause of itself, which has already been proven impossible in the previous section. Additionally, if it causes the sum, and the sum includes that individual as an effect, then that individual would be the cause of its own cause (since it is part of the sum which it causes), leading to circular causation (دور), which is also impossible. Therefore, this alternative is invalid. The text begins to conclude with 'فثبت أن هذا القسم' but the sentence is cut off; presumably the conclusion is that this division is invalid.

Key Terms
فرد
مجموع
دور
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a part could cause the whole without causing itself.
Correction: The author shows that being cause of the whole necessitates causing all parts, including that part, so self-causation is unavoidable.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation of the second alternative conclude about the individual that is supposed to be the cause of the whole sum?
A) It must be the cause of itself and cause circularity.
B) It must be external to the sum.
C) It must be the final cause of the sum.
D) It must be impossible because it leads to a contradiction in attributes.

Key Takeaway
The second alternative (one individual causing the whole) fails because it leads to the individual being the cause of itself and causing its own cause, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If nothing can cause itself, then every moment of your existence is sustained by something outside you. Are you truly living with that awareness, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you consciously acknowledged that your next heartbeat is caused by Allah alone?
?
The author argues that circular causation is impossible. In your spiritual life, do you ever find yourself in a loop—seeking satisfaction from the same habits that leave you empty? What is that 'first cause' you keep avoiding? How can you break the cycle by turning directly to Allah?
?
You are only one individual in the vast sum of creation. Yet the lesson shows that an individual cannot cause the whole. Where in your life do you overestimate your own control or influence, thinking you can manage the 'big picture' alone? How can you submit to Allah's complete sovereignty and rely on Him for the outcomes you cannot cause?

The First Two Objections: Cycles and Rational Souls
The first two objections against the proof of the impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two objections against the proof of the necessary being, which appeal to the infinite regress of cycles according to philosophers and the infinity of rational souls.

Core Idea
If infinite regress is allowed for cycles and souls, why not for causes?

The author presents a series of objections from those who claim that infinite regress is not impossible, citing examples from philosophy and theology. The first two objections come from the philosophers' view of cyclical motion and the immateriality and infinity of rational souls. Understanding these objections will help you see how critics argue for the possibility of an infinite causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection about cycles
Read the first objection carefully: 'Philosophers believe every cycle is preceded by another without a beginning. If that is permissible, why not allow a chain of causes without a beginning?'
This objection points to an accepted infinite regress in nature.
2
Read the second objection about souls
Read the second objection: 'Rational souls are infinite in number. If infinite souls are accepted, why not infinite causes and effects?'
The objection uses the idea of immortal, infinite souls.
3
Compare the strategy
Notice the common argumentative strategy: both objections show that certain types of infinity are already accepted, therefore the claim that infinite regress is impossible is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : السؤال على هذا الدليل من وجوه :
الأول : إن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرتموه في إبطال التسلسل منقوض بأشياء : أحدها : إن عند الحكماء كل دورة مسبوقة بدورة أخرى ، لا إلى أول ، فإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون كل سبب مسبوقا بسبب آخر ، لا إلى أول .
وثانيهما : إن النفوس الناطقة [ الباقية ] غير متناهية . وإذا جاز [ وجود] ما لا نهاية له من النفوس [ فلم لا يجوز وجود ما لا نهاية له ] من الأسباب والمسببات ؟
وثالثها : إن المتكلمين يثبتون حوادث لا آخر لها في أحوال أهل القيامة ، والفلاسفة [ يقولون بذلك في أدوار هذا العالم ] .
ورابعها : المتكلمون يقولون معلومات الله لا نهاية لها ، وإن مقدورات الله لا نهاية لها فلم لا يجوز مثله في الأسباب والمسببات ؟
وخامسها وهو وارد على الفلاسفة والمتكلمين معا ، وهو إن صحة حدوث الحوادث لا أول لها ، إذ لو حصلت لتلك الصحة أول ، لكان قبل ذلك المبدأ ممتنعا لعينه ، ثم انقلب ممكنا لعينه ، وهو محال ، فثبت أنه لا أول للإمكان والصحة ، وكان التسلسل حاصلا في ثبوت هذا الإمكان .
وسادسها : وهو أن مراتب الأعداد لا نهاية لها ، فكان التسلسل حاصلا فيها .
وسابعها : إنه تعالى عالم بالشيء ، وكل من علم شيئا أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالما به ، وإذا ثبت هذا الإمكان ، وجب أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل في حق الله تعالى، لكونه منزها عن طبيعة القوة والإمكان ، وعلى هذا التقدير فهو سبحانه وتعالى عالم بالشيء ، وعالم بكونه عالما به ، وهكذا في المرتبة الرابعة ، والخامسة إلى ما لا نهاية له ، فقد حصلت هناك [ مراتب غير متناهية ، وكل مرتبة أخيرة منها فإنها متفرعة على المرتبة التي قبلها ، فقد حصلت هناك ] أمور غير متناهية ، وهي مرتبة بالطبع، وهي بأسرها موجودة دفعة واحدة ، فهذا نقض قوي على قولكم : التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات محال .
If it is said: The objection to this proof has several aspects: First: This proof that you have mentioned in refuting the regress is invalidated by several things: One: According to the philosophers, every cycle is preceded by another cycle, without a beginning. So if that is permissible, why is it not permissible that every cause be preceded by another cause, without a beginning? Second: The remaining rational souls are infinite. If it is permissible for souls to be infinite, why is it not permissible for causes and effects to be infinite? Third: The mutakallimun affirm events without end in the states of the people of the Resurrection, and the philosophers affirm that in the cycles of this world. Fourth: The mutakallimun say that God's known things are infinite and that God's power's objects are infinite, so why is it not permissible likewise in causes and effects? Fifth: This applies to both philosophers and mutakallimun, namely that the possibility of the origination of events has no beginning. For if that possibility had a beginning, before that beginning it would be impossible in itself, then it would become possible in itself, which is absurd. So it is established that possibility and validity have no beginning, and regress occurs in the establishment of this possibility. Sixth: That the levels of numbers are infinite, so regress occurs in them. Seventh: He is knowledgeable of a thing, and whoever knows a thing can know that he knows it. If this possibility is established, it must be actual in the case of God, because He is free from the nature of potentiality and possibility. On this assumption, He is knowledgeable of the thing and knowledgeable that He is knowledgeable of it, and so on to the fourth, fifth levels ad infinitum. There have occurred infinite levels, and every last level is derived from the level before it. Thus there have occurred infinite things, naturally ordered, all existing at once. So this is a strong refutation of your statement that regress in causes and effects is impossible.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage lists seven objections against the argument that infinite regress is impossible. Each objection provides an example from the philosophers' cycles, rational souls, resurrection, God's knowledge, etc., arguing that such infinite regresses are accepted elsewhere. The objections target both philosophers and mutakallimun. The seventh objection concerning God's knowledge of His knowledge is particularly detailed, arguing that it leads to an actual infinite regress of knowledge relations in God, which contradicts the claim that regress is impossible.

Key Terms
دورة
نفوس ناطقة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections are the author's own affirmative arguments.
Correction: The objections are presented as criticisms from opponents; the author will likely refute them later.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first objection?
A) The infinity of rational souls
B) The infinite regress of cycles according to philosophers
C) The infinite events in the afterlife
D) The infinite levels of numbers

Key Takeaway
The first two objections attempt to show that infinite regress is accepted in other contexts (cycles and souls), so it should be allowed for causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors use a false analogy—cycles and souls are not the same as a causal chain. Think of a time you used a false analogy to defend a position you were attached to. Will you repent from that intellectual dishonesty today?
?
You accept some things as infinite (like your own thoughts) but reject infinite regress in causes. Where else do you make convenient exceptions in your beliefs? Are you willing to be consistent even if it costs you comfort?
?
The objectors here are not the author; they are straw men. Have you ever dismissed a truth because you listened to a weak objection without seeking the full response? Commit today to investigate every objection you encounter, especially about Allah's existence.

Objections 3-4: Events and Divine Attributes
The third and fourth objections regarding infinite events and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth objections, which cite the infinite events in the afterlife and God's infinite knowledge and power.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge is infinite, why can't causes be infinite?

The third and fourth objections shift to theological examples: the mutakallimun affirm never-ending events in the afterlife, and they also assert that God's objects of knowledge and power are infinite. If such infinities are accepted, the objectors ask, why not accept an infinite chain of causes and effects?

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection about resurrection events
Read the third objection: 'The mutakallimun affirm events without end in the afterlife; the philosophers also say so in the cycles of the world.'
This objection highlights that infinite future events are accepted.
2
Read the fourth objection about God's knowledge
Read the fourth objection: 'The mutakallimun say God's known things and objects of power are infinite. So why not accept infinite causes and effects?'
This objection uses the infinity of divine attributes.
3
Reflect on the pattern
Notice that both objections point to accepted infinities in theology to argue for the possibility of an infinite causal regress.

Objections 5-7: Possibility, Numbers, and Divine Self-Knowledge
The fifth, sixth, and seventh objections regarding possibility, numbers, and divine self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final three objections, which are more philosophical, dealing with the possibility of origination, infinite numbers, and God's self-knowledge leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Even possibility, numbers, and God's knowledge generate infinite regress.

The final three objections are especially powerful. The fifth argues that the very possibility of events has no beginning, leading to a regress of possibilities. The sixth notes that numbers are infinite by nature. The seventh, most detailed, argues that God's knowledge of His own knowledge generates an actual infinite regress of levels of knowledge, all existing simultaneously. These are intended to demonstrate that infinite regress is not impossible but actually occurs.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth objection about possibility
Read the fifth objection: 'The possibility of events has no beginning, leading to a regress in the establishment of that possibility.'
This objection targets the very notion of possibility.
2
Read the sixth objection about numbers
Read the sixth objection: 'The levels of numbers are infinite, so regress occurs in them.'
Numbers are a classic example of actual infinity.
3
Read the seventh objection about divine self-knowledge
Read the seventh objection: 'God knows a thing, and knows that He knows it, ad infinitum. This creates an infinite regress of knowledge levels, all existing simultaneously.'
This is the most complex objection, dealing with reflexive knowledge.
4
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how these three objections together challenge the claim that infinite regress is impossible by showing that it occurs in fundamental aspects of reality.

D) God's anger

Key Takeaway
Objections 5-7 show that infinite regress appears in possibility, numbers, and even God's knowledge, undermining the claim that regress is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's knowledge of you includes an infinite chain of self-awareness—He knows that He knows everything about your life. If that is true, then every thought, feeling, and action you have is not only known but known in an infinite depth. How does that awareness change the way you will speak or act in the next hour? What will you do to live with that reality?
?
The fifth objection argues that even the possibility of your actions has no beginning—it is eternal in God's knowledge. If your every possibility is already present in God's eternal knowledge, how seriously does that make you take the intentions behind your choices? Will you now pause before every action and ask what intention you are actualizing from that eternal possibility?
?
Numbers are infinite, and you use them every day—counting time, money, achievements. Yet this lesson reminds you that the infinite is real and present. Are you living as if your time and resources are finite and will run out, or as if they are part of an eternal order? What is one concrete change you will make today to prioritize the eternal over the temporary?

The Sixth and Seventh Arguments for Infinite Regress
Presents two arguments for infinite regress as counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two concrete arguments that demonstrate the actual existence of infinite regress: one from the infinite ranks of numbers and another from God's knowledge of His own knowledge.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is not only possible but actually exists in numbers and in God's own knowledge.

The author presents two powerful counterexamples to those who claim infinite regress is impossible. The first uses the obvious endlessness of numerical ranks. The second draws on God's knowledge: if God knows something, He can know that He knows it, and this chain has no end. Both show actual infinite series that exist simultaneously.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth argument
The author points to the ranks of numbers, which are obviously endless. Notice that this is presented as a simple observation.
Numbers are a clear case of an actual infinite regress.
2
Read the seventh argument
God knows a thing. Since He knows it, He can know that He knows it. In God, this possibility is actualized, leading to an infinite chain of knowledge-of-knowledge. All these ranks exist at once.
This is not a potential regress but an actual one.
3
Understand the refutational force
The author calls this a 'strong refutation' because it provides concrete examples of infinite regress, contradicting the claim that it is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسادسها وهو أن مراتب الأعداد لا نهاية لها فكان التسلسل حاصلا فيها وسابعها إنه تعالى عالم بالشيء وكل من علم شيئا أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالما به وإذا ثبت هذا الإمكان وجب أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل في حق الله تعالى لكونه منزها عن طبيعة القوة والإمكان وعلى هذا التقدير فهو سبحانه وتعالى عالم بالشيء وعالم بكونه عالما به وهكذا في المرتبة الرابعة والخامسة إلى ما لا نهاية له فقد حصلت هناك مراتب غير متناهية وكل مرتبة أخيرة منها فإنها متفرعة على المرتبة التي قبلها فقد حصلت هناك أمور غير متناهية وهي مرتبة بالطبع وهي بأسرها موجودة دفعة واحدة فهذا نقض قوي على قولكم التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات محال لا يقال هذا السؤال لا يتوجه إلى الحكماء لأنهم يقولون إنه تعالى عقل وعاقل ومعقول والكل واحد ولا يتوجه أيضا عن المتكلمين لأنهم يقولون العلم بالعلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالشيء لأنا نقول أما الكلام الذي حكيتم عن الفلاسفة فهو محض الطامات وذلك لأن العلم بالسواد مثلا عندهم صورة متساوية لذات السواد في الماهية وذات الله تعالى مخالفة للسواد في الماهية فلزم القطع بأن علمه تعالى بالسواد مغاير لذاته المخصوصة وكذلك القول في جميع المعلومات وأيضاً فقد دللنا بالبراهين القاطعة في أول هذا الكتاب على أن العلم حالة نسبية إضافية وإذا كان كذلك فنقول علم الله تعالى بالسواد عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة بين ذاته وبين السواد وعلمه بكونه عالما بالسواد نسبة مخصوصة بين ذاته وبين النسبة الأولى والنسبة إلى أحد الشيئين مغايرة للنسبة إلى الشيء الآخر فيثبت أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بذلك الشيء وأيضاً لو كان العلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالشيء لكان العالم بالشيء عالما بتلك المراتب التي لا نهاية لها على التفصيل ومعلوم أنه باطل وأيضاً فإنه يمكننا أن نعتقد كون الرجل الفلاني عالما بالعلم الفلاني مع أنا نشك في كونه عالما بذلك العلم وأيضا فإنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين قولنا إن زيدا عالم بالسواد وبين قولنا إنه عالم بكونه عالما بالسواد ولو كان أحد العلمين نفس الثاني لما بقيت هذه التفرقة فهذا تمام الكلام في توجيه هذا النقض
And the sixth of them: that the ranks of numbers have no end, so infinite regress occurs in them. And the seventh of them: that He, exalted, knows a thing, and everyone who knows a thing it is possible for him to know that he knows it; and when this possibility is established, it must actually occur in the case of God, because He is exalted above the nature of potentiality and possibility. On this supposition, He, glorified and exalted, knows the thing, and knows that He knows it, and likewise in the fourth rank, and the fifth, without end. So there occur there infinite ranks, and every later rank is derived from the rank before it. So there occur there infinite things, arranged by nature, and all of them exist at once. This is a strong refutation of your statement: infinite regress in causes and effects is impossible. It cannot be said that this question does not apply to the philosophers, because they say that He, exalted, is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and all are one; nor does it apply to the theologians, because they say that knowledge of the knowledge of a thing is the same as the knowledge of the thing. For we say: as for the statement you narrated from the philosophers, it is pure nonsense, because knowledge of blackness, for example, according to them, is a form equivalent to the essence of blackness in quiddity, and the essence of God is different from blackness in quiddity, so it necessarily follows that His knowledge of blackness is different from His particular essence, and likewise for all known things. Moreover, we have proven with conclusive proofs at the beginning of this book that knowledge is a relative state. If so, we say: God's knowledge of blackness is a specific relation between His essence and blackness, and His knowledge that He knows blackness is a specific relation between His essence and the first relation, and the relation to one of the two things is different from the relation to the other thing. So it is established that knowledge of the knowledge of a thing is different from the knowledge of that thing. Also, if knowledge of a thing were the same as knowledge of the thing, then one who knows a thing would know those infinite ranks in detail, and it is known that this is false. Also, we can believe that a certain man possesses a certain knowledge while we doubt that he possesses that knowledge. Also, we perceive an evident distinction between our statement: Zayd knows blackness, and our statement: he knows that he knows blackness; and if one of the knowledges were the same as the second, this distinction would not remain. This completes the discussion of this objection.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof by counterexample, presenting two additional arguments for infinite regress and then defending them against a counter-objection. The author first states the sixth argument: the ranks of numbers are infinite, so regress occurs in them. Then the seventh: God knows a thing; if He knows a thing, He can know that He knows it, and this possibility must be actual in God, leading to an infinite regress of knowledge-of-knowledge ranks, all existing simultaneously. This is presented as a strong refutation of the claim that infinite regress is impossible. The author then anticipates an objection: this regress does not apply to philosophers who identify God as intellect, intelligent, and intelligible as one, nor to theologians who say knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge of the thing. The author refutes both: the philosophers' view is nonsense because knowledge is a relation; God's knowledge of blackness is a specific relation different from His essence, and knowledge of that knowledge is another relation. Thus they are distinct. He provides additional arguments: (1) If they were identical, one would know infinite ranks in detail (false). (2) We can believe someone knows something while doubting they know that they know it. (3) We intuitively distinguish between 'knows X' and 'knows that he knows X.' Therefore, the regress stands.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is not only possible but actually exists in numbers and in God's own knowledge of His knowledge.

?
The argument uses numbers as a model for infinite regress. You live in a finite world with limited time and resources. Do you trust that God's mercy and power are truly infinite, or do you subconsciously limit Him? When you sin, do you really believe His forgiveness has no end?

Defense of the Knowledge Regress Against Objections
Refutes objections and proves knowledge-of-knowledge is distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of knowledge is distinct from knowledge of the thing, and how the author defends the infinite regress of God's knowledge against objections from philosophers and theologians.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the knowledge of a thing is not identical to knowledge of the thing itself.

The author anticipates an objection: philosophers identify God as intellect/intelligent/intelligible as one, and theologians say knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge. He dismantles both views, showing that knowledge is a relation, and each relation is distinct. He then provides three additional proofs for the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that the knowledge regress does not apply to philosophers (who see God as all-one) or theologians (who see knowledge-of-knowledge as identical to knowledge).
2
Refutation of the philosophers' view
The author argues that knowledge of blackness, for example, is a form representing blackness, but God's essence is different from blackness, so His knowledge is distinct from His essence.
This shows that knowledge is not identical to God's essence.
3
Refutation of the theologians' view
Knowledge is a relative state (نسبة إضافية). God's knowledge of blackness is a relation between His essence and blackness; knowledge of that knowledge is a relation between His essence and the first relation. These are different relations, hence different knowledges.
4
Additional arguments
The author gives three more proofs: (1) If identical, one would know infinite ranks in detail (false). (2) We can believe someone knows something while doubting they know that they know it. (3) We intuitively distinguish between 'Zayd knows blackness' and 'Zayd knows that he knows blackness'.
These reinforce the distinction.

Infinite Regress of Ratios in Magnitudes
Presents the second objection against the impossibility of infinite regress using ratios in magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinite divisibility of magnitudes provides a counterexample that demonstrates an actual infinite regress.

Core Idea
Infinite ratios in a magnitude show actual infinite regress is real.

This lesson examines a powerful objection against the claim that infinite regress is impossible. The author uses the example of a magnitude being infinitely divisible into halves, quarters, and so on, which yields an infinite number of ratios that exist simultaneously. Understanding this argument is crucial for debates about causality and the nature of infinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Try to grasp the core argument: a magnitude can be divided infinitely, resulting in infinite ratios.
2
Identify the key steps
Notice the logical flow: (1) magnitude accepts halving, (2) halving each half yields quarters, (3) this continues without end, (4) therefore infinite ratios exist, (5) each later ratio depends on previous ones.
This is a clear chain of reasoning.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that an actual infinite regress of ratios exists simultaneously. This refutes the idea that infinite regress is impossible.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: it shows that in the physical world (magnitudes), actual infinite regress can occur, so arguments against an infinite regress in causal chains may need reexamination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها وهو النقض بالنسب فإن كل مقدار فإنه يقبل التنصيف ثم إذا نصف كل واحد من نصفيه فإنه يقبل التربيع ولما كان المقدار قابلا للتنصيف إلى غير النهاية فحينئذ حصلت هناك نسب غير متناهية وكل مرتبة آخرة فإنها متفرعة على النسب الحاصلة قبلها فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه النقوض
And the second of them: the objection by ratios. For every magnitude accepts division in half; then if we halve each of its halves, it accepts division into quarters. Since the magnitude is divisible into half without end, then there occur there infinite ratios, and every later rank is derived from the ratios occurring before it. This completes the discussion of these objections.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an objection (نقض) against the claim that infinite regress is impossible. The author presents a second counterexample: consider any magnitude (e.g., a line). It can be divided into halves, then each half into quarters, and so on ad infinitum. This yields an infinite number of ratios (e.g., 1/2, 1/4, 1/8, ...) that are arranged in a natural order, each derived from the previous. These ratios all exist simultaneously (not sequentially), forming an actual infinite regress. Thus, this serves as a refutation of the assertion that an actual infinite regress cannot exist.

نسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think infinite division is merely potential and never actual.
Correction: The author treats the resulting ratios as actual and simultaneous, not just potential.

Checkpoint

A) That magnitudes cannot be divided infinitely.
B) That an actual infinite regress of ratios exists simultaneously.
C) That infinite regress is impossible because ratios are not real.
D) That only potential infinite regress is possible.

Key Takeaway
The infinite divisibility of magnitudes shows that actual infinite regress is not only possible but inherent in the nature of magnitudes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Circularity in Describing the Infinite Series as a Whole
Raises an objection of circularity when describing an infinite series as a whole or aggregate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how describing an infinite series as a 'whole' (جملة) introduces circular reasoning, because wholeness already implies finiteness.

Core Idea
Calling an infinite series a 'whole' already assumes its finiteness.

This passage presents a powerful objection against a proof that relies on describing an infinite causal series as a 'whole' or 'aggregate'. The objection argues that the very concept of a whole presupposes finiteness, so the proof secretly assumes what it tries to prove—a classic case of circular reasoning (دور). Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to detect hidden assumptions in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is 'السؤال الثاني' (the second question) against a previous proof. Identify the core claim being attacked.
The objection targets the use of 'whole' (جملة) and 'aggregate' (مجموع).
2
Identify the circularity
The objection states that describing something as a 'whole' implies it is distinct from others (امتياز), and that distinctiveness requires finiteness (متناه). Therefore, calling the infinite series a 'whole' already assumes it is finite—the very thing the proof aims to establish.
This is the key logical move. The proof assumes finiteness by using the term 'whole'.
3
Understand why this defeats the proof
If the proof relies on the series being a 'whole' to argue that it must be finite and therefore need a necessary cause, but 'whole' already means finite, then the argument is circular and invalid.
The proof cannot legitimately use 'whole' to prove finiteness because 'whole' already contains that concept.
4
Reflect on broader implications
Consider how this objection affects arguments about infinite regress. It shows that one must be careful not to treat an infinite series as a completed totality, because that implicitly imposes finiteness.
This is a key critique in Islamic theology against cosmological arguments that assume a 'total series'.

السؤال الثاني على البرهان الذي ذكرتم أن نقول قولكم تلك الجملة المركبة غير تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها إما أن تكون واجبة أو ممكنة إنما يصح ويظهر إذا صح وصف تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها بكونها جملة ومجموعا فلم قلتم إن ذلك صحيح وتقريره وهو أن وصف الشيء بكونه جملة ومجموعا مشعر بامتياز ذلك المجموع عن غيره وامتيازه عن غيره مشروط بكونه متناهيا فثبت أنه لا يمكن وصفه بكونه مجموعا وجملة إلا بعد أن ثبت كونه متناهيا وأنتم أثبتم كونه متناهيا على وصفه بكونه كلا وجملة وحينئذ يلزم الدور
The second question against the proof you mentioned: that your statement 'that composite whole is other than those causes and effects which have no end, either it is necessary or possible' is only valid and clear if it is valid to describe those causes and effects which have no end as a whole and aggregate. So why do you say that is correct? Its explanation: that describing something as a whole and aggregate indicates that aggregate's distinction from others, and its distinction from others is conditional upon its being finite. So it is established that it cannot be described as an aggregate and whole except after it is established to be finite, and you have established it to be finite based on its description as a whole and aggregate, and thus circularity occurs.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an objection (السؤال الثاني) against a proof that uses the concept of a 'whole' or 'aggregate' to argue that an infinite series must end in a necessary being. The objection points out a circularity: the proof describes the infinite series of causes and effects as a 'whole' or 'aggregate' in order to argue that it must be finite and therefore dependent on a necessary being. However, describing something as a whole or aggregate already implies that it is distinguished from other things, and this distinction requires it to be finite. So the proof assumes the finiteness of the series (by calling it a whole) in order to prove its finiteness. This is circular reasoning (دور). Thus, the objection invalidates the proof's reliance on the notion of a whole.

Key Terms
جملة
مجموع
امتياز
متناه
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite series can be properly called a 'whole' or 'aggregate' without implying finiteness.
Correction: The objection shows that the concept of a 'whole' inherently involves being distinct and bounded, which presupposes finiteness. An infinite series cannot be a 'totality' in the same sense.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity identified in the third objection?
A) The proof uses the term 'whole' to prove finiteness, but 'whole' already implies finiteness.
B) The proof assumes infinity to prove finiteness.
C) The proof relies on the concept of necessity to prove possibility.
D) The proof uses empirical evidence without rational basis.

Key Takeaway
Calling an infinite series a 'whole' is question-begging because wholeness implicitly includes finiteness, making any argument that uses it to prove finiteness circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument shows that calling something a 'whole' imposes a boundary. In what area of your life have you been unwittingly imposing boundaries on God's infinite knowledge or mercy, and are you ready to repent from that circular thinking and submit to His limitless reality?

The First Objection: Knowledge of Knowledge vs Knowledge
Three proofs that knowledge of knowledge differs from direct knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the causal argument, which distinguishes between knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, and the three proofs for this distinction.

Core Idea
Knowing that you know is different from just knowing.

Before we examine the author's response to these objections, we must understand what the objections are. The first objection challenges the identity between knowledge of something and knowledge of that knowledge. The author presents three clear arguments to show they are distinct. This lesson walks through those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection carefully. Then read the translation. Note the structure: an initial claim followed by three proofs.
The passage begins with 'مغايرة للنسبة' and ends with 'توجيه هذا النقض'.
2
Identify the three proofs
Look for the words 'وأيضاً' which introduce each proof. List them: infinite regress, belief vs doubt, intuitive distinction.
Each proof is a separate argument against identity.
3
Understand the intuitive distinction
Focus on the Zayd example: knowing blackness vs knowing that you know blackness. This is a self-evident distinction.
This proof is the most direct.
4
Reflect on the objection's role
Consider why this objection threatens the causal argument. If knowledge of knowledge is different, then knowing the cause does not imply knowing that you know the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مغايرة للنسبة إلى الشيء الآخر ، فيثبت : أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بذلك الشيء ، وأيضاً : لو كان العلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالشيء ، لكان العالم بالشيء عالما بتلك المراتب التي لا نهاية لها على التفصيل ، ومعلوم أنه باطل ، وأيضاً : فإنه يمكننا أن نعتقد كون الرجل الفلاني عالما بالعلم الفلاني ، مع أنا نشك في كونه عالما بذلك العلم ، وأيضا فإنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين قولنا : إن زيدا عالم بالسواد، وبين قولنا : إنه عالم بكونه عالما بالسواد ، ولو كان أحد العلمين نفس الثاني ، لما بقيت هذه التفرقة ، فهذا تمام الكلام في توجيه هذا النقض وثانيها : وهو النقض بالنسب ، فإن كل مقدار فإنه يقبل التنصيف ، ثم إذا نصف كل واحد من نصفيه فإنه يقبل التربيع ، ولما كان المقدار قابلا للتنصيف إلى غير النهاية ، فحينئذ حصلت هناك نسب غير متناهية ، وكل مرتبة آخرة فإنها متفرعة على النسب الحاصلة قبلها ، فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذه النقوض .
Different from the relation to the other thing, so it is established: that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of that thing. Also: if knowledge of a thing were the same as knowledge of the thing, then the knower of the thing would be knowledgeable about those infinite degrees in detail, and it is known that this is false. Also: we can believe that a certain man is knowledgeable of a certain science, while we doubt whether he is knowledgeable of that science. Also: we perceive an intuitive distinction between our saying 'Zayd knows blackness' and our saying 'He knows that he knows blackness'; if one of the two knowledges were the same as the other, this distinction would not remain. This completes the explanation of this objection. And the second: the objection concerning relations, for every magnitude can be halved, and then if each of its halves is halved it can be quartered, and since magnitude is divisible infinitely, then there arise infinite relations, and each subsequent level is derived from the relations before it. This completes the presentation of these objections.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (naqd) that an opponent might raise against a previous argument for a necessary existent. The first objection concerns the distinction between knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge. The author lists three proofs for this distinction: (1) if they were identical, the knower would have to know infinite levels of knowledge, which is impossible; (2) we can believe someone knows a science while doubting that he knows that he knows it; (3) there is an intuitive distinction between knowing something and knowing that one knows it. The second objection concerns infinite divisibility: a magnitude can be halved infinitely, producing infinite relations. The author concludes by stating that this completes the presentation of these objections, implying they will be addressed later.

A) The knower would know infinite degrees of knowledge in detail.
B) The knower would be ignorant of the thing.

D) Knowledge would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that knowledge of knowledge is distinct from direct knowledge, using three compelling proofs.

The Second Objection: Infinite Divisibility of Magnitudes
An objection based on infinite divisibility of magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the causal argument, which concerns infinite divisibility of magnitudes and the resulting infinite relations.

Core Idea
If every magnitude can be halved infinitely, then there are infinite relations, challenging causality.

The second objection raises a problem about infinite divisibility. If any magnitude can be divided infinitely, then there are infinitely many relations, which might undermine the causal argument. This lesson explains the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection. Note the key terms: 'مقدار', 'التنصيف', 'التربيع', 'نسب غير متناهية'.
The passage begins with 'وثانيها' and ends with 'تقرير هذه النقوض'.
2
Understand the halving process
The passage states that every magnitude can be halved, and each half can be halved again, leading to quarters, etc. This process continues infinitely.
This is a classic concept of infinite divisibility.
3
Grasp the infinite relations
Because of infinite divisibility, there arise an infinite number of relations (ratios) between the parts. Each new level depends on the previous one.
These relations are not explicitly defined but are implied as the fractions of the whole.
4
Consider the objection's challenge
Reflect on why this infinite chain of relations might pose a problem for the causal argument. It may lead to an infinite regress of causes or conditions.

Objection: The Infinite Chain Cannot Be a Whole
An opponent questions whether an infinite chain can be considered a whole (jumla) in need of a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that an infinite causal chain cannot be meaningfully described as a 'whole' without presupposing finitude, leading to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Describing an infinite chain as a whole is circular: finitude is required to be a whole, but the proof uses being a whole to prove finitude.

In this passage, an opponent raises a sharp objection against the kalam proof for a first cause that treats an infinite causal chain as a single whole. He argues that labeling something as a 'whole' (jumla) implies it is distinct and finite. Since the proof uses the description 'whole' to conclude finitude, it assumes what it seeks to prove — a vicious circle. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the opponent's clear logical structure.
2
Identify the condition for 'whole'
The opponent states that describing something as a whole implies distinction, and distinction requires finitude. Find that statement.
The key phrase: وامتيازه عن غيره مشروط بكونه متناهيا
3
Trace the circularity
The opponent then argues that you cannot call the chain a whole without first knowing it is finite, but the proof uses 'whole' to prove finitude. This is circular.
The term 'دور' (circularity) is used at the end.
4
Reflect on the impact
Consider how this objection undermines the proof. The opponent is not denying a first cause, but challenging the logical validity of this particular argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : على البرهان الذي ذكرتم : أن نقول قولكم تلك الجملة المركبة غير تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها ، إما أن تكون واجبة ، أو ممكنة ، إنما يصح ويظهر إذا صح وصف تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها ، بكونها جملة ومجموعا ، فلم قلتم إن ذلك صحيح ؟ وتقريره : وهو أن وصف الشيء بكونه جملة ومجموعا مشعر بامتياز ذلك المجموع عن غيره ، وامتيازه عن غيره مشروط بكونه متناهيا ، فثبت أنه لا يمكن وصفه بكونه مجموعا وجملة إلا بعد أن ثبت كونه متناهيا ، وأنتم أثبتم كونه متناهيا على وصفه بكونه كلا وجملة وحينئذ يلزم الدور
The second question on the proof you mentioned: we say, your statement 'that compound whole is other than those causes and effects without end, either it is necessary or possible' is only valid if the description of those causes and effects without end as a whole and aggregate is valid. Why do you claim that is true? Its clarification: that describing something as a whole and aggregate indicates that aggregate's distinction from other than it, and its distinction from other than it is conditioned upon it being finite. So it is established that it cannot be described as an aggregate and whole except after it has been established to be finite, and you have established it to be finite based on its description as a whole and aggregate, and thus circularity follows.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection concerning the description of an infinite causal chain as a 'whole' (jumla) or 'aggregate' (majmu'). The opponent argues that describing something as a whole implies it is distinct from other things, and that distinctness requires finitude. Therefore, the proof that the chain must be caused because it is a whole is circular: the proof assumes the chain is finite to claim it is a whole, but then uses the fact that it is a whole to prove it is finite (and thus needs a cause). This is a logical objection against the very method of treating an infinite series as a single object of analysis.

Key Terms
جملة
مجموع
متناه
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies the existence of a first cause.
Correction: The objection only targets the logical structure of the proof, not the conclusion that a first cause exists.

Checkpoint

A) It must be composed of parts.
B) It must be finite.
C) It must have a cause.
D) It must be distinct from other things.

Key Takeaway
Using 'wholeness' as a premise for proving finitude is circular if wholeness itself presupposes finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Fourth Division: Infinite Regress Without a First Cause
The opponent proposes a fourth causal division: infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's proposal of an infinite regress as a fourth possible cause and why it challenges the standard proof for a necessary being.

Core Idea
The opponent's infinite regress argument creates a fourth causal possibility that the standard proof fails to address.

In this passage, al-Razi presents a critical objection to the standard kalam proof for a necessary being. The proof had argued that a possible whole must have a cause either within itself, identical to itself, or outside it. But the opponent cleverly introduces a fourth option: an infinite chain where each member is caused by another, with no first cause. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: objection, counter-objection, and response.
2
Identify the three original divisions
The proof originally divided possible causes into: whole caused by itself, by something within it, or by something outside it. Find these in the passage.
These are the standard divisions in kalam arguments for a necessary being.
3
Understand the fourth division
The opponent proposes a fourth division: each individual in the chain is caused by another without end. See how this differs from the other three.
This infinite regress does not require a first cause or an external cause.
4
Evaluate the objection
The opponent argues that the original proof only refuted irrelevant divisions and neglected this one. Consider whether this critique holds.
Ask yourself: does this fourth division really avoid the impossibility of infinite regress?

السؤال الثالث : هب أنه يصح وصفها بكونها كلا وجملة ومجموعا ، فلم قلتم إنه يجب انتهاؤها إلى واجب الوجود لذاته، أما قوله : « إن ذلك المجموع لما كان ممكنا فله سبب ، وسببه إما أن يكو هو هو ، أو ما يكون داخلا فيه أو ما يكون خارجاً عنه ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون شيئاً داخلا فيه ؟ قوله : ه يلزم كون ذلك الواحد علة لنفسه ، وعلة لعلة نفسه ، قلنا : هذا إنما يلزم لو عللنا تلك الجملة ، بواحد من تلك الجملة ، أما لو عللنا تلك الجملة بكون كل واحد منها مستندا إلى واحد آخر بغير نهاية ، لم يلزم ما ذكرتم من المحال ، والحاصل : أنهم أبطلوا كون تلك الجملة معللة بنفسها ، وأبطلوا كونها معللة بواحد من تلك الجملة ، وعند هذا قالوا : يجب كونها معللة بشيء خارج عن مجموع الممكنات ، وللسائل أن يقول : ها هنا قسم رابع ، وهو كون تلك الجملة معللة بكون كل واحد من آحادها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى نهاية ، فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن هذا القسم قسم مغاير للأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها ، فإن هذا القسم مغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بنفسها ، ومغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بواحد معين متناه ، ومغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بشيء خارج عنها ، فثبت أن هذا القسم مغاير للأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتم فأنتم اشتغلتم بتلك الأقسام ، التي هي خارجة عن المقصود ، وأما هذا القسم الرابع فهو القسم الذي وقع الشك فيه ، وإنما شرعتم في هذا البحث لأجل إبطاله ، ثم تركتموه وأهملتموه ، وما تعرضتم له. فالحاصل : أن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه : ذكرتموه في إبطال أقسام ، لا حاجة لها . وإبطال القسم الذي تعلقت الحاجة به أهملتموه فكان هذا من باب التمويه • فإن قالوا : التقسيم الذي ذكرناه صحيح ، فإنا قلنا : علة تلك الحاجة ، إما نفسها أو شيء داخل فيها أو شيء خارج عنها ، ومعلوم أنه لا مزيد
The third question: Grant that it is valid to describe them as a whole and aggregate, why do you say it must terminate in a necessary existent in itself? As for His saying: 'That whole, since it is possible, has a cause, and its cause is either identical to it, or something within it, or something outside it.' We say: why is it not permissible for it to be something within it? He said: that would entail that that one thing is the cause of itself and the cause of the cause of itself. We say: this only follows if we made that whole caused by one of those [within it]; as for if we made that whole caused by each one of them being dependent on another without end, then the impossibility you mentioned does not follow. The conclusion is: they invalidated that the whole is caused by itself, and they invalidated that it is caused by one of those, and thereupon they said: it must be caused by something outside the sum of possibles. But the questioner can say: here is a fourth division: that the whole is caused by each of its individuals being caused by another without end. It is known necessarily that this division is different from the three divisions you mentioned: it is different from the whole being caused by itself, different from the whole being caused by one specific finite thing, and different from the whole being caused by something outside it. So it is established that this division is different from the three divisions you mentioned. You occupied yourselves with those divisions which are irrelevant to the purpose. As for this fourth division, it is the division that is in doubt, and you began this investigation to refute it, then you abandoned it and neglected it, and did not address it. So the upshot is: the proof you mentioned, you mentioned it to refute divisions that are not needed. And the refutation of the division that is needed you left aside, so this is a case of obfuscation. For they said: the division we mentioned is correct, for we said: the cause of that whole is either itself or something within it or something outside it, and it is known that there is no more...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the third question, which challenges the proof's classification of possible causes for a possible whole. The proof had argued that the cause must be either identical to the whole, within it, or outside it, and that the first two are impossible, thus requiring an external cause. The questioner proposes a fourth division: that each individual in the chain is caused by another ad infinitum, without the whole having a single cause. The questioner argues that this division is distinct from the other three and that the proof failed to refute it, thus the argument is incomplete. The passage includes a response from the original proponents (فإن قالوا) that attempts to defend the classification, but the text cuts off. This illustrates a classic kalam discussion on infinite regress and the necessity of a first cause.

Key Terms
جملة
قسم رابع
معلول
علة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The standard threefold division of causes (self, internal, external) exhausts all possibilities for explaining a possible whole.
Correction: The fourth division of infinite regress shows that an alternative exists where the whole is not caused by itself, a single internal cause, or an external cause, but by an endless chain of causes.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth division proposed by the questioner?
A) The whole is caused by itself.
B) The whole is caused by one specific finite thing.
C) The whole is caused by something outside it.
D) Each individual in the chain is caused by another without end.

Key Takeaway
The fourth division represents a valid logical alternative that the standard proof does not refute, showing the need for a more complete argument against infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a proof for a core belief without examining all possible objections, assuming it was complete? What might you be missing if you do the same with the argument for God's existence? Will you seek out the strongest counterarguments to your convictions this week?
?
The opponent accuses the proponents of obfuscation—ignoring the difficult division. Are there areas in your life where you avoid difficult questions because answering them would be uncomfortable? What is one such question you have been avoiding, and what would it cost you to face it honestly?
?
This passage shows that even a well-structured argument can have a hidden flaw. When you defend your faith or worldview, are you more concerned with winning the debate or with finding the truth? Are you willing to admit if your favorite argument has a weakness?

The Fourth Division in the Causal Argument and Its Defence
Objection and answer regarding the fourth division of causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth division in the causal argument for a necessary cause and how the author defends its distinctness from the three standard divisions.

Core Idea
The fourth division—infinite regress of causes among parts—is a distinct possibility that the standard proof fails to refute.

In this passage, the author presents a critical objection to the standard causal argument. He introduces a fourth division for the cause of the totality of possibles: an infinite chain where each part is caused by another. The text then defends this division against attempts to reduce it to self-causation. Understanding this argument is key to appreciating the depth of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard three divisions
Read the first part where the author states the standard classification: cause of the totality is either itself, something internal, or something external.
2
Understand the fourth division objection
Note the objector's claim that there is a fourth division: the totality is caused by each part being caused by another ad infinitum. This is distinct from the first three.
3
Follow the reduction argument
The opponent argues that this fourth division reduces to self-causation. Pay attention to the reasoning.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author refutes the reduction by distinguishing the state of the aggregate as a whole from the state of its parts. This is a key logical point.
5
Conclude the significance
The author concludes that the standard proof only refuted two divisions, leaving the fourth unaddressed. This reveals a potential gap in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان ممكنا فله سبب، وسببه إما أن يكون هو هو، أو ما يكون داخلا فيه أو ما يكون خارجاً عنه، قلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يكون شيئاً داخلا فيه؟ قوله: يلزم كون ذلك الواحد علة لنفسه، وعلة لعلة نفسه، قلنا: هذا إنما يلزم لو عللنا تلك الجملة بواحد من تلك الجملة، أما لو عللنا تلك الجملة بكون كل واحد منها مستندا إلى واحد آخر بغير نهاية، لم يلزم ما ذكرتم من المحال، والحاصل: أنهم أبطلوا كون تلك الجملة معللة بنفسها، وأبطلوا كونها معللة بواحد من تلك الجملة، وعند هذا قالوا: يجب كونها معللة بشيء خارج عن مجموع الممكنات، وللسائل أن يقول: ها هنا قسم رابع، وهو كون تلك الجملة معللة بكون كل واحد من آحادها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى نهاية، فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن هذا القسم قسم مغاير للأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها، فإن هذا القسم مغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بنفسها، ومغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بواحد معين متناه، ومغاير لكون تلك الجملة معللة بشيء خارج عنها، فثبت أن هذا القسم مغاير للأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتم فأنتم اشتغلتم بتلك الأقسام، التي هي خارجة عن المقصود، وأما هذا القسم الرابع فهو القسم الذي وقع الشك فيه، وإنما شرعتم في هذا البحث لأجل إبطاله، ثم تركتموه وأهملتموه، وما تعرضتم له. فالحاصل: أن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه: ذكرتموه في إبطال أقسام، لا حاجة لها. وإبطال القسم الذي تعلقت الحاجة به أهملتموه فكان هذا من باب التمويه. فإن قالوا: التقسيم الذي ذكرناه صحيح، فإنا قلنا: علة تلك الحاجة، إما نفسها أو شيء داخل فيها أو شيء خارج عنها، ومعلوم أنه لا مزيد على هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، فلما تكلمنا على كل واحد منها كان القسم الذي ذكرتموه داخلا فيها، وعلى هذا التقدير فلا حاجة بنا إلى أن نتكلم على ذلك القسم بعينه، وأيضاً: فتعليل الجملة بكون كل واحد منها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى نهاية يرجع حاصله إلى تعليل تلك الجملة بنفسها، وقد بينا أن ذلك محال. فنقول في الجواب عن هذا السؤال: أما قولكم إن القسم الذي ذكرناه داخل في تلك الأقسام الثلاثة، فنقول: إن صريح العقل يدل على أن هذا القسم الذي ذكرناه قسم معتبر. بلى المقصود من ذكر هذا الدليل ليس إلا إبطاله، فإذا بينا أن الأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها مغايرة لهذا القسم، فحينئذ يظهر أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم لا يفيد المطلوب. أما قوله: "إن تحليل كل واحد منها بالآخر يقتضي تعليل الجملة بنفسها"، فنقول: هذا أيضاً باطل، لأن كون حال المجموع من حيث إنه ذلك المجموع غير، وحال كل واحد من آحاد ذلك المجموع بالنسبة إلى واحد آخر غير، وبالجملة فالفرق بين الكل من حيث هو كل وبين كل واحد من آحاد الكل معلوم في المنطق. وصريح العقل أيضا يدل عليه، فالقول بأن الجملة إنما وجدت لأنها تلك الجملة غير، والقول بأن تلك الجملة وجدت لكون كل آحادها معلل بواحد آخر منها إلى غير النهاية غير. فأين أحد البابين من الآخر؟ والحاصل: أنكم أبطلتم تعليل تلك الجملة بنفسها، وأبطلتم تعليلها بواحد من آحاد تلك الجملة، وبقي ههنا احتمال آخر، وهو كون تلك الجملة معللة بكون كل واحد منها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى غير النهاية، وهذا القسم مغاير للقسمين الأولين، والمقصود من هذا البحث ليس إلا إبطال هذين القسمين، فكان الاشتغال بذكر القسمين الأولين.
Since it is possible, it has a cause, and its cause is either itself, or something internal to it, or something external to it. We said: why may it not be something internal to it? His statement: that would entail that that one is a cause of itself, and a cause of the cause of itself. We said: this would only follow if we caused that totality by one of those individuals; but if we caused that totality by the fact that each one of them depends on another ad infinitum, then the impossibility you mentioned does not follow. The upshot: they refuted that totality being caused by itself, and they refuted it being caused by one of those individuals, and then they said: it must be caused by something external to the sum of possibles. But the objector may say: here is a fourth division, namely that totality being caused by the fact that each one of its individuals is caused by another ad infinitum. It is known by necessity that this division is distinct from the three divisions you mentioned; for this division is distinct from that totality being caused by itself, distinct from it being caused by one specified finite individual, and distinct from it being caused by something external to it. So it is established that this division is distinct from the three divisions you mentioned. You occupied yourselves with those divisions which are irrelevant to the purpose; but this fourth division is the one about which doubt arose, and you began this investigation in order to refute it, yet you abandoned it, neglected it, and did not address it. So the upshot: the proof you presented you presented in refuting divisions that are not needed; and the refutation of the division that is needed you neglected, so this is a case of obfuscation. If they say: the division we mentioned is correct, for we said: the cause of that need is either itself, or something internal to it, or something external to it, and it is known that there is no more beyond these three divisions; so when we discussed each one of them, the division you mentioned was included in them; on this assumption, we have no need to discuss that division specifically. Also: causing the totality by the fact that each one of it is caused by another ad infinitum reduces in essence to causing that totality by itself, and we have shown that to be impossible. Then we say in answer to this question: As for your statement that the division we mentioned is included in those three divisions, we say: sound intellect indicates that this division we mentioned is a genuine division. Indeed, the purpose of mentioning this proof is only to refute it; so when we show that the three divisions you mentioned are distinct from this division, then it becomes clear that the proof you presented does not achieve the desired result. As for his statement: "Analyzing each one by the other entails causing the totality by itself," we say: this also is false, because the state of the aggregate insofar as it is that aggregate is different, and the state of each individual of that aggregate in relation to another individual is different. In short, the distinction between the whole insofar as it is a whole and between each individual of the whole is known in logic; and sound intellect also indicates it. So the statement that the totality exists because it is that totality is different from the statement that the totality exists because all its individuals are caused by another ad infinitum. So where is one of the two categories from the other? The upshot: you refuted causing that totality by itself, and you refuted causing it by one of the individuals of that totality, and there remains here another possibility: namely that totality being caused by the fact that each one of it is caused by another ad infinitum. And this division is distinct from the first two divisions, and the purpose of this investigation is only to refute these two divisions; so you occupied yourselves with mentioning the first two divisions.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit within the broader kalam argument for a necessary cause of possibles. The author first summarizes the standard position: if something is possible (mumkin), it requires a cause, and that cause must be either itself, something internal to it, or something external. The opponent (sail) raises a fourth possibility: the cause could be an infinite regress where each part of the totality is caused by another part. The author then argues that this fourth division is genuinely distinct from the three already discussed, and that the standard refutation only addressed irrelevant divisions, thereby obfuscating the real issue. Next, the author presents a counter-argument from the opponent: the fourth division is actually reducible to self-causation because an infinite regress of causes for the parts still amounts to the whole being caused by itself. The author responds in detail. First, he affirms that sound intellect recognizes the fourth division as an independent category. Second, he rejects the reduction to self-causation by distinguishing between the state of the aggregate as a whole and the state of its parts in relation to others; this distinction is known in logic and reason. The conclusion is that the standard proof only refutes two divisions (self-causation and causation by a finite individual), leaving the third possibility—infinite regress—unrefuted, which was the actual goal of the investigation.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلل (caused)
جملة (totality/whole)
آحاد (individuals/parts)
لا إلى نهاية (ad infinitum)
إبطال (refutation)
تمويه (obfuscation)
ممکن (possible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth division (infinite regress of causes for parts) is the same as self-causation of the whole.
Correction: The author shows that the state of the aggregate as a whole is different from the state of its parts; causing each part by another does not entail the whole causing itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many distinct divisions of causation for a totality of possibles are there in the argument?
A) Three: self, internal, external.
B) Four: self, internal, external, and infinite regress of parts.
C) Two: self and external.
D) Five: including divine causation.

Key Takeaway
The fourth division—infinite regress of causes among parts—is a distinct possibility that the standard proof fails to refute, highlighting the need for careful logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author distinguishes the whole from its parts. Are you sometimes content with the appearance of your worship (the whole) while ignoring that each part (action, intention) may be empty and not directed to God? How can you break that infinite regress of empty habits?
?
Have you ever intellectually settled for a conclusion because it was convenient, without honestly considering all possibilities? What would it cost you to truly examine the full range of arguments, even if it challenged your current position?
