Distinguishing the Whole from Its Parts in Causal Explanation
Response to objection that the division is included within the three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the division under discussion is included in the three divisions by distinguishing the causal status of the whole from its parts.

Core Idea
The whole is not explained by itself nor by a single part, but by the infinite chain of parts explaining each other.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that his division is redundant. The opponent argues that explaining the whole by each part being explained by another leads to self-explanation. The author clarifies that the whole as an aggregate is distinct from its parts, and a third causal possibility remains. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponent claims the division is already included in the three divisions and that explaining the whole via parts leads to self-explanation. Note the two prongs of the objection.
2
Grasp the distinction between whole and parts
Focus on the author's response: the whole as an aggregate (majmu‘) is different from each individual part (aḥād). Logic and reason affirm this distinction. The claim that the whole exists because it is that whole is different from the claim that it exists because each part explains another to infinity.
Pay attention to the terms al-jumla (the whole) and al-majmū‘ (aggregate).
3
Identify the neglected third possibility
The author points out that the opponent only refuted two explanations (self-explanation and explanation by one part), but neglected the third: the whole is explained by the fact that each part is explained by another ad infinitum. This is the intended division.
The purpose of the proof is to refute the first two, not the third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، فلما تكلمنا على كل واحد منها كان القسم الذي ذكرتموه داخلا فيها ، وعلى هذا التقدير فلا حاجة بنا إلى أن تتكلم على ذلك القسم بعينه ، وأيضاً : فتعليل الجملة بكون كل واحد منها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى نهاية ليرجع حاصله إلى تعليل تلك الجملة بنفسها، وقد بينا أن ذلك محال . فنقول في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : أما قولكم إن القسم الذي ذكرناه داخل في تلك ] الأقسام الثلاثة ، فنقول : إن صريح العقل يدل على أن هذا القسم الذي ذكرناه قسم معتبر بلى المقصود من [ ذكر ] هذا الدليل ليس إلا إبطاله ، فإذا بينا أن الأقسام الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها مغايرة لهذا القسم ، فحينئذ يظهر أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم لا يفيد المطلوب، أما قوله : « إن تحليل كل واحد منها بالآخر يقتضي تعليل الجملة بنفسها، فتقول : هذا [ أيضاً ] باطل ، لأن [ كون ] حال المجموع من حيث إنه ذلك المجموع غير، وحال كل واحد من آحاد [ ذلك المجموع بالنسبة إلى واحد آخـر غـير ، وبالجملة فالفرق بين الكل من حيث هو كل وبين كل كل واحد من أحاد الكل معلوم في المنطق . وصريح العقل أيضا يدل عليه ، فالقول بأن الجملة إنما وجدت لأنها تلك الجملة غير، والقول بأن تلك الجملة وجدت لكون كل أحادها معلل بواحد آخر منها إلى غير النهاية غير. فأين أحد البابين من الآخر ؟ والحاصل: أنكم أبطلتم تعليل تلك الجملة بنفسها، وأبطلتم تعليلها بواحد من آحاد تلك الجملة، وبقي ههنا احتمال آخر، وهو كون تلك الجملة معللة بكون كل واحد منها معللا بواحد آخر لا إلى غير النهاية، وهذا القسم مغاير للقسمين الأولين، والمقصود من هذا البحث ليس إلا إبطال هذين القسمين، فكان الاشتغال بذكر القسمين الأولين واهمال هذا القسم، الذي هو المطلوب غفلة عظيمة والله الهادي.
Upon these three divisions, when we spoke about each one of them, the division you mentioned is included in them, and upon this estimation we have no need to speak about that specific division. Also: the explanation of the whole (al-jumla) by the fact that each one of them is explained by another ad infinitum results in the explanation of that whole by itself, and we have shown that is impossible. So we say in answer to this question: As for your statement that the division we mentioned is included in those three divisions, we say: clear reason indicates that this division we mentioned is a valid division; rather, the purpose of mentioning this proof is only to refute it. So if we show that the three divisions you mentioned are different from this division, then it becomes clear that the proof you mentioned does not achieve the desired end. As for his saying: 'The analysis of each one by the other necessitates explaining the whole by itself,' we say: this is also false, because the state of the aggregate (al-majmū‘) insofar as it is that aggregate is different, and the state of each one of the individuals of that aggregate with respect to another individual is different. In summary, the difference between the whole insofar as it is a whole and between each one of the individuals of the whole is known in logic, and clear reason also indicates it. So the statement that the whole exists because it is that whole is different from the statement that that whole exists because each of its individuals is explained by another individual of it ad infinitum. So which of the two doors is from the other? The conclusion: you have refuted the explanation of that whole by itself, and you have refuted its explanation by one of the individuals of that whole, and there remains here another possibility: that the whole is explained by the fact that each one of them is explained by another without end. This division is different from the first two divisions, and the purpose of this inquiry is only to refute these two divisions. So occupying oneself with mentioning the first two divisions and neglecting this division, which is the intended one, is a great negligence. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The opponent claims that the division under discussion is already contained within the three previously mentioned divisions, so there is no need to treat it separately. They also argue that explaining the whole by each part being explained by another part results in self-explanation, which is impossible. The author responds by first asserting that reason clearly shows the division is distinct. The purpose of mentioning the proof is to refute it; if the three divisions are shown to be different, then the opponent's proof fails. Regarding the self-explanation argument, the author distinguishes between the whole as an aggregate and its individual parts—logic and reason affirm that the whole is not the same as its parts. The claim that the whole exists because it is the whole is different from the claim that it exists because each part explains another to infinity. These are distinct possibilities. The opponent has only refuted two: self-explanation and explanation by one part. The third possibility—that the whole is explained by each part being explained by another to infinity—remains, and it is this possibility that needs to be addressed. Neglecting it is a major oversight.

Key Terms

مجموع
تعليل
آحاد
كل
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the whole is explained by itself or by a single part.
Correction: The author shows that the whole as an aggregate is distinct; it is explained by the infinite chain of parts explaining each other.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility of explaining the whole that the opponent neglected?
A) The whole is explained by itself.
B) The whole is explained by one of its parts.
C) The whole is explained by each part explaining another ad infinitum.
D) The whole has no explanation.

Key Takeaway
The whole's existence is explained by the infinite chain of parts explaining each other, not by itself or a single part.

Infinite Regress of Causes: The First Objection Answered
Answer to the objection that an infinite regress of causes cannot be judged as needing a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of causes cannot be treated as a contingent whole and how the proof for a First Cause remains valid.

Core Idea
An infinite regress never actually exists, so it cannot need a cause.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the argument for a First Cause: If every cause depends on a prior cause ad infinitum, why can't the series itself be considered contingent? The author provides a precise response by distinguishing between a series that never exists simultaneously and a series that does. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the key Arabic terms: نقض, مسبوق, مجموع, معدوم, مؤثر.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author contrasts the aggregate of an infinite regress (non-existent eternally) with the infinite series in the proof (exists simultaneously). Understand why only the latter can be judged as needing a cause.
The phrase 'فلم يحصل بمجموعها وجود' is crucial.
3
Reflect on the proof's condition
The author states that the proof is only complete if the cause must exist together with the effect. Consider how this condition makes the infinite series in the proof actual, unlike the objection's series.
Connect to the broader argument for a First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن هذه السؤالات: أن نقول أما النقض الأول: فجوابه: إن كل واحد منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى أول، فلم يحصل بمجموعها وجود في شيء من الأوقات، بل ما كان ذلك المجموع معدوما أزلا وأبدا، والمعدوم يمتنع الحكم عليه بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر، بخلاف هذه العلل، فإنا بينا أن العلة لا بد وأن تكون موجودة مع المعلول، فلو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات لوجدت بأسرها معا، فيكون المجموع موجودا، فحينئذ يصح الحكم عليه بالافتقار والحاجة إلى المؤثر، فظهر الفرق، ولأجل هذا الفرق ذكرنا أن هذا البرهان لا يتم إلا إذا بينا العلة يجب أن تكون موجودة حال وجود المعلول.
And the answer to these questions: we say: As for the first objection, its answer: each one of them is preceded by another without a first, so their aggregate does not obtain existence at any time; rather that aggregate was non-existent eternally and forever, and it is impossible to judge of the non-existent that it necessarily has an effect. This is unlike these causes, for we have shown that the cause must exist together with the effect. If the causes and effects were to regress infinitely, they would all exist together, so the aggregate would exist; then it would be valid to judge it as in need and requiring an effect. So the difference becomes apparent, and because of this difference we mentioned that this proof is not complete unless we show that the cause must exist at the time of the existence of the effect.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a refutation (naqḍ) concerning infinite regress. The opponent objects that if every cause is preceded by another cause without a first, then each individual cause is preceded, but the entire series never exists at any time; it is always non-existent. The author argues that it is impossible to say that a non-existent thing needs an effector. In the case under discussion (the proof for a first cause), the series of causes and effects would exist simultaneously if they were an infinite regress (since cause must exist with effect). Thus the aggregate exists and is contingent. The difference is that in the opponent's objection, the series never exists, so it cannot be judged contingent. The author emphasizes that the proof for a first cause requires that the cause exist together with the effect, so the infinite series would be present and hence need a cause.

Second Objection and Answer: Rational Souls
Answer to the objection that rational souls exist simultaneously without causal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of rational souls as an infinite aggregate does not invalidate the need for a first cause in a causal series.

Core Idea
Aggregate existence ≠ causal chain; only a causal series requires a first cause.

The second objection against the argument for a first cause cites rational souls existing simultaneously as a counterexample. At first glance, it seems to undermine the need for a first cause. However, the author responds by highlighting a crucial distinction: simultaneous existence is not the same as causal dependence. Let's see how this distinction strengthens the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims that rational souls exist together without a cause, challenging the necessity of a first cause.
Consider why this might seem like a problem.
2
Read the author's answer

3
Identify the distinguishing factor
The author points out that in the souls' aggregate, no soul is a cause of another, unlike the causal chain.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider why this distinction saves the argument: a non-causal infinite aggregate does not require a first cause, but a causal chain does.
5
Reflect on the implications
This shows that the argument for a first cause relies on the causal nature of the series, not mere infinity.

Third Objection and Answer: Infinite Events, Cognitions, and Potencies
Answer to the objection about infinite events, cognitions, and possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author distinguishes between problematic infinite regress (without a beginning) and acceptable infinite series (with a beginning but no end).

Core Idea
Infinite series with a beginning are not logically vicious; only those without a first cause are.

The third objection argues that infinite series of events, cognitions, and possible things undermine the need for a First Cause. The author responds by distinguishing two types of infinite series: those without a beginning (which are problematic) and those without an end but with a beginning (which are acceptable). The latter, like the ranks of knowledge, have a first term and thus do not challenge the argument for a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first addresses the objection about infinite events and then the ranks with no end.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the continuation is implied.
2
Identify the two types of infinite series
The author gives two different answers: one for infinite events (their sum does not exist) and one for ranks that have no end but have a first (like the knowledge example).
The knowledge example shows an infinite series with a first.
3
Understand why only series without a first are problematic
An infinite regress without a first term would imply no cause, but if there is a first, the series can be infinite in the other direction without logical issue.
This preserves the need for a First Cause.

Then he addresses ranks of numbers and ranks of validity of events, giving a separate answer for infinite series that have a beginning: for example, knowledge of a thing, then knowledge of that knowledge, and so on. This series has no last but has a first, so it is not an infinite regress in the problematic sense (without a first). The passage ends mid-sentence with 'we' (نحن), likely continuing to explain that in such a series, a first cause exists. The point is that infinite series with a beginning are acceptable and do not refute the need for a first cause.

Answering the First Objection: The Simultaneity Distinction
Author answers the first objection regarding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that an infinite regress of causes would be non-existent and thus not require a cause.

Core Idea
The need for a cause applies only when the sum of causes exists simultaneously.

The author now addresses the first objection to the proof for a First Cause. The objection claims that an infinite chain of causes, with no beginning, would never exist as a whole, so it cannot be said to need a cause. In response, the author distinguishes between two types of infinite regress, showing that the objection fails when applied to the case at hand.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that each cause is preceded by another without a first, so the totality of causes never exists at any time. Therefore, it is meaningless to say that this non-existent totality needs an effecter.
2
Follow the author's distinction
The author points out that in a vicious infinite regress (like the objection assumes), the sum never exists, so the need for a cause does not apply. However, in the causal series we are considering for the world's existence, all causes and effects exist simultaneously, so the totality is existent.
This is the key difference.
3
See the conclusion
Since the simultaneous causal series is existent, it is valid to judge it as being in need of an external effecter. Thus the proof for a First Cause is saved, provided we establish that the cause must coexist with its effect.
The proof is not complete without establishing simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النقض الأول : فجوابه : إن كل واحد منها مسبوق بآخر لا إلى أول، فلم يحصل بمجموعها وجود في شيء من الأوقات، بل ما كان ذلك المجموع معدوما أزلا وأبدا، والمعدوم يمتنع الحكم عليه بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر، بخلاف هذه العلل، فإنا بينا أن العلة لا بد وأن تكون موجودة مع المعلول، فلو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات لوجدت بأسرها معا، فيكون المجموع موجودا، فحينئذ يصح الحكم عليه بالافتقار والحاجة إلى المؤثر، فظهر الفرق، ولأجل هذا الفرق ذكرنا أن هذا البرهان لا يتم إلا إذا بينا العلة يجب أن تكون موجودة حال وجود المعلول
As for the first objection: its answer is that each one of them is preceded by another without a first, so by their totality existence does not occur at any time. Rather, that totality was non-existent eternally and forever, and it is impossible to judge of the non-existent that it necessarily requires an effecter (muʾaththir), contrary to these causes. For we have shown that the cause must exist simultaneously with the effect. So if causes and effects were to regress infinitely, they would all be found together, and thus the totality would exist. Then it becomes valid to judge of it as being in need and requiring an effecter. Thus the difference appears, and because of this difference we mentioned that this proof is not complete unless we show that the cause must exist at the time of the effect's existence.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the author's response to the first objection against the proof for a first cause from infinite regress. The objection argued that if every cause requires a preceding cause ad infinitum, then the entire chain would be non-existent, and thus cannot be said to need a cause. The author distinguishes: in a vicious infinite regress, the sum of causes is never existent at any time—it remains non-existent eternally. For a non-existent thing, it is absurd to claim it needs an effecter. But in the case of a simultaneous causal series (like the one proposed for the existence of the world), if causes and effects were infinite and coexistent, the totality would be existent, and thus it would be valid to judge it as needing an external effecter. Therefore, the proof for a first cause requires showing that the cause must be simultaneous with the effect. This distinction saves the argument from the objection.

Key Terms

تسلسل
علة
معلول
مؤثر
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any infinite regress of causes is impossible, but the author shows that the impossibility applies only when the sum is non-existent, not when all causes exist simultaneously.
Correction: The objection only works against a vicious infinite regress where the sum never exists, not against a simultaneous causal series where the sum is present.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the first objection (that an infinite regress of causes would be non-existent and thus not needing a cause) invalid?
A) Because the sum of an infinite regress is always existent.
B) Because the non-existent can still need a cause.
C) Because in the case of the world's causes, they exist simultaneously, so the totality is existent and thus needs a cause.
D) Because infinite regress is impossible in any form.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction between a vicious infinite regress and a simultaneous causal series rescues the proof for a First Cause.

Answering the Soul Objection: Why Simultaneity Doesn't Breach Causality
Author answers objection that simultaneous souls do not require a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational souls do not form a valid counterexample to the causal argument for a first cause.

Core Idea
A simultaneous non-causal series requires no first cause.

This lesson tackles a classic objection: don't rational souls exist simultaneously without a cause? The author shows that the key is not simultaneity but causality. We'll examine the distinction between a causal series and a mere collection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that if a causal series requires a first cause, then why don't rational souls—which exist simultaneously without an apparent first cause—also require one? This would create a contradiction.
The objection challenges the necessity of a first cause for any coexistent collection.
2
Read the author's answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's terse but precise response: 'none of them is a cause of the other.'
3
Grasp the distinction
The author distinguishes between a causal series (where each term causes the next) and a mere simultaneous collection (like souls) with no causal links. Only the former demands a first cause.
This distinction is crucial for the entire proof of a Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الثاني : وهو النفوس الناطقة • فالجواب : أن ذلك المجموع وإن كان موجودا معا، ولكن ليس شيء منها علة للآخر، بخلاف ما نحن فيه .
As for the second objection, which concerns rational souls (al-nufus al-nāṭiqa): the answer is that that totality, even though it exists simultaneously, yet none of them is a cause of the other, contrary to what we are dealing with.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage responds to the second objection that uses rational souls as a counterexample. The objector claimed that souls exist simultaneously without a first cause, so why should the causal chain of the world require one? The author answers that in the case of souls, although they exist together, there is no causal relationship between them—none is the cause of another. In the argument for a first cause, we are dealing with a causal chain where each term is the cause of the next. Therefore, the objection does not apply, because the souls are not a causal series.

Key Terms
النقض الثاني
النفوس الناطقة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any coexistent infinite series requires a first cause.
Correction: Only causal series require a first cause; a simultaneous collection without causal links does not.

Checkpoint
Why are rational souls not a valid counterexample to the proof for a first cause?
A) Because souls are not existent.
B) Because souls are not simultaneous.
C) Because souls do not form a causal chain.
D) Because souls are infinite in number.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because souls are not causally related; only causal series demand a first cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change if you truly lived that dependence today?
?
The objector used the simultaneous existence of souls as an excuse to deny the need for a first cause. Are there excuses you use to avoid acknowledging your complete need for God? What would it cost you to answer, like the author, by clarifying the difference between causal and non-causal relationships? Commit to identifying one such excuse and addressing it tomorrow.
?
If none of the souls is a cause of another, then your soul's existence is a direct gift from God, not mediated by any human or thing. How might this truth reshape your sense of worth, anxiety about others' opinions, or your connection to God? Write down one practical step to remember this the next time you feel socially pressured.

Answer to the Third Objection: Infinite Events with No End but a Beginning
Author answers objection about infinite events and ranks with no end but have a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series of future events or ranks does not undermine the need for a first cause, because a beginning suffices.

Core Idea
An infinite chain needs a beginning, not an end.

The third objection raises events and ranks that have no end—like infinite future acts of knowledge. At first glance, this seems to challenge the need for a first cause. But the author clarifies that the real requirement is a beginning, not an end. Let's see how he distinguishes between having no end and having no beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the scholar's response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice how the author first addresses the objection generally, then gives a specific example about ranks of knowledge.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ولكن لها أول' (but they have a beginning).
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
First, the author repeats the earlier difference that the sum of infinite numbers is non-existent. Second, he offers a new argument for ranks (maratib) that concedes they have no end but have a beginning.
The second part is the key refutation.
3
Understand the example of ranks of knowledge
The author describes an infinite hierarchy: knowledge of a thing, then knowledge of that knowledge, and so on. This has a first step (begins) but no final step (no end).
This shows that an infinite series can have a start.
4
See why the objection fails
The author reminds us that the original proof only required a first cause (beginning), not an ultimate end. Therefore, infinite future events do not contradict the need for a beginning.
The objection mistakenly assumes that no end implies no beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الثالث : وهو الحوادث التي لا آخر لها ، والمعلومات والمقدورات . فنقول : الفرق أن مجموع تلك الأعداد غير موجودة ، فيكون الفرق ما تقدم. وكذا الجواب عن مراتب الأعداد ، ومراتب صحة حدوث الحوادث وأما المراتب التي لا نهاية لها ، فنقول : هب أنه لا آخر لها ، ولكن لها أول ، فإن أول تلك المراتب هو العلم بالشيء ، ثم يترتب عليه المرتبة الثانية ، وهو العلم بذلك العلم ، ثم يترتب على المرتبة الثانية والمرتبة الثالثة وهلم جرا إلى ما لا نهاية فههنا لا آخر لهذه المراتب ، إلا أن لها أول ، ونحن إنما ادعينا أنه لا بد للأسباب والمسببات من أول ، وما ادعينا أنه لا بد لها من آخر . فظهر الفرق .
As for the third objection, which concerns events that have no end, and objects of knowledge and objects of power: we say the difference is that the sum of those numbers is non-existent, so the difference is as previously. Likewise the answer concerning ranks of numbers and ranks of the possibility of occurrence of events. As for the ranks that have no end, we say: grant that they have no end, but they have a beginning. For the first of those ranks is the knowledge of a thing, then the second rank follows from it, which is the knowledge of that knowledge, then from the second rank follows the third, and so on ad infinitum. So here these ranks have no end, but they have a beginning. And we only claimed that causes and effects must have a beginning, and we did not claim that they must have an end. Thus the difference appears.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection, which brings up events with no end (like future events) and infinite objects of knowledge and power. The author first repeats the earlier distinction: the sum of such infinite numbers is non-existent (as they are not all actual simultaneously), so the same difference applies. Then he turns to infinite ranks (maratib) such as the hierarchy of knowledge (knowing a thing, then knowing that knowledge, etc.). He concedes that such ranks have no end (they continue infinitely), but they do have a beginning—the first act of knowledge. The author's proof only required a first cause (beginning) for the causal series, not that the series have an end. Therefore, this objection fails because it misunderstands the claim: it is that there must be a beginning, not that there must be an end.

Key Terms

الحوادث
لا آخر لها
مراتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any infinite series cannot have a first cause because it has no end, so the need for a first cause is undermined.
Correction: The author shows that a series can have a beginning while having no end. The proof for a first cause only requires a beginning, not an end. Therefore, infinite future events do not contradict the necessity of a first cause.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference the author uses to refute the objection about infinite future events and ranks?
A) Those series have no beginning, so they need a cause.
B) Those series have a beginning but no end, and the proof only requires a beginning.
C) Those series are non-existent, so they need a cause.
D) Those series are all existent simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Infinite series with a beginning but no end do not challenge the necessity of a first cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your deeds can continue in reward even after death—but have you ever stopped to think that the very first moment of belief, the beginning of your journey, was a gift from Allah that you did not earn? How does that truth change how you view your own efforts today? Will you take a moment to repent for claiming any part of that beginning as your own?
?
The author shows that even an infinite chain must have a beginning—your life is such a chain. What is the beginning that you keep ignoring? Your existence, your faith, your every breath—are you living as if you created yourself? Or will you today trace everything back to the First Cause and bow in gratitude?

Answer to Second Question: Totality of Infinite Series as a Set
Author proves that an infinite totality can be described as a set or whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series can be described as a totality, even though it has no end, using two logical proofs.

Core Idea
An infinite whole is a valid subject for predication.

The author now addresses a second objection: that an infinite causal series cannot be meaningfully called a whole or set unless it is finite. He offers two arguments to show that the totality can indeed be described as a sum or aggregate, regardless of finitude.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that a totality can only be described as a set if it is finite. Then see how the author responds by denying the premise.
The objection is the opponent's statement in the first sentence of the passage.
2
Examine the first proof
The author argues that when we predicate 'infinity' of the causes and effects, the subject of that predicate is the totality itself, not each individual. Thus the totality is a valid subject.
Focus on the logical structure: predicate 'la nihāya lahu' is attributed to the totality.
3
Examine the second proof
The author says that when we say 'the infinite exists', we are judging the totality of infinite things as existent. The terms 'total', 'aggregate', 'sum' refer to all those existents together.
This proof uses the concept of existence to show the totality is meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : « تلك الأسباب والمسببات ، إنما يعقل وصفها بكونها مجموعا وجملة وكلا ، لو ثبت كونها متناهية ) . فالجواب : لا نسلم أن الأمر كما ذكرتم ، والدليل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا حيث قلنا : الأسباب والمسيبات لا نهاية لها ، فقد جعلنا اللانهاية محمولا ، ومعلوم أنه ليس موضوع هذا المحمول كل واحد من تلك الأسباب ، بل ليس إلا المجموع ، فثبت أنه يمكن وصفه بكونه مجموعـا وجملة ، سواء فرضناه متناهيا أو غير متناه . الثاني : إنا إذا قلنا ما لا نهاية له موجود ، فقد حكمنا على تلك الأمور لا نهاية لها بأنها موجودة ، ونحن لا نريد بالكل والجملة والمجموع إلا تلك الموجودات بأسرها
As for the second question, which is his statement: 'Those causes and effects can only be described as a sum, aggregate, and whole if they are finite.' The answer: we do not accept that the matter is as you stated, and the evidence for it is two aspects. First: when we say that causes and effects have no end, we have made infinity a predicate, and it is known that the subject of this predicate is not each individual cause, but only the totality. So it is established that it can be described as a sum and aggregate, whether we suppose it finite or infinite. Second: when we say 'that which has no end exists,' we have judged those things with no end to be existent, and we mean by 'total,' 'aggregate,' and 'sum' nothing but those existent things as a whole.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage responds to the objection that the totality of an infinite causal series cannot be meaningfully described as a whole or set unless it is finite. The author provides two proofs. First, when we say 'causes and effects have no end' (infinite), we are making 'infinity' a predicate. The subject of that predicate is not each individual cause, but the totality itself. Therefore, we can speak of the totality as a sum regardless of finitude. Second, when we say 'the infinite exists,' we are judging that those infinite things are existent, and by 'totality' we simply mean all of those existent things together. Thus, the totality is a legitimate concept even for an infinite series.

Key Terms

مجموع
جملة
لا نهاية له
متناه
محمول
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite set cannot be treated as a single entity or whole.
Correction: The author shows that the infinite totality can be the subject of predication (e.g., 'infinite') and can be referred to as a whole when we say 'the infinite exists.'

Checkpoint

A) Because the infinite series is finite in reality.
B) Because 'infinity' is a predicate whose subject is the totality, not each individual.
C) Because each individual cause is itself a totality.
D) Because the series has a beginning.

Key Takeaway
The infinite totality can be meaningfully described as a set because it serves as the subject of the predicate 'infinite' and as the referent of 'the infinite exists.'

Answer to Third Question: The Cause of the Totality is Not the Parts or Their Dependence
Author refutes that the totality's cause is each part's dependence on another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite chain of dependencies cannot serve as the cause of the totality, because it introduces a problematic duality between the parts and their relations.

Core Idea
The cause of a chain cannot be the chain itself.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that an infinite regress of dependencies could explain the existence of the whole. He shows that this explanation actually contains two distinct concepts—the parts themselves and their relations—and neither can be the cause without circularity or presupposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector suggests the totality exists because each part depends on another ad infinitum. Notice the structure of the objection.
The objection is a rhetorical question asking why this is not permissible.
2
Identify the two concepts
The author points out that in this explanation we get two concepts: the sum of the essences of the individuals and the dependence relations. Understand why they are distinct.
The essences are the parts themselves; the relations are accidents of those parts.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The author poses a dichotomy: the cause is either the individuals or the dependence relations. See why each option fails in the subsequent argument.
The passage ends with this dichotomy; the next part shows each option fails.
4
Conclude the refutation
Realize that neither option works, so the objection is invalid. The totality cannot be explained by an infinite chain of dependencies.
This conclusion follows from the dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن تلك الجملة إنما وجدت لأجل أن كل واحد من أحادها استند إلى واحد آخر ، إلى غير النهاية . . . فالجواب : عنه : أن نقول : هذا أيضاً باطل لأنا إذا قلنا : الجملة إنما وجدت لاستناد كل واحد من أحاد تلك الجملة إلى واحد آخر ، فقد حصل ههنا مفهومان : - أحدهما : مجموع ذوات تلك الأحاد الثاني : كون كل واحد منها مستندا إلى الآخر ، ولا شك في تغاير هذين المفهومين ، إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : هذا الذي جعلتموه علة لتلك الجملة ، إما أن يكون هو تلك الأحاد أو تلك الاستنادات العارضة لتلك الأحاد ،
As for the third question, which is his statement: 'Why is it not permissible to say that that totality exists because each one of its individuals depends on another, ad infinitum...?' The answer: we say that this too is false, because when we say 'the totality exists due to the dependence of each individual on another,' two concepts arise: one is the sum of the essences of those individuals, and the second is the fact that each one is dependent on the other. There is no doubt that these two concepts are distinct. Once you understand this, we say: that which you have made the cause of that totality is either those individuals or those dependence relations that are accidents of those individuals.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that the infinite causal chain itself explains the existence of the totality. The objector suggests that the totality exists because each part depends on another without end. The author points out that in this explanation, we have two distinct concepts: (1) the sum of the essences (dhawat) of the individuals, and (2) the relations of dependence (istinadat) that are accidents (arida) of those individuals. The cause of the totality cannot be both at once. If it is the individuals themselves, that is circular because they are part of the totality. If it is the dependence relations, those are accidents that presuppose the existence of the individuals. Thus, the objection fails to provide a genuine cause for the totality beyond the individuals themselves, which is insufficient.

Key Terms
السؤال الثالث
جملة
أحاد
استناد
مفهومان
ذوات
عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite chain of dependencies can be the cause of the whole without further explanation.
Correction: The author shows that this explanation actually contains two distinct concepts, and a true cause must be one or the other, neither of which is satisfactory.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the totality exists because each part depends on another ad infinitum?
A) Because the parts do not exist.
B) Because it leads to two distinct concepts (the parts and their dependencies), neither of which can be the cause.
C) Because the dependencies are not real.
D) Because the totality is already explained by the parts.

Key Takeaway
The objection that an infinite chain of dependencies explains the totality fails because it introduces a duality that cannot be resolved without circularity or presupposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a chain of secondary causes—people, plans, or possessions—to give your life meaning, as if the chain itself were the cause? How does this passage expose the emptiness of that trust?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you see yourself as one of the 'individuals' in a causal chain, or do you recognize that your ultimate dependence is on the Creator alone? What would it take for you to live that conviction today?
?
Are you comfortable acknowledging that you cannot be the cause of your own being? If the cause of the totality cannot be found within the parts, what does that demand of your relationship with the One who is truly Independent?
